Requirement to Mention Possession (Yad) in Claims for Immovable Property
Ruling on explicit mention of yad for immovable property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that the defendant must be in possession for a valid claim and explain why mutual agreement does not establish possession in court.

Core Idea
Possession (yad) must be explicitly stated for immovable property; mutual agreement is insufficient to prove it.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, the defendant in a property claim must be the actual possessor. This lesson explains why the claimant must mention that the property is in the defendant's hand for immovable property, and why mere agreement between parties cannot establish possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for a valid defendant
Understand that the defendant must be in possession (yad) of the disputed property; otherwise, they cannot be sued as a litigant (khasm).
This is the foundational principle for determining the proper party in property lawsuits.
2
Distinguish movable vs. immovable property
For movable property, possession is observable (mushahad) so explicit mention is not required. For immovable property, possession is not immediately visible, so the claim must state that the property is in the defendant's hand.
Immovable property like land can be possessed behind closed doors; explicit assertion is needed.
3
Understand why mutual agreement does not prove possession
If both parties agree that the property is in the defendant's hand, this is not sufficient proof because they might be colluding to defraud a third party. The court requires external evidence of possession.
The Hanafi rule: tasāduq (mutual agreement) does not establish yad in court.
4
Apply the ruling
In a lawsuit for immovable property, the judge will not accept the claim unless the claimant explicitly states that the property is in the defendant's possession. Even if the defendant admits it, the judge must still verify possession via witnesses or other evidence.
This protects against collusion and ensures a just proceeding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه في يده عطف على قوله: "ذكر حدوده" أي وذكر أيضاً أن العقار في يد المدعى عليه؛ لأن المدعى عليه لا يكون خصماً إلا إذا كان العقار في يده، فلابد من ذكره، ولا يحتاج إلى هذا القيد في المنقول؛ لأنه مشاهد في اليد. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۷/۲] بتصادقهما : أي بتصادق المدعي والمدعى عليه بأن ذكر المدعي: أن العقار المدعى في يد المدعى عليه فصدقه المدعى عليه في ذلك فلا يثبت اليد بهذا؛ لأن اليد فيه غير مشاهدة، ولعله في يد هما، وهما تواضعا فيه؛ ليكون لهما ذريعة إلى أخذه بحكم الحاكم.
'And that it is in his hand' is conjoined to his saying 'mentioning its boundaries', i.e., he also mentions that the immovable property is in the hand of the defendant, because the defendant is only a litigant (khasm) if the property is in his hand, so it must be mentioned. This qualification is not needed for movable property because it is witnessed in the hand. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/187] 'By their mutual agreement': i.e., by the mutual agreement of the claimant and defendant, in that the claimant says that the claimed immovable property is in the defendant's hand, and the defendant affirms that, then possession (yad) is not established by this; because possession is not witnessed (unseen), and perhaps it is in the hand of both, and they conspired in this [claim] to use it as a means to take it by the judge's ruling.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter by requiring that the claimant must also state that the property is in the defendant's possession (yad). This is necessary because the defendant is only a valid opponent (khasm) when he actually possesses the property. For movable property, this is obvious since possession is observed, but for immovable property it must be explicitly mentioned. The text also warns that mere mutual agreement between claimant and defendant that the property is in the defendant's hand does not prove possession; they might be colluding to obtain a judgment against a third party. The court requires actual evidence of possession (like witnesses or apparent control) not just statements.

Key Terms
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control over property, a legal basis for being a defendant.
الخصم (al-khaṣm): The opposing party in a lawsuit, typically the defendant.
التصادق (al-taṣāduq): Mutual agreement between parties; a claim that does not alone establish legal possession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi claims for immovable property, the claimant must explicitly state that the property is in the defendant's possession, and mutual agreement does not prove possession.

Procedure After a Valid Claim: Ask, Admit, Prove, or Oath
Ruling statement on court procedure after valid claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four-step procedure a judge follows after a valid claim in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The judge's response to a valid claim follows a fixed sequence: ask, admit/deny, evidence, oath.

When a claim is validly filed, the judge must not rule immediately. A clear procedure is prescribed: first ask the defendant, then either act on admission, request evidence from the plaintiff, or administer an oath to the defendant. This ensures fairness and the correct allocation of burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger
The procedure begins only after the claim is deemed valid (sahih ad-da'wa). A valid claim must meet basic conditions: clarity, legal interest, etc.
The passage begins with 'فإن صحت الدعوى' – 'if the claim is valid'.
2
First action: ask the defendant
The judge asks the defendant about the claim. The defendant may either admit (iqrar) or deny (inkar).
This is the first obligation on the judge.
3
Branch on admission
If the defendant admits, the judge rules against him based on that admission. No further evidence or oath needed.
Iqrar is a binding proof (iqrar al-'uqala' hujja).
4
Branch on denial
If the defendant denies, the judge turns to the plaintiff and asks for evidence (bayyinah).
Burden of proof is on the claimant (al-bayyina 'ala man idda'a).
5
Final branch: evidence or oath
If the plaintiff provides evidence, the judge rules in his favor. If not, the judge administers an oath (yamin) to the defendant to swear that the claim is false.
The oath is on the one who denies (al-yamin 'ala man ankara).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن صحت الدعوى سأل المدعى عليه عنها، فإن أقر أو أنكر، فبرهن المدعي قضى عليه، وإلا حلف
Then if the claim is valid, he asks the defendant about it; if he admits or denies, then the plaintiff provides evidence, he rules against him, otherwise he makes him swear an oath.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the procedure after a valid claim is filed. The judge first asks the defendant about the claim. If the defendant admits (iqrar), the judge rules against him based on that admission. If the defendant denies (inkar), the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence. If the plaintiff provides evidence, the judge rules in his favor. If the plaintiff cannot provide evidence, the judge administers an oath (yamin) to the defendant. This is the standard Hanafi procedure in litigation: the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies. The passage does not elaborate on the form of the oath or exceptions, but it is a concise statement of the basic sequence.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Admission; the defendant's acknowledgment of the claim.
يمين (yamin): Oath administered to the defendant when plaintiff lacks evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the defendant denies the claim and the plaintiff lacks evidence?
A) The judge rules against the defendant
B) The judge administers an oath to the defendant
C) The judge dismisses the case
D) The judge asks for more witnesses

Key Takeaway
After a valid claim, the judge must ask the defendant, then rule on admission, or require evidence from plaintiff, or administer an oath to the defendant if no evidence is provided.

Proof of Possession and Description of Claim
Explaining the prerequisites for a valid claim: proof of possession and description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid claim in Hanafi fiqh, including proof of possession for immovable vs movable property and the required description for debts.

Core Idea
A claim is not heard unless the defendant's possession is established and the claimed item is clearly described.

This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa explains the procedural prerequisites for a valid lawsuit. It distinguishes between immovable and movable property in proving possession, and sets the requirement for detailed description when the claim involves a debt or fungible items. Without meeting these conditions, the claim will not be entertained by the judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on the prerequisites for a valid claim (da'wa) before a judge. The hukm is that these conditions are wajib (obligatory) for the claim to be heard.
2
Distinguish between immovable and movable property
For immovable property (ʿiqār), the plaintiff must prove the defendant's possession either by evidence (bayyina) or the judge's own knowledge (ʿilm al-qāḍī). For movables (manqūl), possession is established by mutual agreement of the parties (taṣāduquhumā) because possession is observable.
The judge's knowledge is accepted cautiously to avoid collusion.
3
Note the requirement of explicit demand
The plaintiff must explicitly demand (muṭālaba) the return of the claimed property from the defendant. This removes any possibility that the property is held lawfully (e.g., as mortgage or deposit).
Even in movables, the plaintiff should state 'in his hand without right'.
4
Describe the description requirement for debts
When claiming a debt or fungible (mithlī), the plaintiff must specify the genus (jins), type (nawʿ), description (ṣifa), quantity (qadr), and cause of obligation (sabab al-wujūb). Without a cause, the claim is not heard.
For minted coins, also specify the mint and quality (good/bad).
5
Recognize the consequence of non-compliance
If any of these conditions are missing, the claim is not accepted by the judge. The plaintiff must rectify the deficiency or the case is dismissed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى
أحكام الدعوى
بل ببينة أو علم القاضي، بخلاف المنقول، وأنه يطالبه به، وإن كان دينا ذكر العقار في الذمة المدعي وصفه وأنه يطالبه به فإن صحت الدعوى سأل المدعى عليه عنها، فإن أقر أو على وجه المذكور أي القاضي أنكر، فبرهن المدعي قضى عليه، وإلا حلف أي المدعي أي أقام البينة على دعواه أي القاضي بلا طلب المدعي أي القاضي المدعى عليه القاضي أي عن الدعوى بل ببينة: أي بل يثبت اليد ببينة المدعي؛ لأن المدعى عليه لا يكون خصماً إلا إذا كان العقار في يده، فلابد من إثباته، وهذا إذا ادعى ملكاً مطلقاً، أما في دعوى الغصب ودعوى الشراء من ذي اليد فلا يقتصر إلى البينة؛ لأن دعوى الفعل كما تصح على ذي اليد تصح على غيره أيضاً أو علم القاضي: [باليد في الصحيح؛ لاحتمال تزويرهما. Commentary
This passage from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) is a commentary expanding on the matn. It explains the prerequisites for a valid claim. The key points: (1) For immovable property (عقار), the plaintiff must first prove that the defendant is in possession (يد) because without possession, the defendant is not a valid opponent. This proof can be through the plaintiff's evidence or the judge's own knowledge—the latter being accepted to avoid collusion but with caution. (2) For movables (منقول), possession can be established by the mutual agreement of the parties since possession is directly observable. (3) The plaintiff must explicitly demand the return of the claimed property to remove any possibility that the defendant holds it lawfully (e.g., as mortgage). (4) If the claim involves a debt or fungibles (مثليات), the plaintiff must provide a detailed description including genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause of obligation. Without a cause, the claim is not heard. The commentary cites various authorities and references works like Ramz al-Haqa'iq and Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control of property; a key factor in determining who is the defendant in a claim.
العقار (al-‘iqār): Immovable property such as land or buildings; requires special proof of possession.
المنقول (al-manqūl): Movable property; possession is established by mutual agreement due to observability.
المطالبة (al-muṭālaba): The plaintiff's explicit demand for the return of the claimed property.
الوصف (al-waṣf): Description of the claimed item, especially for debts and fungibles, including genus, type, quality, quantity, and cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required to establish the defendant's possession of immovable property in a claim?
A) Mutual agreement of both parties
B) Evidence or the judge's knowledge
C) The defendant's admission
D) A written document from the plaintiff

Key Takeaway
A valid claim requires proof of the defendant's possession and, for debts, a detailed description of the item.

Defendant's Response: Admission, Denial, and the Oath Procedure
Explaining procedure when defendant admits or denies, and the judge's role in administering oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three possible responses of the defendant (admission, ambiguous/silence, denial) and state the Hanafi procedure for each, including the conditions and exceptions for administering the oath.

Core Idea
Admission is self-proving; denial requires plaintiff's evidence or defendant's oath — the oath is the judge's right and requires plaintiff's request.

When a claim is filed, the defendant's response determines the next legal step. This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains what happens if the defendant admits, says 'neither admit nor deny', remains silent, or denies. It also details the critical rules for administering the oath when the plaintiff has no proof, including an intra-Hanafi disagreement on when the oath may be given without the plaintiff's request. Understanding these procedures is essential for anyone studying Hanafi judicial procedure (qada).

Guided Path
1
Identify the defendant's possible responses
List the three scenarios covered: admission, ambiguous statement ('I do not admit nor deny'), and denial. Also consider silence by disability or without disability.
The text gives specific rulings for each.
2
Determine the consequence for each response
Admission → conclusive proof, judge gives judgment. Ambiguous → imprisonment until he clarifies. Silence (without disability) → according to Abu Yusuf, treated as denial and defendant sworn. Silence with disability (e.g., mute) not discussed here.
The phrase 'الأشبه أنه إنكار' indicates the preferred view treats silence as denial.
3
Analyze the oath procedure for denial
If defendant denies and plaintiff has no evidence, judge administers the oath to the defendant. The oath is the right of the judge and must be done before him; any agreement to swear elsewhere is invalid.
The oath is never returned to the plaintiff in Hanafi law.
4
Identify the condition of plaintiff's request
The oath is only administered upon the plaintiff's request. Without the request, the judge does not swear the defendant. This is the relied-upon position.
Abu Yusuf holds exceptions in four cases; these are not the standard view.
5
Note the exceptions and the mufta bihi
Abu Yusuf's four exceptions: buyer returning defective goods, pre-emptor, wife claiming maintenance from absent husband, and claimant of ownership. But the standard position (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) requires request. The mufta bihi is the standard.
The text also mentions unanimous consensus for claims against a deceased: the judge swears without request from executor/heir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أقر إلخ [أي المدعى عليه بما يدعي عليه المدعي. (عيني)] ولو قال: لا أقر ولا أنكر حبسه حتى يقر أو ينكر ; لأنه ظالم فجزاؤه الحبس، وكذا لو لزم السكوت بلا آفة عند أبي يوسف، والأشبه أنه إنكار فيستحلف قضى عليه [أي على المدعى عليه؛ لوجود الحجة الملزمة للقضاء، وهي الإقرار في الفصل الأول، والبينة في الفصل الثاني (عيني)] إطلاق لفظ القضاء في صورة الإقرار توسع؛ لأن الإقرار حجة بنفسه، ولا يتوقف على القضاء، فكان الحكم من القاضي إلزاماً بالخروج عن موجب ما أقر به، بخلاف البينة، ولأنها إنما تصير حجة باتصال القضاء (كفاية (فتح وإلا حلف أي وإن لم يقر المدعى عليه و لم يبرهن المدعي على دعواه لعجزه عن البينة، حلف القاضي المدعى عليه، وقيد بتحليفه؛ لأن المدعى عليه بطلبه، ولا يرد يمين على مدع، ولا بيّنة لذي اليد في الملك المطلق، وبينة الخارج لو حلف بطلب المدعي يمينه بين يدي القاضي من غير استحلاف القاضي، فهذا ليس بتحليف؛ لأن التحليف حق القاضي، ولو استحلفا على أن يحلف عند غير القاضي ويكون بريئاً، فهو باطل، وكذا لو اصطلحا على أن المدعي لو حلف فالخصم ضامن للمال، وحلف لم يضمن. بطلبه: لأنه لا تحليف إلا بعد الطلب عند الطرفين في جميع الدعاوي، وعند أبي يوسف: يستحلف بلا طلب في أربعة مواضع: المشتري في الرد بالعيب والشفيع على عدم إبطاله الشفعة والمرأة إذا طلبت فرض النفقة على زوجها الغائب، والمستحق، وأجمعوا على أن من ادعى دينا على الميت يحلفه القاضي بلا طلب الوصي والوارث، وقيد بطلب المدعي؛ لأنه لو لم يطلبه لا حلف عليه. (فتح)
Then if he admits, etc. [i.e., the defendant admits what the plaintiff claims against him. (Ayni)] And if he says: 'I do not admit nor deny,' he is imprisoned until he admits or denies; because he is an oppressor, and his punishment is imprisonment. Likewise, if he remains silent without a disability according to Abu Yusuf, and the more likely view is that it is a denial, so he is sworn. He judges against him [i.e., against the defendant; due to the existence of conclusive evidence for judgment: admission in the first case, and evidence in the second case (Ayni)] The use of the word 'judgment' in the case of admission is by extension; because admission is evidence by itself, and does not depend on judgment. So the judge's ruling is an obligation to exit from what he admitted, unlike evidence, because it only becomes evidence by connection with judgment. (Kifayah, Fath) And otherwise he swears: i.e., if the defendant does not admit and the plaintiff does not provide evidence due to inability, the judge swears the defendant. And it is restricted to his swearing; because the defendant [is sworn] by the plaintiff's request. And the oath is not returned to the plaintiff, nor is evidence of the possessor accepted in absolute ownership. And the evidence of the outsider: if he swears by the plaintiff's request his oath before the judge without the judge administering the oath, this is not swearing; because administering the oath is the right of the judge. And if both parties agree that he should swear before someone other than the judge and be free, it is invalid. Likewise, if they compromise that if the plaintiff swears, the opponent is liable for the property, and he swears, he is not liable. By his request: because there is no swearing except after request from both parties in all lawsuits. According to Abu Yusuf: he is sworn without request in four places: the buyer in the return due to defect, the pre-emptor regarding not invalidating pre-emption, the woman when she requests the fixing of maintenance for her absent husband, and the claimant of ownership (mustahaqq). And they unanimously agree that one who claims a debt against a deceased, the judge swears him without request from the executor and heir. And it is restricted to the plaintiff's request; because if he does not request it, he is not sworn. (Fath)
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, explaining the procedure after a claim is made. If the defendant admits, judgment is given based on admission; if he remains silent or says 'neither admit nor deny,' he is imprisoned until he responds. According to Abu Yusuf, silence is treated as a denial and the defendant is sworn. Then the passage discusses the oath: if the defendant denies and the plaintiff has no evidence, the judge swears the defendant. The oath is only administered upon the plaintiff's request, except in certain cases where Abu Yusuf says it can be done without request (e.g., buyer returning defective goods, pre-emptor, wife claiming maintenance from absent husband, and the claimant of ownership). But the standard ruling (mufta bihi) is that the oath requires the plaintiff's request. The passage also clarifies that the oath must be performed before the judge; any agreement to swear elsewhere or to make the opponent liable if the plaintiff swears is invalid. The authority weight here is from the later commentaries (Fath, Ayni) but the core ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Admission by the defendant of the claim; it is a conclusive proof that does not require a court judgment for its validity.
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath administered by the judge to the defendant when the plaintiff has no evidence; it is a right of the judge and requires the plaintiff's request.
التحليف (al-taḥlīf): The act of administering the oath; it is exclusive to the judge, not a private matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Automatically by the judge without request
B) Only upon the plaintiff's request
C) Upon the request of either party
D) Only if the defendant requests it

Key Takeaway
The defendant's oath in Hanafi law is a right of the judge, but is administered only upon the plaintiff's request, except in a few disputed cases.

Returning the Oath (Radd al-Yamin): Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on returning the oath to the plaintiff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on returning the oath and explain why it disagrees with the Shafi'i view using the hadith and usul argument.

Core Idea
The oath is never returned to the plaintiff: the hadith's definite article makes the rule general for all deniers.

In court, when the defendant refuses to take the oath, what happens? The Hanafi school holds that the oath remains on the defendant absolutely; it is never transferred to the plaintiff. The Shafi'i school, however, allows the oath to be returned to the plaintiff. This lesson unpacks the legal proof from a key hadith and the Hanafi usul principle of generality (umum) that supports the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the Hanafi position
Identify the contested ruling: when a defendant refuses the oath (nukul), can the judge return it to the plaintiff? The Hanafi school says no: the oath stays with the defendant absolutely.
The Hanafi view is that nukul does not transfer the oath; other coercive measures may be used.
2
Present the opposing Shafi'i position and their reasoning
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one view) hold that if the defendant refuses, the oath is returned to the plaintiff, and if the plaintiff swears, he wins. Their reasoning: the defendant's refusal suggests falsity, so the plaintiff's oath becomes evidence.
They also analogize from the hadith of 'yamin ma'a al-shahid' (oath with one witness).
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil: the hadith and the usul argument
The Hanafi rely on the famous hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' They argue that the definite article 'al-' in 'al-yaminu' indicates generality (istighraq) — all oaths are on deniers. Returning the oath would violate this text.
This usul principle (alif lam li al-istighraq) is central to the Hanafi argument.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof (yamin ma'a al-shahid)
The Shafi'i cite a hadith allowing judgment with an oath plus one witness. The Hanafi respond that this hadith is weak (da'if), rejected by Yahya ibn Ma'in, and denied by its own narrator Suhayl ibn Salih. Thus it cannot oppose the authentic, general hadith.
The Hanafi response uses hadith criticism (jarh wa ta'dil) to neutralize the opposing proof.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and why the Hanafi view is mufta bihi
Practically, under Hanafi law, the judge does not accept the plaintiff's oath; he may imprison or coerce the defendant to swear. Under Shafi'i law, the plaintiff can swear and win. The Hanafi position is considered stronger due to the authenticity and clarity of the dalil.
The tarjih within the Hanafi school relies on the strength of the hadith and usul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يرد إلخ مطلقا سواء نكل الخصم أو لم ينكل. (فتح)] وقال الشافعي: إذا لم يكن للمدعي بينة أصلا وحلف القاضي المدعى عليه فنكل، يرد اليمين على المدعي، فإن حلف قضي له، وإلا لا، وبه قال مالك وأحمد في غير الأظهر؛ لأن يمين المدعى عليه محتملة للكذب والصدق، ويمين محتملة، بل هي دليل على ظهور صدق دعواه، فيحكم بها. المدعي . غير ولنا: قوله عليه: "لو أعطي الناس بدعواهم لادّعى أناس دماء رجال وأموالهم، لكن البينة على المدعي واليمين على أنكر"، رواه مسلم ، وأحمد جعل جنس اليمين على المنكر ؛ لأن الألف واللام للاستغراق؛ إذ لام التعريف تحمل من على الاستغراق، وتقدم على تعريف الحقيقة إذا لم يكن هناك معهود، وليس وراء الجنس شيء آخر من أفراد ذلك الجنس فيكون المعنى: أن جميع الأيمان على المنكرين، فلو ورد اليمين على المدعي لزم المخالفة لهذا النص، ولأنه عليه قسم بينهما، والقسمة تنافي الشركة، ولهذا لا تقبل بينة ذي اليد وأيضاً عند الشافعي: إذا أقام المدعي . شاهداً واحداً وعجز عن الآخر يحلف ويقضى له؛ لما روي أنه عليها قضى باليمين مع الشاهد الواحد، ولنا ما رويناه، وما رواه ضعیف رده يحيى بن معين، ولأنه يرويه ربيعة عن سهيل بن صالح وأنكره سهيل فلا يبقى حجة بعد ما أنكره الراوي، فضلاً عن أن يكون معارضاً لصحاح المشاهير. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۸/۲]
And it is not returned, etc., absolutely, whether the opponent refuses or does not refuse. (Fath) And al-Shafi'i said: If the plaintiff has no evidence at all and the judge swears the defendant, and he refuses (nukul), then the oath is returned to the plaintiff; if he swears, judgment is given for him, otherwise not. Malik and Ahmad also said this, except in the less apparent view. Because the defendant's oath is possible falsehood and truth, and the plaintiff's oath is also possible, rather it is evidence of the appearance of the truth of his claim, so judgment is made by it. But we have (our proof): His statement (peace be upon him): 'If people were given according to their claims, they would claim the blood of men and their property, but the burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the one who denies.' Reported by Muslim, and Ahmad made the genus of oath on the denier; because the definite article (alif lam) indicates generality; since the definite article carries 'man' towards universality, and takes precedence over the definition of reality when there is no specific referent, and there is nothing beyond the genus in individuals of that genus. So the meaning is: all oaths are on the deniers. So if the oath were returned to the plaintiff, it would contradict this text. And because the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided between them, and division negates partnership. For this reason, the evidence of the possessor is not accepted. And also according to al-Shafi'i: if the plaintiff produces one witness and is unable to produce another, he swears and judgment is given for him; based on the narration that he (peace be upon him) judged with an oath along with a single witness. But we have what we narrated, and what he narrated is weak; Yahya ibn Ma'in rejected it, and because it is narrated by Rabi'a from Suhayl ibn Salih, and Suhayl denied it, so it is no longer a proof after the narrator denied it, let alone being opposing to famous authentic hadiths. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:188)
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The issue is whether the oath can be returned to the plaintiff if the defendant refuses to take it. The Hanafi position is that the oath is never returned to the plaintiff; it stays with the defendant absolutely. Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one view) hold that if the defendant refuses (نكل), the oath is returned to the plaintiff, and if the plaintiff swears, judgment is given for him. Their reasoning: the defendant's oath might be false, and the plaintiff's oath can indicate the truth of his claim. The Hanafi response relies on the hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' The Hanafis argue that the definite article in 'al-yaminu 'ala al-munkir' indicates generality—all oaths are on deniers. Returning it to the plaintiff would violate this text. Additionally, the Prophet divided the roles between claimant and denier; returning the oath mixes them. They also reject the Shafi'i proof for the oath with a single witness (يمين مع شاهد), deeming the hadith weak and denied by its own narrator. The authority weight here comes from hadith analysis and usul al-fiqh arguments.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal by the defendant to take the oath after it is offered by the judge.
الجنس (al-jins): Genus or category; here referring to the category of oaths being on deniers, as indicated by the definite article.
رد اليمين (radd al-yamīn): Returning the oath from the defendant to the plaintiff after the defendant refuses to swear.
يمين مع شاهد (yamīn ma'a shāhid): The practice of accepting an oath along with one witness to establish a claim, used by Shafi'i as an analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The oath is returned to the plaintiff
B) The oath is never returned to the plaintiff
C) It depends on the judge's discretion
D) The plaintiff can demand a new oath

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the defendant's oath is never returned to the plaintiff, based on the generality of the hadith and the division of roles between claimant and denier.

Preference of the Outsider's Evidence Over the Possessor's in Ownership Disputes
Inter-madhhab dispute on evidence preference in ownership claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the outsider's evidence is preferred over the possessor's in absolute ownership claims, and identify the exceptions (earlier date, non-recurring cause) where the possessor's evidence prevails.

Core Idea
Possession gives advantage, so the outsider's evidence is needed to overcome it.

In Hanafi law, when both the possessor and an outsider claim absolute ownership of a property (without specifying a cause), the outsider’s evidence is generally accepted over the possessor’s. This may seem counterintuitive because possession (yad) is itself a strong indicator of ownership. However, the school reasons that the possessor already holds the property; requiring the outsider to provide evidence ensures fairness. This lesson unpacks the rule, its exceptions (earlier dates and non-recurring causes), and contrasts it with the Shafi’i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Rule
When both parties present evidence without dates or with the same date, the possessor's evidence is rejected and judgment goes to the outsider. This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Apply the Dating Exception
If the possessor's evidence has an earlier date, it is accepted and he wins. The earlier date suggests a prior right that predates the other's claim.
3
Distinguish Recurring vs Non-Recurring Causes
For non-recurring causes (e.g., offspring, milking), the possessor's earlier evidence prevails. For recurring causes (e.g., building, planting), the outsider wins even if the possessor's date is earlier.
Non-recurring: cause cannot be repeated; recurring: can be done repeatedly.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the possessor's evidence is stronger because it is confirmed by possession. Understand how this leads to a different practical outcome.
5
Synthesize the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under Hanafi law, an outsider can win against a possessor with evidence (subject to exceptions); under Shafi'i, the possessor's evidence would generally trump, making it harder for the outsider.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بينة إلخ يعني لو ادعى خارج داراً أو منقولاً ملكاً مطلقاً بأن قال: هذا ملكي، وسكت عن السبب، وذو اليد ادعاه كذلك، وبرهنا و لم يؤرخا أو أرخا تاريخاً واحداً، لا تقبل بينة ذي اليد ويقضى للخارج إلا أن يكون تاريخ ذي اليد أسبق، فحينئذ يقضى لذي اليد، ولو ادعى الخارج الملك المطلق، وذو اليد الشراء من فلان، وبرهنا وأرخا، وتاريخ ذي اليد أسبق، فإنه يقضى للخارج. (عيني ، فتح) وبينة الخارج: بيان لقوله: "ولا" بينة لذي اليد" أما إذا ادعى ذو اليد نتاج دابة أو ادعيا تلقي الملك من واحد، وأحدهما قابض، أو ادعيا الشراء وأرّخا تاريخا وتاريخ ذي اليد أسبق، فإن في هذه الفصول تقبل بينة ذي اليد بالإجماع إذا كان سبباً لا يتكرر، مثل: غزل القطن والكتان وحلب اللبن، وإن كان يتكرر كالبناء والغرس يقضى للخارج وإن سبق تاريخ ذي اليد، وقال الشافعي بينة ذي اليد أولى بالعمل بها؛ لأن بينة ذي اليد تأكدت باليد،
And no evidence of the possessor, etc., means: if an outsider claims absolute ownership of a house or movable, saying 'this is mine' without stating a cause, and the possessor also claims it likewise, and both provide evidence without dating or with the same date, the possessor's evidence is not accepted and judgment is given for the outsider, unless the possessor's date is earlier, in which case judgment is for the possessor. And if the outsider claims absolute ownership and the possessor claims purchase from so-and-so, and both provide evidence with dates, and the possessor's date is earlier, then judgment is for the outsider. (Ayni, Fath) And the evidence of the outsider is an explanation of his saying: 'and no evidence for the possessor.' However, if the possessor claims the offspring of an animal, or both claim receipt (talqi al-milk) from one person with one of them in possession, or both claim purchase with dates and the possessor's date is earlier, then in these cases the possessor's evidence is accepted by consensus if the cause is non-recurring, such as spinning cotton and flax, and milking milk. But if it is recurring, like construction and planting, judgment is for the outsider even if the possessor's date is earlier. And al-Shafi'i said: the evidence of the possessor is more worthy of being acted upon, because the possessor's evidence is strengthened by possession.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage addresses a key rule in Hanafi fiqh: in a dispute over ownership, the evidence of the person who is not in possession (خارج) is preferred over that of the possessor (ذو اليد) when both claim absolute ownership without specifying a cause. However, there are exceptions: if the possessor's evidence has an earlier date, or if the claim involves a non-recurring cause (like offspring, receipt, or purchase with an earlier date), the possessor's evidence is accepted. For recurring causes like construction, the outsider still prevails. The passage also mentions the Shafi'i view that the possessor's evidence should be preferred because possession strengthens it. The Hanafi position is based on the principle that the possessor already has the advantage of possession, so evidence is needed to overcome the presumption; the burden of proof is on the outsider. The authority weight here is from the later commentaries, but the rule is well-established in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الخارج (al-khārij): The person who is not in possession of the disputed property; the claimant opposing the possessor.
ذو اليد (dhū al-yad): The person in possession of the disputed property; the defendant in a claim of ownership.
الملك المطلق (al-milk al-muṭlaq): Absolute ownership claimed without specifying a cause (e.g., saying 'this is mine' without mentioning purchase, inheritance, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi passage, when both parties present evidence with the same date, whose evidence is accepted?
A) The possessor's
B) The outsider's
C) Neither
D) The judge decides based on other factors

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the outsider's evidence is generally preferred over the possessor's in absolute ownership claims, but the possessor can prevail if his evidence has an earlier date or the claim involves a non-recurring cause.

intermediate
8 min
The Oath is on the Denier – Not Returned to the Plaintiff
Inter-madhhab dispute on oath return after nukul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on returning the oath after nukul, explain the Hanafi proof from the hadith, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The oath belongs exclusively to the denier; nukul results in judgment, not oath return.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi school and other schools regarding the procedure when a defendant refuses to swear. The Hanafi position, grounded in the prophetic hadith and the principle of division of roles, holds that the oath is never returned to the plaintiff. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi usul al-fiqh in action, particularly the interpretation of generality in the hadith.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its practical effect
Identify the Hanafi position: the oath is NOT returned to the plaintiff, and nukul leads to judgment against the defendant. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: in Shafi'i fiqh, the plaintiff gets the oath.
The rule is absolute – applies whether the defendant refuses or not.
2
Analyze the Hanafi dalil (the hadith)
The key proof is the hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier.' Apply Hanafi usul: the definite article 'al' in 'al-yamin' indicates generality ('umum), so ALL oaths are on deniers. This argument is based on the linguistic rule that 'al' when used for the category (jins) and not for a specific instance implies universality.
also consider the division (qismah) argument – roles are separated, so mixing is prohibited.
3
Examine the opposing view and its proof
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one view) hold that if the defendant refuses (nukul), the judge offers the oath to the plaintiff. Their proof: the defendant's oath is probable, and his refusal increases the probability of the plaintiff's truth. They also cite a hadith about judgment with one witness plus oath.
The opposing proof is based on probability (zann) and a specific report.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response and determine the tarjih
The Hanafi school responds by: (a) affirming the clear generality of the hadith; (b) rejecting the opposing hadith as weak (da'if) – it is narrated by Rabi'a from Suhayl ibn Salih, and Suhayl himself denied it; (c) using qiyas: the division of roles (claimant with evidence, denier with oath) precludes mixing. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
The tarjih authority is the strength of the textual evidence and the absence of a valid counter-dalil.

Source Text

[رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۸/۲]
By his request, and an oath is not returned to a plaintiff, and there is no evidence for the possessor in absolute ownership, and the evidence of the outsider. If the plaintiff requests his oath before the judge without the judge administering the oath, this is not an administration of oath because administering the oath is the right of the judge. If they agree to swear before a non-judge and be cleared, it is invalid. Similarly, if they settle that if the plaintiff swears, the opponent guarantees the property, and he swears, he does not guarantee. By his request: because there is no administration of oath except after request by both parties in all claims. According to Abu Yusuf: He administers the oath without request in four places: the buyer in the return due to defect, the pre-emptor on not invalidating his pre-emption, the woman when she demands the fixing of maintenance from her absent husband, and the rightful claimant. And they agreed that whoever claims a debt against a deceased, the judge administers the oath to him without the request of the executor or heir. And it is restricted to the request of the plaintiff because if he does not request it, no oath is administered upon him. (Fath) 'And no oath is returned' etc. absolutely, whether the opponent refuses to swear or not. (Fath)] And al-Shafi'i said: If the plaintiff has no evidence at all and the judge administers the oath to the defendant and he refuses, the oath is returned to the plaintiff; if he swears, judgment is given for him, otherwise not. Malik and Ahmad (in one view) said the same because the defendant's oath is subject to falsehood and truth, and the plaintiff's oath is also subject to falsehood and truth, but it is evidence of the appearance of the truth of his claim, so judgment is given by it. The plaintiff. Not. And our proof: His statement عليه: "If people were given according to their claims, they would claim the blood and property of men, but the burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier." Reported by Muslim. And Ahmad made the category of oath on the denier because the definite article 'alif lam' indicates generality; since the definite article carries 'man' to generality, and it precedes the definition of the true nature when there is no particular, and there is nothing beyond the category other than individuals of that category, so the meaning is that all oaths are on deniers. If the oath were returned to the plaintiff, it would violate this text. And because he عليه divided between them, and division precludes partnership. Therefore, the evidence of the possessor is not accepted. Also, according to al-Shafi'i: If a plaintiff brings one witness and is unable to bring the other, he swears and judgment is given for him because of the report that he عليه gave judgment based on the oath with one witness. Our proof is what we reported, and what he reported is weak, rejected by Yahya ibn Ma'in, and because it is narrated by Rabi'a from Suhayl ibn Salih, and Suhayl denied it, so it remains no proof after the narrator denied it, let alone being in opposition to famous authentic reports. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 188/2]
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning whether a judge may return the oath to the plaintiff if the defendant refuses to swear (nukul). The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states the Hanafi ruling: the oath is never returned to the plaintiff, and the judge administers the oath only at the plaintiff's request. The commentator (Fath al-Qadir) elaborates that this is absolute—whether the defendant refuses to swear or not. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad (in one view) hold that if the defendant refuses, the oath is returned to the plaintiff; he may swear and win the case. The Hanafi school responds with the hadith 'The burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier,' arguing that the definite article 'al' in 'al-yamin' implies generality, meaning all oaths are on deniers. Additionally, the division of roles—claimant with evidence, denier with oath—precludes mixing (shirkah). The passage also rebuts the Shafi'i position on the oath with one witness, deeming the relevant hadith weak and denied by its own narrator.

Key Terms
اليمين على المنكر (al-yamin 'ala al-munkir): The legal principle that the oath is due from the defendant (the one who denies the claim), not from the plaintiff.
النكول (al-nukul): The refusal of the defendant to take the oath after it is offered by the judge.
التحليف (al-tahlif): The act of administering an oath by the judge; a right of the judge and cannot be delegated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the consequence of the defendant's refusal to swear (nukul)?
A) The judge offers the oath to the plaintiff.
B) The judge rules against the defendant immediately.
C) The judge compels the defendant to swear by imprisonment.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the oath is exclusively on the denier, and nukul leads to judgment against the defendant, not return of the oath.

Evidence Priority in Absolute Ownership Claims
Ruling on evidence priority in absolute ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for prioritizing evidence in absolute ownership claims and distinguish when the possessor's evidence is accepted.

Core Idea
In absolute ownership, the outsider's evidence trumps the possessor's because the hand already proves ownership.

This passage addresses a procedural question in qada: when both an outsider and a possessor claim absolute ownership of property and bring evidence, whose evidence takes priority? The Hanafi school has a nuanced rule that gives preference to the outsider's evidence unless the possessor has an earlier date or claims a non-repetitive cause. This ruling is contrasted with the Shafi'i position and justified by the principle that the hand is itself a proof of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claim type
Determine that the claim is for absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq) without specifying a cause. The parties are an outsider (kharrij) and a possessor (dhu al-yad).
Absolute ownership means no cause is mentioned, e.g., simply 'this is mine'.
2
Apply the default rule
If both produce evidence with no date or the same date, the outsider's evidence is accepted and judgment for the outsider.
The possessor's evidence is not accepted because the hand already indicates ownership.
3
Apply the exception for earlier date
If the possessor's evidence has an earlier date, then the possessor wins, even in absolute ownership.
The earlier date shows the possessor's ownership predates the hand.
4
Distinguish specific causes
When the claim is based on a specific non-repetitive cause (e.g., birth, spinning), the possessor's evidence is accepted by consensus. For repetitive causes (e.g., building, planting), the outsider's evidence prevails even if the possessor has an earlier date.
The hand does not indicate non-repetitive causes, so evidence is needed.
5
Analyze the Shafi'i disagreement
Compare the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i position that the possessor's evidence is strengthened by possession. Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the hand is already proof, making possessor's evidence redundant.
This disagreement only applies to absolute ownership claims, not specific causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بينة إلخ يعني لو ادعى خارج داراً أو منقولاً ملكاً مطلقاً بأن قال: هذا ملكي، وسكت عن السبب، وذو اليد ادعاه كذلك، وبرهنا و لم يؤرخا أو أرخا تاريخاً واحداً، لا تقبل بينة ذي اليد ويقضى للخارج إلا أن يكون تاريخ ذي اليد أسبق، فحينئذ يقضى لذي اليد، ولو ادعى الخارج الملك المطلق، وذو اليد الشراء من فلان، وبرهنا وأرخا، وتاريخ ذي اليد أسبق، فإنه يقضى للخارج. (عيني، فتح) وبينة الخارج: بيان لقوله: "ولا" بينة لذي اليد" أما إذا ادعى ذو اليد نتاج دابة أو ادعيا تلقي الملك من واحد، وأحدهما قابض، أو ادعيا الشراء وأرّخا تاريخا وتاريخ ذي اليد أسبق، فإن في هذه الفصول تقبل بينة ذي اليد بالإجماع إذا كان سبباً لا يتكرر، مثل: غزل القطن والكتان وحلب اللبن، وإن كان يتكرر كالبناء والغرس يقضى للخارج وإن سبق تاريخ ذي اليد، وقال الشافعي بينة ذي اليد أولى بالعمل بها؛ لأن بينة ذي اليد تأكدت باليد، = أي القاضي للمدعي وهو النكول الحقيقي أي القاضي - فصار كما إذا أقاما البينة على النتاج أو على نكاح امرأة في يد أحدهما فإنه يكون أولى، وكذا لو ادعيا أمة أنها أمته دبرها أو أعتقها أو استولدها، وأقاما بينة كانت بينة صاحب اليد أولى ولنا: أن بينة الخارج أكثر إثباتاً وإظهاراً؛ لأن قدر ما أثبته اليد لا تثبته بينة ذي اليد؛ إذ اليد دليل مطلق الملك، بخلاف النتاج؛ لأن اليد لا تدل عليه وكذا على الإعتاق وأخويه وعلى الولاء الثابت بها، وهذا الاختلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي في الملك المطلق، وأما إذا أقام ذو اليد البينة : الملك بسبب كما في النتاج، فإن بينة ذي اليد أحق بالإجماع. (عيني، فتح) أحق من بينة ذي اليد إن أقاما بينة. (عيني)
'And no evidence' etc. means: If an outsider claims a house or movable property as absolute ownership by saying 'this is mine' and remains silent about the cause, and the possessor claims it likewise, and they both bring evidence and do not date or date with the same date, the evidence of the possessor is not accepted and judgment is given for the outsider, unless the date of the possessor is earlier, then judgment is given for the possessor. If the outsider claims absolute ownership and the possessor claims purchase from so-and-so, and both bring evidence and date, and the possessor's date is earlier, then judgment is given for the outsider. (Ayni, Fath) 'And the evidence of the outsider' is an explanation of his words 'and no evidence for the possessor'. As for when the possessor claims the birth of an animal, or both claim receiving ownership from one person and one of them is in possession, or both claim purchase and date and the possessor's date is earlier, then in these chapters the evidence of the possessor is accepted by consensus if the cause is not repetitive, such as spinning cotton and linen and milking milk. But if it is repetitive like building and planting, judgment is given for the outsider even if the possessor's date is earlier. Al-Shafi'i said: The evidence of the possessor is more deserving to be acted upon because the evidence of the possessor is strengthened by possession. = That is, the judge [offers the oath] to the plaintiff, and it is real nukul, meaning the judge. So it becomes as if both brought evidence on birth or on a marriage of a woman in the hands of one of them, then he is more deserving. Likewise, if both claimed a slave girl that she is his mother who was given a tadbir or freed or produced a child, and brought evidence, the evidence of the possessor is more deserving. And our proof: The evidence of the outsider is more proving and more apparent because to the extent that the hand has proven, the evidence of the possessor does not prove it; since the hand is evidence of absolute ownership, unlike birth because the hand does not indicate it, and likewise for freeing and its two siblings and for the wala' established by it. This difference between us and al-Shafi'i is in absolute ownership. As for when the possessor brings evidence of ownership by a cause like birth, then the evidence of the possessor is more deserving by consensus. (Ayni, Fath) More deserving than the evidence of the possessor if both bring evidence. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning conflicts of evidence between an outsider (third party) and the current possessor (dhu al-yad) when both claim absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq) without specifying a cause. The Hanafi position is that the outsider's evidence is preferred over the possessor's, unless the possessor's evidence has an earlier date. However, if the possessor claims ownership by a specific cause that is not repetitive and the hand does not indicate it (e.g., birth of an animal), then the possessor's evidence is preferred by consensus. The commentator (Ayni, Fath) explains the rule: the hand is itself a proof of absolute ownership, so the possessor's evidence adds nothing; the outsider's evidence, on the other hand, establishes ownership anew. Repetitive causes like building or planting do not shift the preference. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing that the combination of hand and evidence strengthens the possessor's case. The Hanafi response is that the hand already proves absolute ownership, so the possessor's evidence is superfluous; the outsider's evidence provides independent proof. This dispute only applies to absolute ownership claims; when the cause is specific and non-repetitive, consensus favors the possessor.

Key Terms
الملك المطلق (al-mulk al-mutlaq): Absolute ownership claimed without specifying how it was acquired (e.g., purchase, inheritance).
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who is currently in possession of the property that is the subject of the claim.
بينة الخارج (bayyinat al-kharrij): Evidence brought by the person who is not in possession (the outsider).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If both an outsider and a possessor claim absolute ownership of a house and both bring evidence without any date, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The possessor's evidence is preferred because he is in possession.
B) The outsider's evidence is preferred.
C) The case is dismissed for lack of valid evidence.
D) Both parties share the ownership equally.

Key Takeaway
In absolute ownership claims, the Hanafi school prefers the outsider's evidence because the hand already proves ownership, making the possessor's evidence superfluous; exceptions exist for earlier dates and non-repetitive causes.

Nukul (Refusal to Swear) and Its Legal Effect
Ruling with proof on nukul and its effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nukul, explain the proof from ijma, and distinguish real nukul from legal nukul.

Core Idea
The defendant's refusal to swear leads to judgment against him; the oath is not returned to the plaintiff.

This passage establishes the Hanafi rule that when a defendant refuses to take the oath after being properly offered, the judge may rule against him. It distinguishes between explicit refusal and silence, and presents the consensus of the Companions as the primary proof. The passage also notes differences within the Hanafi school and with al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Nukul (refusal to swear) is a procedural act. Its legal consequence is that the judge rules against the defendant. Note the two types: real nukul (explicit 'I will not swear') and legal nukul (silence).
The judge must offer the oath three times as a recommendation.
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi school relies on ijma (consensus) of the Companions. The key incident is with Shuraih and Ali, where Ali affirmed judgment by nukul without returning the oath.
Other Companions: Umar, Ibn Abbas, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit action of the Companions and Ali's approval ('You are correct') constitute a consensus. In Hanafi usul, ijma of the Companions is a qat'i proof that overrides qiyas.
Shafi'i's proof from another report of Ali is countered by this established consensus.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Two main differences: 1) Shafi'i returns the oath to the plaintiff; 2) Within Hanafi school, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require the judge to offer three times as obligatory (hatm lazim), while the relied-upon view is that it is recommended (mustahab).
Also, on whether falsehood is revealed: Abu Yusuf says yes (ta'zir), Muhammad says no; fatwa on Abu Yusuf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling: judgment by nukul is valid after three recommended offers. The defendant's falsehood does not become apparent unless Abu Yusuf's view is followed for ta'zir.
Authority: Fath al-Qadir and al-Inayah support the recommended (mustahab) view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحق، وقضى له إن نكل مرة بـ "لا أحلف"، أو سكت، وعرض اليمين ثلاثا ندبا، = أي القاضي للمدعي وهو النكول الحقيقي أي القاضي - فصار كما إذا أقاما البينة على النتاج أو على نكاح امرأة في يد أحدهما فإنه يكون أولى، وكذا لو ادعيا أمة أنها أمته دبرها أو أعتقها أو استولدها، وأقاما بينة كانت بينة صاحب اليد أولى ولنا: أن بينة الخارج أكثر إثباتاً وإظهاراً؛ لأن قدر ما أثبته اليد لا تثبته بينة ذي اليد؛ إذ اليد دليل مطلق الملك، بخلاف النتاج؛ لأن اليد لا تدل عليه وكذا على الإعتاق وأخويه وعلى الولاء الثابت بها، وهذا الاختلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي في الملك المطلق، وأما إذا أقام ذو اليد البينة : الملك بسبب كما في النتاج، فإن بينة ذي اليد أحق بالإجماع. (عيني، فتح) أحق من بينة ذي اليد إن أقاما بينة. (عيني) إن نكل: [أي المدعى عليه نكولا مرة صريحا بقوله : لا أحلف (عيني) لأن النكول بذل أي ترك منع أو إقرار فيه شبهة البدل، فلا يوجب شيئاً إلا بالقضاء، ولابد أن يكون النكول في مجلس القاضي، سواء كان صريحاً بأن قال: "لا أحلف" وهو النكول الحقيقي، أو دلالة بأن سكت ولم يقل شيئاً وهو النكول الحكمي، وقال الشافعي: لا يقضى بنكوله، بل يرد اليمين على المدعي إذا نكل المدعى عليه فإن حلف يقضى له بالمال، وإن نكل انقطعت المنازعة بينهما؛ لما روي عن علي الا الله أنه حلف المدعي بعد نكول المدعى عليه. ولنا: إجماع الصحابة له على ما ذكرنا، وروي عن علي الا الله أيضاً أنه وافق إجماعهم، فإنه روي عن شريح له: أن المنكر طلب منه رد اليمين على المدعي، فقال له: ليس لك إليه سبيل، وقضى بالنكول بين يدي عليه، فقال له علي: قالون، ومعناه بالرومية أصبت رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۸/۲] وكذا روي عن عمر وابن عباس ، وهو مذهب أبي موسى الأشعري له، وإذا ثبت الإجماع بطل القياس. (فتح، عناية) أو سكت: أي نكل دلالة بسكوته وهو النكول الحكمي (عيني) ثلاثاً ندباً: [أي ثلاث مرات من جهة الاستحباب] يقول له في كل مرة: إني أعرض عليك اليمين، فإن حلفت وإلا قضيت عليك بما ادعاه إعلاماً له بالحكم؛ لأنه موضع خطاً لاختلاف العلماء فيه، فإن الشافعي لا يراه، فإذا كرر القول عليه ولم يحلف، حكم عليه إذا علم أنه لا آفة به من طرش وخرس، وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد أن التكرار حتم لازم حتى لو قضى القاضي بالنكول مرة لا ينفذ، والصحيح: أنه ينفذ، والعرض ثلاثاً . مستحب، ولابد أن يكون النكول في مجلس القاضي، ثم إذا حلف المدعى عليه فالمدعي على دعواه، لا يبطل حقه بيمينه إلا أنه ليس له أن يخاصمه ما لم تقم البينة على وفق دعواه، فإذا وجد بينة وأقامها عليه، قضى له بالدعوى حينئذ. القضاة السلف لا بعض من يسمعون البينة بعد الحلف، وهذا قول مهجور غير مأخوذ به؛ لأن عمر له قبل البينة من المدعي بعد يمين المنكر، ثم المدعي إذا أقام البينة بعد حلف المدعى عليه هل يظهر كذب المدعى عليه أم لا؟ والصواب أنه لا يظهر كذبه حتى لا يعاقب عقوبة شاهد الزور، وقيل: عند أبي يوسف: يظهر كذبه حتى يعزّر، وعليه الفتوى، وعند محمد لا يظهر رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۹/۲]
More deserving, and he judges for him if he refuses once by saying 'I will not swear,' or remains silent, and he presents the oath three times as a supererogatory practice. That is, the judge [offers] to the plaintiff, and it is real nukul, meaning the judge. So it becomes as if both brought evidence on the birth or on a marriage of a woman in the hands of one of them, then he is more deserving. Likewise, if both claimed a slave girl that she is his mother given tadbir or freed or produced a child, and brought evidence, the evidence of the possessor is more deserving. And our proof: The evidence of the outsider is more proving and more apparent because to the extent that the hand has proven, the evidence of the possessor does not prove it; since the hand is evidence of absolute ownership, unlike birth because the hand does not indicate it. This difference between us and al-Shafi'i is in absolute ownership. As for when the possessor brings evidence of ownership by a cause like birth, then the evidence of the possessor is more deserving by consensus. (Ayni, Fath) More deserving than the evidence of the possessor if both bring evidence. (Ayni) If he refuses: [i.e., the defendant refuses once explicitly by saying 'I will not swear' (Ayni) because nukul is an abandonment, a prevention or an admission containing a resemblance of substitute, so it does not obligate anything except by judgment. It is necessary that nukul occurs in the court of the judge, whether explicit by saying 'I will not swear' which is real nukul, or by implication by remaining silent, which is legal nukul. Al-Shafi'i said: Judgment is not given by his nukul, rather the oath is returned to the plaintiff if the defendant refuses; if he swears, the property is adjudged to him, and if he refuses, the dispute between them ends. For it is reported from Ali رضي الله عنه that he made the plaintiff swear after the defendant's nukul. And our proof: The consensus of the Companions as we mentioned, and it is also reported from Ali رضي الله عنه that he agreed with their consensus, for it is reported from Shuraih رضي الله عنه that the denier asked him to return the oath to the plaintiff, and he said to him: You have no way to that, and he judged by nukul before him, then Ali said to him: Qalun, meaning in Greek: you are correct. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 188/2] Similarly, it is reported from Umar and Ibn Abbas, and it is the madhhab of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari رضي الله عنه. When consensus is established, analogy is invalidated. (Fath, Inayah) Or remains silent: i.e., he refused by implication by being silent, which is legal nukul. (Ayni) Three times as supererogatory: [i.e., three times from the perspective of recommendation] He says to him each time: I present the oath to you; if you swear, otherwise I will judge against you according to what he claimed, informing him of the ruling. Because this is a place of error due to the difference of scholars, for al-Shafi'i does not see it. So when he repeats the statement to him and he does not swear, he judges against him when he knows that there is no impediment like deafness or muteness. From Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that the repetition is obligatory and necessary; even if the judge judges by nukul once, it is not executed. The correct view is that it is executed, and presenting three times is recommended. It is necessary that nukul occurs in the court of the judge. Then when the defendant swears, the plaintiff remains on his claim; his right is not invalidated by the oath, except that he cannot sue him unless he produces evidence in accordance with his claim. So if he finds evidence and presents it against him, then judgment is given to him. Some of the early judges used not to hear evidence after an oath, but this is an abandoned opinion not acted upon because Umar رضي الله عنه accepted evidence from the plaintiff after the oath of the denier. Then when the plaintiff produces evidence after the defendant's oath, does the defendant's falsehood become apparent or not? The correct view is that his falsehood does not become apparent, so he is not punished with the punishment of a false witness. It is said: according to Abu Yusuf, his falsehood becomes apparent so he is reprimanded (ta'zir), and this is the mufta bihi. According to Muhammad, it does not become apparent. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 189/2]
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the definition and effects of nukul (refusal to swear an oath). The Hanafi position is that when the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) is offered the oath and refuses—either explicitly saying 'I will not swear' (real nukul) or by remaining silent (legal nukul)—the judge rules against him after offering the oath three times. This is a recommended practice, not obligatory, though Abu Yusuf and Muhammad considered it necessary. The judge must ensure the defendant has no excuse (e.g., deafness). The passage then discusses the post-nukul situation: if the defendant eventually swears, the plaintiff's right is not voided; he can later bring evidence and win. There is also an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the defendant's falsehood becomes apparent if evidence emerges later: Abu Yusuf says it does (leading to ta'zir), while Muhammad says it does not; the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. The commentary brings a consensus argument: the Companions (e.g., Ali, Umar, Ibn Abbas, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari) upheld judgment by nukul, and a story of Shuraih and Ali confirms this. This consensus overrides qiyas.

Key Terms
النكول الحقيقي (al-nukul al-haqiqi): Explicit refusal to swear by saying 'I will not swear' (la ahlif).
النكول الحكمي (al-nukul al-hukmi): Tacit refusal to swear by remaining silent after the judge's offer.
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the Companions, which is a binding proof in the Hanafi system.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on nukul (refusal to swear) according to the mufta bihi?
A) The judge must return the oath to the plaintiff.
B) The judge rules against the defendant after one refusal; offering three times is recommended.
C) The judge must offer the oath three times as an obligatory condition.
D) The defendant cannot be judged until he confirms his refusal three times.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules against a defendant who refuses to take the oath, relying on the consensus of the Companions, and does not return the oath to the plaintiff.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Ruling on Nukul (Refusal to Take an Oath) – Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Defines nukul and its rulings, including the judge's decision and the recommendation to repeat the offer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on nukul and explain the Hanafi proof based on ijma.

Core Idea
Nukul (refusal to swear) is an implicit admission in Hanafi law, leading to judgment against the defendant.

This lesson examines the legal consequences when a defendant in a court refuses to take an oath. The Hanafi school treats such refusal (nukul) as a transfer of the claim or abandonment of defense, leading to a judgment against the defendant. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing that the oath should be returned to the plaintiff. We will explore the evidence from the consensus of the Companions that supports the Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: if the defendant refuses to swear (explicitly or implicitly), the judge passes judgment against him.
The refusal must occur in the judge's court.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the oath is returned to the plaintiff after the defendant's refusal.
This is based on a hadith from 'Ali.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i proof: a narration that 'Ali made the plaintiff swear after the defendant refused.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the same narration shows 'Ali approved of Shurayh's judgment based on nukul, and there is a clear consensus of the Companions that invalidates the analogy.
The consensus is narrated from 'Umar, Ibn 'Abbas, Abu Musa, and Shurayh.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Contrast the outcomes: Hanafi: immediate judgment against defendant. Shafi'i: plaintiff must still swear to win.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن نكل: [أي المدعى عليه نكولا مرة صريحا بقوله : لا أحلف (عيني) لأن النكول بذل أي ترك منع أو إقرار فيه شبهة البدل، فلا يوجب شيئاً إلا بالقضاء، ولابد أن يكون النكول في مجلس القاضي، سواء كان صريحاً بأن قال: "لا أحلف" وهو النكول الحقيقي، أو دلالة بأن سكت ولم يقل شيئاً وهو النكول الحكمي، وقال الشافعي: لا يقضى بنكوله، بل يرد اليمين على المدعي إذا نكل المدعى عليه فإن حلف يقضى له بالمال، وإن نكل انقطعت المنازعة بينهما؛ لما روي عن علي الا الله أنه حلف المدعي بعد نكول المدعى عليه. ولنا: إجماع الصحابة له على ما ذكرنا، وروي عن علي الا الله أيضاً أنه وافق إجماعهم، فإنه روي عن شريح له: أن المنكر طلب منه رد اليمين على المدعي، فقال له: ليس لك إليه سبيل، وقضى بالنكول بين يدي عليه، فقال له علي: قالون، ومعناه بالرومية أصبت رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۸/۲] وكذا روي عن عمر وابن عباس ، وهو مذهب أبي موسى الأشعري له، وإذا ثبت الإجماع بطل القياس. (فتح، عناية) أو سكت: أي نكل دلالة بسكوته وهو النكول الحكمي (عيني) ثلاثاً ندباً: [أي ثلاث مرات من جهة الاستحباب] يقول له في كل مرة: إني أعرض عليك اليمين، فإن حلفت وإلا قضيت عليك بما ادعاه إعلاماً له بالحكم؛ لأنه موضع خطاً لاختلاف العلماء فيه، فإن الشافعي لا يراه، فإذا كرر القول عليه ولم يحلف، حكم عليه إذا علم أنه لا آفة به من طرش وخرس، وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد أن التكرار حتم لازم حتى لو قضى القاضي بالنكول مرة لا ينفذ، والصحيح: أنه ينفذ، والعرض ثلاثاً . مستحب، ولابد أن يكون النكول في مجلس القاضي، ثم إذا حلف المدعى عليه فالمدعي على دعواه، لا يبطل حقه بيمينه إلا أنه ليس له أن يخاصمه ما لم تقم البينة على وفق دعواه، فإذا وجد بينة وأقامها عليه، قضى له بالدعوى حينئذ. القضاة السلف لا يسمعون البينة بعد الحلف، وهذا قول مهجور غير مأخوذ به؛ لأن عمر له قبل البينة من المدعي بعد يمين المنكر، ثم المدعي إذا أقام البينة بعد حلف المدعى عليه هل يظهر كذب المدعى عليه أم لا؟ والصواب أنه لا يظهر كذبه حتى لا يعاقب عقوبة شاهد الزور، وقيل: عند أبي يوسف: يظهر كذبه حتى يعزّر، وعليه الفتوى، وعند محمد لا يظهر رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۹/۲]
If he refuses: meaning the defendant refuses once explicitly by saying 'I will not swear' (Ayni) because nukul is a transfer, meaning leaving off preventing or an admission that carries a semblance of substitute, so it does not necessitate anything except by judgment. And it is necessary that the refusal be in the court of the judge, whether explicit by saying 'I will not swear'—and that is the real refusal—or implicitly by being silent and not saying anything—and that is the legal refusal. And al-Shafi'i said: judgment is not passed upon his refusal, but the oath is returned to the plaintiff if the defendant refuses; if he swears, judgment is passed for him for the property; if he refuses, the dispute between them is cut off; due to what was narrated from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he made the plaintiff swear after the defendant's refusal. And we have: the consensus of the Companions regarding what we mentioned. And it is also narrated from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he agreed with their consensus. For it is narrated from Shurayh (may Allah be pleased with him): that the denier asked him to return the oath to the plaintiff, and he said to him: 'You have no path to that,' and he passed judgment based on refusal before 'Ali, and 'Ali said to him: 'Qalun' which in Greek means 'you hit the mark.' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:188) Similarly narrated from 'Umar and Ibn 'Abbas, and it is the madhhab of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari (may Allah be pleased with him). And when consensus is established, qiyas is invalid. (Fath, Inayah) Or he is silent: meaning he refuses implicitly by his silence, and that is the legal refusal. (Ayni) Three times as a recommendation: meaning three times on the side of recommendation. He says to him each time: 'I offer you the oath; if you swear, otherwise I will pass judgment against you for what he claims' informing him of the ruling because it is a point of error due to the difference of scholars regarding it, for al-Shafi'i does not see it. So if he repeats the statement to him and he does not swear, he passes judgment against him when he knows that there is no deficiency in him such as deafness or muteness. And from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that repetition is necessary and binding such that if the judge passes judgment based on refusal once, it is not executed. And the correct view is that it is executed, and offering three times is recommended. And it is necessary that the refusal be in the court of the judge. Then if the defendant swears, the plaintiff remains on his claim; his right is not nullified by his oath, except that he may not contest him until he produces evidence in accordance with his claim. If he finds evidence and presents it against him, then judgment is passed for him on the claim. The early judges did not hear evidence after the oath, and this is an abandoned statement not followed; because 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) accepted evidence from the plaintiff after the oath of the denier. Then, if the plaintiff presents evidence after the defendant's oath, does the defendant's falsehood become apparent or not? The correct view is that his falsehood does not become apparent, so that he is not punished with the punishment of a false witness. And it is said: according to Abu Yusuf: his falsehood becomes apparent so that he is disciplined, and the fatwa is upon it; and according to Muhammad, it does not become apparent. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:189)
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on nukul (refusal to take an oath) when the defendant is required to swear. The Hanafi position is that if the defendant explicitly refuses by saying 'I will not swear' or implicitly by silence, the judge passes judgment against him (i.e., in favor of the plaintiff). This is based on the consensus of the Companions, as narrated from 'Ali, 'Umar, Ibn 'Abbas, and others. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that the oath should be returned to the plaintiff. The text clarifies that the refusal must occur in the judge's court. The judge should offer the oath three times as a recommendation, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, repetition is obligatory; the correct view is that the judge's judgment after one refusal is valid. After the defendant swears, the plaintiff's claim is not invalidated, but he cannot contest until he brings evidence. There is a difference of opinion on whether presenting evidence after the oath reveals the defendant's falsehood.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): The defendant's refusal to take an oath; considered a transfer (بذل) or leaving off of defense, leading to a judgment against him.
النكول الحقيقي (al-nukul al-haqiqi): Explicit refusal by the defendant saying 'I will not swear'.
النكول الحكمي (al-nukul al-hukmi): Implicit refusal by the defendant remaining silent when the oath is presented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if the defendant explicitly refuses to take an oath?
A) The oath is returned to the plaintiff.
B) The judge rules against the defendant.
C) The case is dismissed.
D) The defendant is imprisoned until he swears.

Key Takeaway
Nukul in Hanafi law is treated as an admission by the defendant, leading to a judgment against him without the plaintiff needing to swear.

Abu Hanifa on Six Claims Where Defendant Is Not Sworn
States the six types of claims where defendant is not sworn, with reasoning and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on when a defendant is not sworn, explain the talil (nukul as bazl), and compare the Sahibayn's opposing view, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The defendant is not sworn in six personal-status claims because nukul is bazl, not iqrar — a difference that shapes judicial procedure.

This lesson examines a key procedural dispute within the Hanafi school: when does the defendant's refusal to take an oath (nukul) become a basis for judgment? Imam Abu Hanifa restricted it to property matters, while his two companions extended it to most claims. We'll explore the reasoning and the practical impact on the judge's decision.

Guided Path
1
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
Note the six matters where the defendant is not sworn according to Abu Hanifa: nikah, raj'a, fay', istilad, riqq, nasab, wala'. Also note that hadd and li'an are excluded by consensus.
These are personal-status rights, not property.
2
Understand the legal cause (talil)
Abu Hanifa treats nukul as bazl (concession), not iqrar. Bazl applies only where rights can be legitimized by consent initially, like property. In these matters, bazl is not valid, so nukul cannot lead to judgment.
This preserves the defendant's dignity by not forcing an oath that would be meaningless.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's counter-argument
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad treat nukul as iqrar (confession), which is valid in all claims. Therefore, the defendant is sworn; if he refuses, judgment is against him.
They rely on the general hadith 'al-yamin 'ala man ankara'.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to the hadith
Abu Hanifa does not reject the hadith. He argues that the purpose of the oath — enabling judgment by nukul — is not achievable in these matters, so the obligation falls, like prayer is excused for one unable to perform.
This is an example of istihsan overriding general proof.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
According to Qadi Fakhr al-Din and Imam al-Bazdawi, the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view: the defendant is sworn in all claims except hadd. Some later scholars give the judge discretion.
In practice, a Hanafi judge will likely administer the oath in most cases, but must be aware of the underlying dispute.
