Oath on the Result vs Cause in Lawsuits: General Rule and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Detailed ruling on when to swear on the result rather than the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between swearing on the cause (sabab) and the result (hasil) in Hanafi lawsuits, apply the majority rule for removable causes, identify exceptions, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's dissenting view.

Core Idea
When the cause can be undone, the oath is on the existing effect, not the past event.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explores a precision point in Hanafi procedural law: how to frame an oath when the underlying legal transaction may have been nullified after its occurrence. The school's majority rule avoids perjury while protecting both parties, but Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone involved in Islamic judicial procedure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
The lawsuit arises from a cause (sale, marriage, etc.) that can be nullified after occurrence. If the defendant swore he never did the act, but he did and later it was rescinded, he would either commit perjury or the plaintiff's claim would fail unjustly.
Example: A man sells an item, then rescinds the sale. The buyer sues. If the seller swears 'I never sold,' he lies if the sale happened. But swearing 'no existing sale' is truthful.
2
Learn the Hanafi Solution
The defendant swears on the negation of the current legal effect (hasil), not the original cause. The principle: 'The purpose of causes is their legal effects, so he swears on the negation of effects, not causes.'
This applies when the cause can be removed by a legal remover (e.g., rescission, divorce).
3
Memorize the Four Oath Formulas
For sale: 'There is no existing sale between you.' For marriage: 'There is no existing marriage.' For usurpation: 'You must return it or its substitute.' For divorce: 'She is not irrevocably divorced from you.'
The formulas negate the current legal outcome, not the past act.
4
Identify the Exceptions
If the defendant does not believe in the right claimed (e.g., a Shafi'i in a pre-emption case by adjacency), swearing on the hasil would cause him no guilt, harming the plaintiff. Then the oath is on the cause by consensus. Also, for non-removable causes like emancipation of a Muslim slave, the oath is on the cause because the status cannot revert.
These exceptions protect the plaintiff's right to a meaningful oath.
5
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the oath should always be on the cause because the oath is the plaintiff's right. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) maintain the hasil rule. The relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwaya) is that of the majority.
The thamarah al-khilaf: In a sale case, Abu Yusuf would swear 'I did not sell,' while the majority swears 'no existing sale.' The latter avoids perjury if rescission occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحلف إلخ : أي على صورة إنكار المنكر، وهو صورة دعوى المدعي، قاله في "البحر" [٣٠٥/٧]. وفي "الخلاصة": أي حاصل الدعوى، وقال الزيلعي تبيين الحقائق ٣٤٣/٥]: المقصود من الأسباب أحكامها، فيحلف على نفي الأحكام لا على نفي الأسباب، وظاهره أن الحاصل هو الحكم المرتب على السبب، وهو الأظهر . ثم اعلم أن إدخال النكاح في المسائل التي يحلف فيها على الحاصل عند الطرفين محمول على ما إذا كان مع النكاح دعوى المال، وهو قوله: "ما يجب عليك رده فيه قصور ؛ إذ لا يشمل الهالك، فلو زاد "أو بدله"؛ ليشمل المثل لو كان المغصوب مثلياً، أو القيمة لو كان قيمياً، لكان أولى. وكذا في قوله: "ما هي بائن منك"؛ لأنه خاص بالبائن، وأما الرجعي فيحلف بالله ما هي خالق في النكاح الذي بينكما. وقوله: "في دعوى البيع والنكاح والغصب والطلاق" أي يحلف على الحاصل في هذه الصور مطلقاً، ادعى أنه ابتاع . هذا عبداً بألف فجحد حلف بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم، ولا يحلف بالله ما بعت، فلعله باعه من حتى إذا ثم أقاله. فلو حلف على نفي أصل السبب يكون كاذباً، ولو لم يحلف يجب تسليم المبيع العائد إلى ملكه بالإقالة؛ لأنه لو ادعى المعنى الطارئ لا يقبل منه، فإذا حلف على الحاصل فقد وفى للمدعي والمدعى عليه حقهما؛ لعلمنا أن مقصود المدعي من دعوى البيع ونحوه ثبوت الحكم، ومتى أمكن إيفاء حقهما كان أولى من إيفاء حق أحدهما وإتلاف حق الآخر، ثم في هذا الكلام لف ونشر على الترتيب. والأصل: أن الدعوى إذا وقعت في سبب يرتفع بعد وقوعه، وليس في تحليفه على الحاصل ضرر بالمدعي كالصور المذكورة، فإن البيع يرتفع بالإقالة والغصب يرتفع بالرد وبالهبة والنكاح بالطلاق والخلع، والطلاق بتجديد العقد أو الرجعة، فإن اليمين فيها تكون على الحاصل لا على السبب عند الطرفين فلا يحلف بالله ما نكحت، ولا بالله ما بعت، ولا بالله ما غصبت، ولا بالله ما طلقت؛ لأن هذه الأشياء قد تقع ثم ترتفع برافع كالطلاق والإقالة والهبة والنكاح الجديد فلا يمكن تحليفه على السبب فيحلفه على الحاصل؛ لكي لا يتضرر للمدعى عليه؛ لأنه لو أقر بالسبب ثم ادعى طروء ،الرافع، لا يقبل منه، فيحتال بهذا الطريق؛ إذ لا ضرر فيه على المدعي؛ لأن المقصود من الأسباب أحكامها فيحلف على نفيها لا على نفي السبب، إلا إذا كان في التحليف على الحاصل ترك النظر = على الحاصل أي بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم، ونكاح قائم وما يجب عليك ردّه، وما بائن منك الآن في دعوى البيع والنكاح والغصب والطلاق. وإن ادعى شفعة إذا كانت الدعوى في الطلاق هي بالجوار أو نفقة المبتوتة والمشتري أو الزوج لا يراها . أي بالمجاورة زوج المبتوتة البائنة ! أي المدعي في جانب المدعي، فحينئذ يحلف على السبب بالإجماع كمسألتي المتن وهما دعوى الشفعة والنفقة، وعند أبي يوسف يحلف في الجميع على السبب أي فيما يرتفع بعد وقوعه ومالا يرتفع؛ لأن اليمين حق المدعي، فيحلف على وفق دعواه، والمذهب في التحليف قول الطرفين، وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعند الثاني: يحلف على السبب إلا إذا قال المدعى عليه إذا عرض عليه القاضي اليمين أيها القاضي قد يبيع الإنسان شيئاً ثم يقايله، فحينئذ يحلفه القاضي على الحاصل وعنه أن القاضي ينظر إلى إنكار المدعى عليه، فإن أنكر السبب كالمبيع ونحوه، يحلف على السبب، وإن أنكر الحكم يحلف على الحاصل، وعليه أكثر القضاة، وقال فخر الإسلام: يفوض إلى رأي القاضي. وإن كان سبباً لا يرتفع برافع فالتحليف على السبب إجماعاً، كالعبد المسلم إذا ادعى العتق على مولاه وجحد المولى يحلف على السبب، بخلاف الأمة ولو مسلمة والعبد الكافر؛ لأن الرق يتكرر عليهما بالارتداد ونقض العهد، ثم الالتحاق بدار الحرب، ولا يتكرر على العبد المسلم؛ إذ لا يقبل منه إلا الإسلام أو السيف عند ارتداده فيحلف المولى على السبب بالله ما أعتقه لعدم الضرورة إلى التحليف على الحاصل؛ إذ لا يجوز أن الرق بعد العتق مسلماً؛ لأن تكرار الرق إنما يكون بتقدير وقوع الاستيلاء عليه بعد الارتداد، وهو بالنسبة إلى المسلم ليس بمتصور؛ لأنه يقتل بالارتداد. (عيني، فتح، عناية) بيع قائم: في الحال إذا كانت الدعوى في المبيع. (عيني ونكاح قائم أي وما بينكما نكاح قائم في الحال إذا يعود كانت الدعوى في النكاح. ( عيني) عليك رده أو بدله في الحال إذا كانت الدعوى في الغصب. (عيني) في دعوى: يرجع إلى قوله : "وما بينكما بيع قائم (عيني) والنكاح: أي في دعوى النكاح يرجع إلى قوله: ونكاح قائم (عيني) والغصب : أي وفي دعوى الغصب يرجع إلى قوله : "ما يجب عليك رده". (عيني) والطلاق أي وفي دعوى الطلاق يرجع إلى قوله: "وما هي بائن منك". (عيني) أو نفقة: أي ادعى نفقة المرأة المطلقة البائنة. (عيني) والمشتري: أي والحال أن مشتري الدار التي ادعى المدعي شفعتها بالجوار. (عيني) لا يراها بإفراد الضمير على واحد من الشفعة والنفقة بالنظر لدعوى الشفيع ودعوى المبتوتة، وفي نسخة: "والمشتري والزوج بحرف الواو ، ولا" يراهما بضمير التثنية؛ لوجوب المطابقة بين الضمير والمرجع أي لا يعتقد الشفعة ونفقة المبتوتة بأن كان المشتري أو الزوج شافعي المذهب، فلو حلف على الحاصل: بالله ما له عليّ حق الشفعة يحلف ولا يحنث في معتقده؛ لأنه لا يرى الشفعة يستحق بالجواز فيفوت النظر في حق المدعي، =
And he swears etc.: i.e., upon the form of denial of the defendant, which is the form of the plaintiff's claim, as said in al-Bahr. In al-Khulasa: i.e., the substance of the claim. Al-Zayla'i said: The purpose of causes is their legal effects, so he swears on the negation of the effects, not on the negation of the causes. That which appears is that the substance is the legal effect attached to the cause, and that is most apparent. Then know that including marriage in the cases where the oath is on the substance according to both parties is interpreted as when with marriage there is a claim for wealth. His statement: 'What you must return' has a shortcoming as it does not include the destroyed item. So if he added 'or its substitute' to include the similar if the usurped item is fungible, or the value if it is valuable, it would be better. Similarly in his statement: 'She is irrevocably divorced from you' because it is specific to irrevocable divorce. As for revocable divorce, he swears by Allah that she is not free in the marriage that is between you. And his statement: 'In the claim of sale, marriage, usurpation, and divorce' means he swears on the substance in these cases absolutely. If someone claims he bought this slave for one thousand and he denies, he swears by Allah there is no existing sale between you, and he does not swear by Allah that he did not sell, for perhaps he sold and then rescinded. If he swears on the negation of the original cause, he would be a liar, and if he does not swear, the sold item which had returned to his ownership by rescission must be handed over; because if he claims the intervening meaning it would not be accepted from him. So when he swears on the substance, he has fulfilled the right of both the plaintiff and defendant; because we know that the intention of the plaintiff in the claim of sale and similar is the affirmation of the legal effect. And whenever it is possible to fulfill both rights, it is better than fulfilling one's right and destroying the other's right. Then in this speech there is a wrapped and spread in order. The principle is: When the lawsuit occurs regarding a cause that can be removed after its occurrence, and there is no harm to the plaintiff in making him swear on the substance, like the mentioned cases: sale can be removed by rescission, usurpation by return or gift, marriage by divorce or khul', divorce by renewal of contract or return. So in these the oath is on the substance not the cause according to both parties. So he does not swear by Allah that he did not marry, nor that he did not sell, nor that he did not usurp, nor that he did not divorce; because these things may have occurred and then been removed by something that removes them, like divorce, rescission, gift, new marriage. So he cannot be made to swear on the cause; so he is made to swear on the substance, in order not to harm the defendant; because if he admits the cause then claims the intervening remover, it is not accepted from him. So this method is adopted; since there is no harm to the plaintiff because the purpose of causes is their legal effects, so he swears on their negation not on the negation of the cause, except if swearing on the substance involves disregarding consideration. On the substance: i.e., by Allah there is no existing sale between you, no existing marriage, and what you must return, and she is not irrevocably divorced from you now in the claim of sale, marriage, usurpation, and divorce. If he claims pre-emption if the lawsuit is about divorce by adjacency or maintenance of a woman irrevocably divorced, and the buyer or the husband does not believe it. i.e., by adjacency, the husband of the irrevocably divorced woman! i.e., the plaintiff in the plaintiff's side, then he swears on the cause by consensus, like the two issues of the text: the claim of pre-emption and maintenance. According to Abu Yusuf, he swears on the cause in all: i.e., both in what can be removed after its occurrence and what cannot, because the oath is the right of the plaintiff, so he swears according to his claim. And the school in the matter of making an oath is the statement of both parties (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and that is Zahir al-Riwaya. According to the second (Abu Yusuf): he swears on the cause, except when the defendant says when the judge presents the oath to him: "O judge, a person may sell something and then rescind it." So then the judge makes him swear on the substance. And from him that the judge looks at the defendant's denial: if he denies the cause, like the sold item etc., he swears on the cause; if he denies the legal effect, he swears on the substance. And upon this is most of the judges. Fakhru'l-Islam said: it is left to the judge's opinion. If the cause is not removable by a remover, then the oath is on the cause by consensus, like a Muslim slave who claims emancipation from his master and the master denies; the master swears on the cause, in contrast with a female slave even if Muslim and a non-Muslim slave; because slavery is repeatedly applicable to them by apostasy and breach of contract, then joining the land of war, and it does not repeat on the Muslim slave; since from him is accepted only Islam or the sword upon his apostasy. So the master swears on the cause by Allah he did not emancipate him for lack of necessity to swear on the substance, because it is not possible for slavery to return after emancipation for a Muslim; because the repetition of slavery is only by supposition of capture after apostasy, and that is not conceivable for a Muslim because he is killed for apostasy. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inaya). 'Existing sale': immediately if the lawsuit is about the sold item. ('Ayni) 'Existing marriage': i.e., and there is no existing marriage between you immediately if the lawsuit is about marriage. ('Ayni) 'What you must return or its substitute' if the lawsuit is about usurpation. ('Ayni) 'In the claim': refers to his statement 'and there is no existing sale between you'. ('Ayni) 'And marriage': i.e., in the claim of marriage refers to his statement 'and no existing marriage'. ('Ayni) 'And usurpation': i.e., in the claim of usurpation refers to his statement 'what you must return'. ('Ayni) 'And divorce': i.e., in the claim of divorce refers to his statement 'and she is not irrevocably divorced from you'. ('Ayni) 'Or maintenance': i.e., he claimed maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman. ('Ayni) 'And the buyer': i.e., while the buyer of the house for which the plaintiff claimed pre-emption by adjacency. ('Ayni) 'Does not see it' with singular pronoun for either pre-emption or maintenance considering the claim of the pre-emptor and the claim of the irrevocably divorced woman. In a copy: 'And the buyer and the husband with the letter waw, and does not see them' with dual pronoun for required agreement between pronoun and referent, meaning they do not believe in pre-emption and maintenance of the irrevocably divorced, because the buyer or husband is of the Shafi'i school. So if he swears on the substance: by Allah there is no right of pre-emption for him against me, he swears and does not break his oath in his belief, because he does not believe pre-emption is owed by adjacency, so consideration for the plaintiff's right is lost. =
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the oath in certain lawsuits should be upon the cause (sabab) or the result (hasil). The general rule: when the underlying cause (e.g., sale, marriage, usurpation, divorce) can be nullified after occurrence (by rescission, divorce, return, etc.), the oath is on the result—the defendant swears that there is no existing right now. This protects the defendant from being forced to admit a cause that has been removed, while still fulfilling the plaintiff's need for proof. The specific formulas: for sale, 'there is no existing sale'; for marriage, 'there is no existing marriage'; for usurpation, 'you must return it or its substitute'; for divorce, 'she is not irrevocably divorced from you'. Exceptions: If the defendant does not believe in the right claimed (e.g., a Shafi'i buyer in a pre-emption case does not recognize pre-emption by adjacency), swearing on the result would allow him to break his oath without penalty, harming the plaintiff. In such cases, the oath is on the cause by consensus. Abu Yusuf differs: he holds that the oath should always be on the cause because the oath is the plaintiff's right. But the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwaya) is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the oath is on the result when the cause is removable. If the cause cannot be removed (e.g., a Muslim slave's emancipation), the oath is on the cause by consensus because slavery cannot return for a Muslim.

Key Terms
الحاصل (al-hasil): The legal effect (hukm) resulting from a cause; in oaths, the existing right or status at the time of the lawsuit.
السبب (al-sabab): The original act or contract (sale, marriage, etc.) that gives rise to a legal effect.
اليمين (al-yamin): Oath; a legal tool to resolve a lawsuit when evidence is lacking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a lawsuit for sale where the buyer claims he bought a slave for 1000 and the seller denies, how does the defendant swear according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) By Allah I did not sell
B) By Allah there is no existing sale between us
C) By Allah I never owned that slave
D) By Allah I did not receive the price

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the oath in removable-cause lawsuits is on the result (hasil) to avoid perjury, except when the defendant does not believe the right or the cause is non-removable; Abu Yusuf's dissenting view is marjuh but reflects legitimate ijtihad.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Swearing on the Cause vs. the Current State in Judicial Oaths
Explains when defendant is sworn on cause or current state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a defendant is sworn on the cause versus the current state and apply the Hanafi rules to determine the proper oath form.

Core Idea
The oath form depends on what the defendant denies: the cause or the legal effect.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, the default oath denies the current existence of the claimed right (al-hasil). However, when the defendant denies the very cause of the claim or when the cause cannot be undone, the oath shifts to the cause (al-sabab). This lesson unpacks the rule, the intra-Hanafi dispute, and the exceptional cases where oath on the cause is required by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default oath on the current state
The default form of oath (yamin ala al-hasil) denies the present existence of the claimed right, e.g., 'there is no existing sale.' This is used when the defendant acknowledges the cause but denies its effect.
2
Identify when the oath shifts to the cause
The oath shifts to the cause (yamin ala al-sabab) when the defendant denies the underlying cause itself (e.g., 'I did not buy'), or when the cause cannot be reversed by any legal means (e.g., emancipating a Muslim slave).
Two conditions: (1) defendant denies the cause, or (2) the cause is irreversibly effective.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf held that the oath is always on the cause because the oath is the claimant's right. The majority (including Fakhr al-Islam) looked at the defendant's denial: if he denies the cause, swear on cause; if he denies the ruling, swear on current state.
The mufta bihi is the majority view: judge examines the defendant's denial.
4
Apply the exception for irreversible causes
When the cause cannot be removed (e.g., freeing a Muslim slave), the oath is on the cause by consensus, as there is no possibility of recurrence.
Compare with a female Muslim slave or a male non-Muslim slave, where slavery can recur due to apostasy or war.

كتاب الدعوى

كيفية اليمين والاستحلاف

على الحاصل أي بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم، ونكاح قائم وما يجب عليك ردّه، وما بائن منك الآن في دعوى البيع والنكاح والغصب والطلاق. وإن ادعى شفعة إذا كانت الدعوى في الطلاق هي بالجوار أو نفقة المبتوتة والمشتري أو الزوج لا يراها . أي بالمجاورة زوج المبتوتة البائنة ! أي المدعي في جانب المدعي، فحينئذ يحلف على السبب بالإجماع كمسألتي المتن وهما دعوى الشفعة والنفقة، وعند أبي يوسف يحلف في الجميع على السبب أي فيما يرتفع بعد وقوعه ومالا يرتفع؛ لأن اليمين حق المدعي، فيحلف على وفق دعواه، والمذهب في التحليف قول الطرفين، وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعند الثاني: يحلف على السبب إلا إذا قال المدعى عليه إذا عرض عليه القاضي اليمين أيها القاضي قد يبيع الإنسان شيئاً ثم يقايله، فحينئذ يحلفه القاضي على الحاصل وعنه أن القاضي ينظر إلى إنكار المدعى عليه، فإن أنكر السبب كالمبيع ونحوه، يحلف على السبب، وإن أنكر الحكم يحلف على الحاصل، وعليه أكثر القضاة، وقال فخر الإسلام: يفوض إلى رأي القاضي. وإن كان سبباً لا يرتفع برافع فالتحليف على السبب إجماعاً، كالعبد المسلم إذا ادعى العتق على مولاه وجحد المولى يحلف على السبب، بخلاف الأمة ولو مسلمة والعبد الكافر؛ لأن الرق يتكرر عليهما بالارتداد ونقض العهد، ثم الالتحاق بدار الحرب، ولا يتكرر على العبد المسلم؛ إذ لا يقبل منه إلا الإسلام أو السيف عند ارتداده فيحلف المولى على السبب بالله ما أعتقه لعدم الضرورة إلى التحليف على الحاصل؛ إذ لا يجوز أن الرق بعد العتق مسلماً؛ لأن تكرار الرق إنما يكون بتقدير وقوع الاستيلاء عليه بعد الارتداد، وهو بالنسبة إلى المسلم ليس بمتصور؛ لأنه يقتل بالارتداد.
Book of Claims. The manner of the oath and administering the oath. On the current state, meaning: 'By Allah, there is no existing sale between you two, no existing marriage, and what is obligatory upon you to return, and what is irrevocably separated from you now' in claims of sale, marriage, usurpation, and divorce. And if he claims pre-emption when the claim is about divorce by adjacency or maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman, and the buyer or the husband does not see it [i.e., does not believe in that right]. So at that point he swears on the cause by consensus, like the two issues of the text: claims of pre-emption and maintenance. And according to Abu Yusuf, he swears on the cause in all cases—that is, in matters that can be removed after their occurrence and matters that cannot be removed—because the oath is the right of the claimant, so he swears according to his claim. The madhhab regarding administering the oath is the statement of both sides, and it is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya). And according to the second [Abu Hanifa? Or another view? Actually, the text says 'ind al-thani' meaning Abu Yusuf again? The translation is tricky.], he swears on the cause unless the defendant says when the judge presents the oath: 'O judge, a person may sell something and then rescind it,' then the judge makes him swear on the current state. And from him [Abu Yusuf], that the judge looks at the defendant's denial: if he denies the cause, like the sold item and similar, he swears on the cause; if he denies the ruling, he swears on the current state. And most judges act on this. And Fakhr al-Islam said: it is left to the judge's discretion. And if the cause cannot be removed by any remover, then administering the oath on the cause is by consensus, like a Muslim slave who claims emancipation from his master and the master denies: he swears on the cause, unlike a female slave even if Muslim and a non-Muslim male slave, because slavery can recur upon them through apostasy and violation of covenant, then joining the abode of war, and it does not recur upon the Muslim slave because he accepts nothing but Islam or the sword upon his apostasy. So the master swears on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not emancipate him' due to no need to swear on the current state, because it is not possible for slavery to return after emancipation for a Muslim, since the recurrence of slavery would only occur by being captured after apostasy, and that is inconceivable for a Muslim because he is killed for apostasy.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the general principle of how a defendant is sworn in a claim. The default is that the defendant swears on the current state (al-hasil), meaning he denies that the claimant has any current right against him (e.g., 'there is no existing sale, marriage, etc.'). However, when the defendant does not even acknowledge the underlying cause (sabab) of the claim—such as when the claimant's right is based on a cause that the defendant denies exists—then the defendant is sworn on the cause instead. For example, in pre-emption (shuf'a) and maintenance (nafaqa) for an irrevocably divorced woman, the defendant may not believe in the right itself (e.g., a Shafi'i who does not recognize pre-emption by adjacency or maintenance for a ba'ina woman). In such cases, the oath is on the cause by consensus (ijma'). The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the oath should always be on the cause in all types of claims, because the oath is the claimant's right and should be according to his claim. But the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the judge considers the nature of the defendant's denial—if he denies the cause, he swears on the cause; if he denies the legal effect (hukm), he swears on the current state. Fakhr al-Islam leaves it to the judge's discretion. An exception is when the cause cannot be undone by any legal means (e.g., emancipation of a Muslim slave): then the oath is on the cause by consensus, because there is no possibility of recurrence.

Key Terms
اليمين على الحاصل (al-yamin ala al-hasil): An oath denying the current existence of the claimed right (e.g., 'there is no existing sale').
اليمين على السبب (al-yamin ala al-sabab): An oath denying the underlying cause of the claim (e.g., 'I did not buy').
سبب لا يرتفع برافع (sabab la yartafi' bi rafi'): A cause that cannot be reversed by any legal means, such as emancipation of a Muslim slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab (mufta bihi), in which scenario is the defendant sworn on the current state (al-hasil)?
A) The defendant says: 'I did not buy the property.'
B) The defendant says: 'There is no existing obligation for me to pay.'
C) The defendant is a Shafi'i denying pre-emption by adjacency.
D) The defendant's master is accused of emancipating a Muslim slave.

Key Takeaway
The oath form depends on the defendant's denial: denying the cause triggers oath on the cause; denying the effect (hukm) triggers oath on the current state, with exceptions for irreversible causes.

Oath on Cause in Pre-emption and Maintenance
Addresses oath administration when defendant denies the right's basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when a defendant's personal belief would nullify an oath on the current state, the judge must administer the oath on the cause (sabab) to protect the claimant's right.

Core Idea
When a defendant's belief makes a current-state oath ineffective, the judge must swear on the objective cause to protect the claimant's right.

Imagine a Shafi'i defendant who denies a claim for pre-emption by adjacency because his school does not recognize that right. If the judge simply asked him to swear 'I owe you no pre-emption,' he would truthfully swear in his belief, and the claimant's right would be lost. The Hanafi school solves this by having the judge administer an oath on the cause—the underlying factual event—so that the oath remains effective regardless of the defendant's personal fiqh views. This lesson explores that procedural rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Locate the two scenarios in the passage: pre-emption by adjacency (shuf'a bil-jiwar) and maintenance for the irrevocably divorced woman (nafaqat al-mabtutah). Understand why the defendant's Shafi'i belief conflicts with the claim.
In both cases, the defendant does not believe the right exists at all.
2
Recognize the problem with swearing on the current state
See why swearing on the hasil (current state) is ineffective: the defendant would swear truthfully according to his belief and would not break the oath, thus the claimant's right is thwarted.
The phrase 'fayfutu al-nazar' indicates loss of consideration for the claimant.
3
State the Hanafi solution: oath on the cause
The judge must administer the oath on the sabab (cause): the buyer swears 'I did not buy the house in your neighborhood' and the husband swears 'she is not a waiting woman.' This is an objective fact the defendant cannot deny if it occurred.
The text says 'يحلف على السبب' – he swears on the cause.
4
Address the objection about harming the defendant
Consider the potential objection: the defendant may have bought but later waived his right, so swearing he did not buy would be false. The response: the judge must harm one of the two, and it is more just to harm the defendant because the claimant's right is based on the original state (the purchase or divorce), and the defendant's alleged loss of right is due to contingent causes that must be proven.
The principle 'al-asl' (the original state) is invoked: adhere to the original until evidence of change.
5
Note the underlying legal principle
Extract the general rule: when a defendant's personal belief would make a hasil oath ineffective, the judge must switch to an oath on the sabab to preserve the claimant's right. This reflects the Hanafi commitment to protecting rights via objective facts.
This is a procedural rule in Hanafi qada.

(عيني، فتح، عناية)
Or maintenance: that is, she claimed maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman. (Ayni) And the buyer: that is, while the buyer of the house for which the claimant claimed pre-emption by adjacency. (Ayni) does not see it with the pronoun singular for one of pre-emption and maintenance, considering the claim of the pre-emptor and the claim of the irrevocably divorced woman; and in a copy: 'and the buyer and the husband' with the conjunction waw, and 'they do not see them' with the dual pronoun, due to the necessity of agreement between pronoun and referent—meaning they do not believe in pre-emption and maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman, because the buyer or husband is of the Shafi'i school. So if he swore on the current state: 'By Allah, I owe you no right of pre-emption', he swears and does not break his oath in his belief, because he does not see that pre-emption is deserved by adjacency, so the consideration for the claimant is lost. = Similarly, he should not swear on the current state in the case of maintenance by saying: 'By Allah, I owe her no maintenance from the aspect she claims', because he swears and does not break his oath in his belief, since he does not see the obligation of maintenance for the irrevocably divorced woman, so the consideration for the female claimant is lost. He swears on the cause: [by consensus, that he swears: By Allah, I did not buy] and the buyer says: 'By Allah, I did not buy a house in the adjacency of the claimant', and the husband says: 'By Allah, she is not a waiting woman'. If it is said: by swearing on the cause, the defendant is harmed because it is possible that he bought and then waived the pre-emption or remained silent about the demand, and his harm is not greater than the harm to the claimant, the answer is that he is more entitled to that because the judge has no alternative but to cause harm to one of them, and the claimant claims what is based on the original state, because when purchase is proven, the right is established for him, and its lapse only occurs through incidental causes, so one must adhere to the original until evidence of the incidental is established. (Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah)
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the manner of oath in two specific cases: pre-emption (shuf'a) by adjacency and maintenance (nafaqa) for an irrevocably divorced woman. The problem arises when the defendant (buyer or husband) is a Shafi'i who does not believe in the legal validity of these rights. If such a defendant were sworn on the current state (hasil)—e.g., 'By Allah, I owe you no right of pre-emption'—he would swear truthfully according to his belief, and the claimant's right would be thwarted. Therefore, the judge must swear the defendant on the cause (sabab) instead: the buyer swears 'By Allah, I did not buy the house in your adjacency' and the husband swears 'By Allah, she is not a waiting woman (mu'tadda).' This ensures the oath is on something objective (the purchase or the divorce) that the defendant cannot deny in conscience if it occurred. The text then addresses an objection: does swearing on the cause harm the defendant unfairly? For example, he may have bought but later waived his right to pre-emption, so swearing he did not buy would be false. The answer is that the judge must harm one of the two litigants, and it is more just to harm the defendant because the claimant's right is based on the original state (purchase or divorce), while the defendant's alleged loss of right is due to contingent causes (waiver, silence). The original state is presumed until the contingent cause is proven. This reflects the Hanafi principle of adhering to the original (al-asl) until a countervailing evidence is established.

Key Terms
الشفعة بالجوار (al-shuf'a bil-jiwar): The right of pre-emption based on adjacency (neighbor's right to buy first), which is recognized by Hanafis but not by Shafi'is.
المبتوتة (al-mabtutah): An irrevocably divorced woman (ba'ina) who is not entitled to maintenance according to Shafi'i, but is entitled according to Hanafi during her waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Swear him on the current state: 'I owe you no right of pre-emption'.
B) Swear him on the cause: 'I did not buy the property'.
C) Swear him on his madhhab: 'I do not believe in pre-emption'.
D) Swear him on the Quran: 'I swear by Allah'.

Key Takeaway
When a defendant's personal belief would render a current-state oath ineffective, the judge must administer the oath on the cause (sabab) to protect the claimant's right.

Oath on Knowledge vs. Oath on Certainty
Distinguishes when to swear on knowledge or certainty in claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an oath on knowledge and an oath on certainty and apply the Hanafi rule to scenarios involving the defendant's own act or another's act, including the exception of radd bil-'ayb.

Core Idea
Oath on your own act = certainty; on another's act = knowledge; the seller's guarantee creates an exception.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, the form of the oath required from the defendant depends on whether the claim concerns the defendant's own act or the act of another. This lesson explores the general rule, its Quranic derivation, and an important exception when the seller guarantees freedom from defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
Determine whether the claim concerns the defendant's own act (e.g., buying, giving a gift) or the act of another (e.g., what the deceased did).
This determines whether the oath will be on certainty or knowledge.
2
Assess defendant's personal knowledge
If the defendant claims personal knowledge of the matter, he must swear on certainty. If he disclaims knowledge, he swears on knowledge.
Example: a depositary claiming the owner took the deposit swears on certainty because he claims knowledge.
3
Apply the general rule
If the act is the defendant's own, he swears on certainty (e.g., 'I did not buy'). If it is another's act, he swears on knowledge (e.g., 'I have no knowledge that this belongs to you').
Derived from the Quranic oath of the Jews: first on certainty (their act) then on knowledge (knowing a killer).
4
Recognize the exception: radd bil-'ayb
In a claim for return due to defect, the seller swears on certainty even though the defect is the act of another (the slave). This is because the seller guaranteed the item free from defects, making the oath relate to his own guarantee.
Al-Halwani stated this is the only exception to the general principle.
5
Apply the rule when defendant claims knowledge
If the defendant says 'I know the claim is false', he must swear on certainty. Examples: depositary who claims the owner received the deposit, or agent who claims the principal received the price.
In all cases where certainty is required, swearing on knowledge is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحلف على السبب: [اتفاقاً بأن يحلف بالله ما اشتريت وما طلقت (ط، معدن] بأن يقول المشتري: بالله ما اشتريت داراً في جوار المدعي، ويقول الزوج بالله ما هي معتدة، فإن قيل: بالحلف على السبب يتضرر المدعى عليه؛ لجواز أن يكون قد اشترى وسلم الشفعة أو سكت عن الطلب، وليس الضرر به بأولى بالضرر من المدعي، أجيب بأنه أولى بذلك؛ لأن القاضي لا يجد بدأ من إلحاق الضرر بأحدهما والمدعي يدعي ما أصل؛ لأن هو الشراء إذا ثبت يثبت الحق له، وسقوطه إنما يكون بأسباب عارضة فيجب التمسك بالأصل حتى يقوم الدليل على العارض. (عيني، فتح، عناية)

وعلى العلم: أي لو ورث رجل عبداً فادعاه شخص آخر بأن العبد له ولا بينة للمدعي، يحلف الرجل الوارث على العلم بأن يقول: بالله ما لي علم بأن هذا العبد للمدعي، ولا يحلف على القطع بأن العبد ليس ملكا له؛ لأن الوارث لا يعلم بما فعل المورث فيمتنع عن اليمين فيلحقه بذلك ضرر، وهو محق ظاهراً فلا يصار إليه دفعاً للضرر عنه، وهذا إذا تحقق الإرث بعلم القاضي وإقرار المدعي أو بينة المدعى عليه، فإن لم يتحقق كونه ميرا مراثاً على البتات. ولو قال المؤلف: "لو ادعى على الوارث عيناً أو ديناً مكان لو ورث عبداً"، لكان أولى؛ ليشمل دعوى الدين على الميت عيني (فتح فادعاه بأن العبد له ولا بينة للمدعي وعلى البتات: أي لو وهب العبد المدعي للمدعى عليه، أو اشترى المدعى عليه العبد المدعي ثم ادعاه آخر، ولا بينة للمدعي، يحلف المدعى عليه على القطع لا على العلم والمراد وصول العبد إلى المدعى عليه بسبب اختياري ولو كان غير الشراء والهبة.
ووجه الحلف على القطع أن الهبة والشراء سبب موضوع للملك باختيار المالك ومباشرته فيطلق له الحلف على القطع. والأصل فيه أن اليمين متى وقعت على فعل الغير فاليمين على العلم ومتى وقعت على فعل نفسه يكون على البتات، ألا ترى أنه عليها حلف اليهود " بالله ما قتلتم، ولا علمتم له قاتلاً فحلفهم على البتات أولاً؛ لأنه فعلهم، وفي الثاني على العلم؛ لأنه فعل غيرهم قال الحلواني: هذا أصل مستقيم في المسائل كلها إلا في مسألة الرد بالعيب، فإنه إذا ادعى المشتري أن العبد آبق ونحو ذلك - وأراد تحليف البائع - فإنه يحلف على البتات مع أن الإباق فعل غيره وإنما كان كذلك؛ لأن البائع ضمن تسليم المبيع سالماً . عن العيوب، فالتحليف يرجع إلى ما ضمن بنفسه، فيحلف على البتات رمز الحقائق: ١٩٤/٢]
وهذا إذا قال المنكر : لا علم لي فلو ادعى العلم حلف على البتات كمودع ادعى قبض صاحبها، وكذا الوكيل بالبيع إذا ادعى قبض الموكل الثمن فإنه يحلف على البتات؛ لادعائه العلم بذلك ثم في كل موضع وجب فيه اليمين على البتات فحلف على العلم لا يكون معتبراً حتى لا يقضى عليه بالنكول ولا تسقط اليمين عنه، =
He swears on the cause: [by consensus, that he swears: By Allah, I did not buy and I did not divorce (T, Ma'dan)] that the buyer says: 'By Allah, I did not buy a house in the adjacency of the claimant', and the husband says: 'By Allah, she is not a waiting woman'. If it is said: by swearing on the cause, the defendant is harmed because it is possible that he bought and then waived the pre-emption or remained silent about the demand, and his harm is not greater than the harm to the claimant, the answer is that he is more entitled to that because the judge has no alternative but to cause harm to one of them, and the claimant claims what is based on the original state, because when purchase is proven, the right is established for him, and its lapse only occurs through incidental causes, so one must adhere to the original until evidence of the incidental is established. (Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah). And on knowledge: that is, if a man inherits a slave and someone else claims the slave as his own, and the claimant has no evidence, the heir swears on knowledge: 'By Allah, I have no knowledge that this slave belongs to the claimant', and he does not swear on certainty that the slave is not his property, because the heir does not know what the deceased did, so he would refrain from the oath and thus suffer harm, while he is apparently in the right, so one does not resort to that to avoid harm to him. This applies if the inheritance is confirmed by the judge's knowledge, the claimant's admission, or the defendant's evidence. If it is not confirmed that he inherited, then he swears on certainty. And if the author had said: 'If someone claims a specific item or debt against the heir instead of inheriting a slave', it would be better to include claims of debt against the deceased. (Ayni, Fath) And on certainty: that is, if the claimant gives the slave as a gift to the defendant, or the defendant buys the slave from the claimant, then someone else claims it, and the claimant has no evidence, the defendant swears on certainty, not on knowledge. The meaning is that the slave came to the defendant through a voluntary cause, even if it is not purchase or gift. The reason for swearing on certainty is that gift and purchase are causes for ownership by the will and direct action of the owner, so he is allowed to swear on certainty. The principle is: whenever the oath concerns the act of another, the oath is on knowledge; whenever it concerns one's own act, it is on certainty. Do you not see that the oath of the Jews was: 'By Allah, you did not kill him, and you have no knowledge of a killer'? So they were sworn on certainty first, because it was their act, and in the second on knowledge, because it was the act of others. Al-Halwani said: This is a sound principle in all issues except the issue of return due to defect (radd bil-ayb). If the buyer claims that the slave was a runaway, etc., and wants to have the seller sworn, he swears on certainty even though the running away is the act of another. This is because the seller guaranteed delivery of the sold item free from defects, so the oath relates to what he himself guaranteed, so he swears on certainty (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/194). This is when the denier says: 'I have no knowledge'. If he claims knowledge, then he swears on certainty, like a depositary who claims that the owner received the deposit, and likewise an agent for sale who claims that the principal received the price, then he swears on certainty because he claims knowledge of that. Then, in every place where the oath on certainty is obligatory, if he swears on knowledge, it is not valid, so he is not judged as a defaulter (nukul) and the oath is not dropped from him.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the distinction between swearing on knowledge (al-'ilm) and swearing on certainty (al-battat) in judicial oaths. The general principle is that if the oath concerns one's own act (e.g., buying, giving a gift), the defendant swears on certainty (e.g., 'I did not buy'). If the oath concerns the act of another (e.g., what the deceased did), the defendant swears on knowledge (e.g., 'I have no knowledge that this is yours') because he cannot be certain of another's actions. The text provides examples: an heir being sued for a slave claimed by a third party; the heir swears on knowledge because he does not know the deceased's dealings. But if the slave came to the defendant through his own voluntary act (purchase, gift), he swears on certainty. The principle is derived from the Quranic story of the Jews who were first sworn on certainty (their own act of killing) and then on knowledge (knowing a killer). The text then notes an exception: the case of return due to defect (al-radd bil-'ayb). If a buyer claims the slave had a hidden defect (like being a runaway), the seller is sworn on certainty, even though the defect is an act of the slave (another), because the seller guaranteed the item free from defects, so the oath relates to his own guarantee. Finally, if the defendant claims knowledge (i.e., 'I know it is not true'), then he must swear on certainty, as in the case of a depositary claiming the owner already took the deposit, or an agent claiming the principal received the price.

Key Terms
اليمين على العلم (al-yamin ala al-ilm): An oath swearing that one has no knowledge of the claim (e.g., 'I have no knowledge that this belongs to you').
اليمين على البتات (al-yamin ala al-battat): An oath of certainty swearing absolutely that the claim is false (e.g., 'I did not buy').
الرد بالعيب (al-radd bil-'ayb): The option to return a defective item purchased; in this context, the buyer claims a defect (like being a runaway).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle, in which of the following cases should the defendant swear on knowledge (al-ilm) rather than certainty (al-battat)?
A) A buyer sues a seller for a defect, and the seller denies knowledge of the defect.
B) An heir is sued for a slave claimed by another, and the heir does not know the deceased's dealings.
C) A depositary is sued for return of a deposit, and he claims the owner already took it.
D) A buyer sues a seller for a runaway slave, and the seller denies the defect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: oaths on one's own act are on certainty; oaths on another's act are on knowledge, with the exception of defect claims where the seller swears on certainty due to his guarantee.

Types of Oaths in Court: Sabab, Ilm, and Batat
Introduces three oath types with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of oaths (sabab, ilm, batat) and apply the Hanafi principle that an oath on another's act is on knowledge and on one's own act is on certainty.

Core Idea
Oath on one's own act is on certainty; on another's act, on knowledge.

In Hanafi court procedure, when the plaintiff lacks evidence, the defendant must take an oath. But what form does that oath take? This lesson unpacks the three types—sabab (cause), ilm (knowledge), and batat (certainty)—and establishes the core principle: if the act being sworn about is the defendant's own, he swears on certainty; if it is another's, he swears on knowledge. The rule is derived from the Quranic story of the Jews in Surah Al-Baqarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three oath types
List the three types: sabab (cause), ilm (knowledge), batat (certainty). Note the context: sabab is used when the claim arises from a specific cause like purchase or divorce; ilm is used when the act is by another (e.g., testator); batat is used when the act is the defendant's own.
2
Analyze the sabab oath
Read the passage on sabab. Note the example: seller swears he did not buy a house near plaintiff. Understand the objection that swearing on cause may harm the defendant if he waived rights, but the judge must balance harm and prioritizes the original cause.
Refer to the Ayni, Fath, Inaya commentary.
3
Examine the ilm oath
In the inheritance case, the heir swears on knowledge because he does not know the deceased's actions. Understand why swearing on certainty would harm the heir, who is apparently in the right.
4
Examine the batat oath
When the defendant acquired property voluntarily (purchase/gift), he swears on certainty because he directly caused the ownership. Contrast with the ilm case.
5
Apply the general principle
State the principle: oath on another's act = ilm; oath on one's own act = batat. Verify with the hadith of the Jews. Note the exception for defect claims (radd bi al-ayb) where the seller swears on certainty even though the defect is another's act.
The exception shows the principle is not absolute; it is overridden by the seller's guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى ١٦ كيفية اليمين والاستحلاف يحلف على السبب، وعلى العلم لو ورث عبدا فادعاه آخر، وعلى البتات - وكذا لا يحلف على الحاصل في صورة النفقة بأن يقول : بالله ما لها علي حق النفقة من الوجه الذي تدعي؛ لأنه يحلف ولا يحنث في اعتقاده؛ لأنه لا يرى وجوب النفقة للمبتوتة فيفوت مراعاة النظر للمدعية. يحلف على السبب: اتفاقاً بأن يحلف بالله ما اشتريت وما طلقت (ط، معدن) بأن يقول المشتري: بالله ما اشتريت داراً في جوار المدعي، ويقول الزوج بالله ما هي معتدة، فإن قيل: بالحلف على السبب يتضرر المدعى عليه؛ لجواز أن يكون قد اشترى وسلم الشفعة أو سكت عن الطلب، وليس الضرر به بأولى بالضرر من المدعي، أجيب بأنه أولى بذلك؛ لأن القاضي لا يجد بدأ من إلحاق الضرر بأحدهما والمدعي يدعي ما أصل؛ لأن هو الشراء إذا ثبت يثبت الحق له، وسقوطه إنما يكون بأسباب عارضة فيجب التمسك بالأصل حتى يقوم الدليل على العارض. (عيني، فتح، عناية) حلف وعلى العلم: أي لو ورث رجل عبداً فادعاه شخص آخر بأن العبد له ولا بينة للمدعي، يحلف الرجل الوارث على العلم بأن يقول: بالله ما لي علم بأن هذا العبد للمدعي، ولا يحلف على القطع بأن العبد ليس ملكا له؛ لأن الوارث لا يعلم بما فعل المورث فيمتنع عن اليمين فيلحقه بذلك ضرر، وهو محق ظاهراً فلا يصار إليه دفعاً للضرر عنه، وهذا إذا تحقق الإرث بعلم القاضي وإقرار المدعي أو بينة المدعى عليه، فإن لم يتحقق كونه ميرا مراثاً على البتات. ولو قال المؤلف: "لو ادعى على الوارث عيناً أو ديناً مكان لو ورث عبداً"، لكان أولى؛ ليشمل دعوى الدين على الميت عيني (فتح فادعاه بأن العبد له ولا بينة للمدعي وعلى البتات: أي لو وهب العبد المدعي للمدعى عليه، أو اشترى المدعى عليه العبد المدعي ثم ادعاه آخر، ولا بينة للمدعي، يحلف المدعى عليه على القطع لا على العلم والمراد وصول العبد إلى المدعى عليه بسبب اختياري ولو كان غير الشراء والهبة. ووجه الحلف على القطع أن الهبة والشراء سبب موضوع للملك باختيار المالك ومباشرته فيطلق له الحلف على القطع. والأصل فيه أن اليمين متى وقعت على فعل الغير فاليمين على العلم ومتى وقعت على فعل نفسه يكون على البتات، ألا ترى أنه عليها حلف اليهود " بالله ما قتلتم، ولا علمتم له قاتلاً فحلفهم على البتات أولاً؛ لأنه فعلهم، وفي الثاني على العلم؛ لأنه فعل غيرهم قال الحلواني: هذا أصل مستقيم في المسائل كلها إلا في مسألة الرد بالعيب، فإنه إذا ادعى المشتري أن العبد آبق ونحو ذلك - وأراد تحليف البائع - فإنه يحلف على البتات مع أن الإباق فعل غيره وإنما كان كذلك؛ لأن البائع ضمن تسليم المبيع سالماً عن العيوب، فالتحليف يرجع إلى ما ضمن بنفسه، فيحلف على البتات رمز الحقائق: ١٩٤/٢] وهذا إذا قال المنكر : لا علم لي فلو ادعى العلم حلف على البتات كمودع ادعى قبض صاحبها، وكذا الوكيل بالبيع إذا ادعى قبض الموكل الثمن فإنه يحلف على البتات؛ لادعائه العلم بذلك ثم في كل موضع وجب فيه اليمين على البتات فحلف على العلم لا يكون معتبراً حتى لا يقضى عليه بالنكول ولا تسقط اليمين عنه، وكل موضع وجب فيه اليمين على العلم فحلف على البتات يعتبر حتى تسقط اليمين عنه ويقضى عليه إذا نكل؛ لأن الحلف على البتات تأكد فيعتبر، بخلاف العكس. (عيني، فتح، زيلعي)
Book of Claims. Chapter on the manner of oath and administering oath. He swears on the cause, and on knowledge if he inherits a slave and another claims it, and on certainty. Likewise, he does not swear on the actual obligation in the case of maintenance by saying: 'By Allah, she has no right to maintenance from the aspect she claims' because he would swear and not be guilty of perjury in his belief since he does not see maintenance obligatory for the irrevocably divorced woman, thus the consideration for the plaintiff is lost. He swears on the cause: unanimously, that he swears by Allah, 'I did not buy' and 'I did not divorce' as the buyer says: 'By Allah, I did not buy a house adjacent to the plaintiff,' and the husband says: 'By Allah, she is not in her waiting period.' If it is said: By swearing on the cause, the defendant is harmed because he may have bought and waived the pre-emption right or remained silent about the demand, and the harm to him is not more deserving than the harm to the plaintiff, the answer is that he is more deserving because the judge cannot avoid causing harm to one of them, and the plaintiff claims what is original; because when purchase is proven, the right is established for him, and its falling is only due to incidental causes, so the original must be adhered to until evidence for the incidental arises. (Ayni, Fath, Inaya) He swears on knowledge: i.e., if a man inherits a slave and another person claims that the slave is his and the plaintiff has no evidence, the heir swears on knowledge by saying: 'By Allah, I have no knowledge that this slave belongs to the plaintiff,' and he does not swear on certainty that the slave is not his property because the heir does not know what the deceased did, so he would refrain from the oath and suffer harm, whereas he is apparently in the right, so that is not resorted to in order to prevent harm from him. This is when the inheritance is established by the judge's knowledge, the plaintiff's admission, or the defendant's evidence; if the inheritance is not established, he swears on certainty. And if the author had said: 'If one claims against the heir a specific thing or a debt instead of 'if he inherits a slave,' it would be better to include a claim of debt against the deceased. (Ayni, Fath) He claimed it, and the slave is his, and the plaintiff has no evidence. And on certainty: i.e., if the plaintiff gave the slave as a gift to the defendant, or the defendant bought the claimant's slave, then another claims it, and the plaintiff has no evidence, the defendant swears on certainty, not on knowledge, meaning the slave came to the defendant through a voluntary cause, even if it is not purchase or gift. The reason for swearing on certainty is that gift and purchase are causes for ownership by the owner's choice and direct action, so he is allowed to swear on certainty. The principle is that whenever the oath pertains to another's act, it is on knowledge; whenever it pertains to one's own act, it is on certainty. Do you not see that the Jews were made to swear: 'By Allah, you did not kill, nor do you know his killer'? So they were made to swear on certainty first because it was their act, and on knowledge second because it was another's act. Al-Halwani said: This is a sound principle in all cases except in the case of rejection due to defect. For if the buyer claims that the slave is a runaway etc. and wants to make the seller swear, he swears on certainty even though the running away is another's act. This is because the seller is responsible for delivering the sold item free from defects, so the swearing refers to what he himself guaranteed, so he swears on certainty. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 194/2) This is when the defendant says: 'I have no knowledge.' If he claims knowledge, he swears on certainty, like a depositary who claims that the owner has taken possession, and likewise an agent for sale who claims that the principal has received the price, he swears on certainty because he claims knowledge of that. Then, wherever the oath is required on certainty and he swears on knowledge, it is not considered, so he is not judged by default and the oath does not fall from him; and wherever the oath is required on knowledge and he swears on certainty, it is considered, so the oath falls from him and he is judged if he defaults, because swearing on certainty is stronger, so it is considered, unlike the opposite. (Ayni, Fath, Zaylai)
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanded by the commentators, explains the manner of oath (yamin) in court. It distinguishes three types: oath on the cause (sabab), oath on knowledge (ilm), and oath on certainty (batat). The author first states that a defendant swears on the cause when the claim is based on a specific event like purchase or divorce. For example, a buyer swears he did not buy a house near the plaintiff, or a husband swears his wife is not in her waiting period. A potential objection is raised: swearing on the cause might harm the defendant if he actually bought but waived his right to pre-emption, but the answer is that the judge must harm one party, and the plaintiff's claim is based on the original cause, so priority is given to the original until evidence of a waiver appears. Next, the passage discusses oath on knowledge in inheritance cases: if a person inherits a slave and another claims it, the heir swears he has no knowledge that the slave belongs to the claimant, not that the slave is not his property, because the heir does not know the deceased's transactions. This protects the heir from harm. If the inheritance is not proven, he swears on certainty. The commentators suggest the author could have included debts as well for comprehensiveness. Then, oath on certainty applies when the defendant acquired the property through a voluntary act like purchase or gift, because he directly caused the ownership. The general principle is: oath on another's act is on knowledge, oath on one's own act is on certainty. Evidence for this is the Quranic story of the Jews who were made to swear on certainty for their own act (killing) and on knowledge for others. Al-Halwani notes an exception: in the case of rejection due to defect (radd bi al-'ayb), the seller swears on certainty even though the defect (e.g., the slave running away) is another's act, because the seller guaranteed the item free of defects. The passage concludes with details: if the defendant says he has no knowledge, he swears accordingly; but if he claims knowledge, he swears on certainty (e.g., a depositary or agent claiming knowledge of receipt). The commentary ends with a rule: if oath on certainty is required and he swears on knowledge, it is not valid; but if oath on knowledge is required and he swears on certainty, it is valid because certainty is stronger.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn in court, typically by the defendant when the plaintiff lacks evidence.
سبب (sabab): The cause of the claim; swearing on the cause means swearing about the occurrence of the cause, not the resulting right.
علم (ilm): Knowledge; oath on knowledge means swearing that one has no knowledge of the plaintiff's right, used when the act is another's.
بتات (batat): Certainty; oath on certainty means swearing categorically that the claim is false, used when the act is one's own.
رد بالعيب (radd bi al-ayb): Rejection due to defect; a claim by the buyer that the purchased item has a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the inheritance case (heir inherits a slave, another claims it), what is the correct form of oath?
A) Oath on certainty that the slave is not the claimant's
B) Oath on knowledge that he has no knowledge of the claim
C) Oath on the cause of inheritance
D) Oath on certainty that the deceased did not own the slave

Key Takeaway
The form of an oath in court depends on whether the act being sworn about is the defendant's own (batat) or another's (ilm).

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Validity and Default Rules for Oath Forms
Discusses the validity of oath forms when swapped and the consequences of default.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that swearing on a stronger form (batat) when a weaker form (ilm) is required validates the oath, but the opposite does not, and determine the consequence of default (nukul).

Core Idea
Swearing on batat when ilm is required is valid; the reverse is invalid.

Once the correct form of oath is determined, what happens if the defendant swears in a different form? The Hanafi school has a clear rule: if batat (certainty) is required and the defendant swears on ilm (knowledge), the oath is invalid and he may be judged by default. But if ilm is required and he swears on batat, it is accepted because batat is stronger. This lesson also covers cases where the defendant claims to have knowledge, as well as examples from depositary and agent contracts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule
Read the final part of the passage: wherever batat is required, swearing on ilm is not considered; wherever ilm is required, swearing on batat is valid. This is because batat is akhadd (stronger).
2
Examine the scenario of defendant claiming knowledge
If the defendant says 'I have no knowledge' he swears on ilm; but if he claims knowledge (e.g., a depositary claims the owner took possession), he swears on batat because he is asserting certainty.
Examples: depositary (wadi) and agent (wakil) claiming receipt.
3
Apply the rule to nukul (default)
If the defendant refuses to swear, or swears incorrectly, the result is a judgment against him. The passage states: if batat required and he swears ilm, he is not considered to have performed the oath and may be judged; if ilm required and he swears batat, the oath is considered fulfilled and nukul does not apply.
4
Contrast with the earlier principles
Note that this rule is about the form of the oath, not the content. It complements the distinction between ilm and batat from the previous lesson.

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanded by the commentators, explains the manner of oath (yamin) in court. It distinguishes three types: oath on the cause (sabab), oath on knowledge (ilm), and oath on certainty (batat). The author first states that a defendant swears on the cause when the claim is based on a specific event like purchase or divorce. For example, a buyer swears he did not buy a house near the plaintiff, or a husband swears his wife is not in her waiting period. A potential objection is raised: swearing on the cause might harm the defendant if he actually bought but waived his right to pre-emption, but the answer is that the judge must harm one party, and the plaintiff's claim is based on the original cause, so priority is given to the original until evidence of a waiver appears. Next, the passage discusses oath on knowledge in inheritance cases: if a person inherits a slave and another claims it, the heir swears he has no knowledge that the slave belongs to the claimant, not that the slave is not his property, because the heir does not know the deceased's transactions. This protects the heir from harm. If the inheritance is not proven, he swears on certainty. The commentators suggest the author could have included debts as well for comprehensiveness. Then, oath on certainty applies when the defendant acquired the property through a voluntary act like purchase or gift, because he directly caused the ownership. The general principle is: oath on another's act is on knowledge, oath on one's own act is on certainty. Evidence for this is the Quranic story of the Jews who were made to swear on certainty for their own act (killing) and on knowledge for others. Al-Halwani notes an exception: in the case of rejection due to defect (radd bi al-'ayb), the seller swears on certainty even though the defect (e.g., the slave running away) is another's act, because the seller guaranteed the item free of defects. The passage concludes with details: if the defendant says he has no knowledge, he swears accordingly; but if he claims knowledge, he swears on certainty (e.g., a depositary or agent claiming knowledge of receipt). The commentary ends with a rule: if oath on certainty is required and he swears on knowledge, it is not valid; but if oath on knowledge is required and he swears on certainty, it is valid because certainty is stronger.

Key Terms
نكول (nukul): Default; when the defendant refuses to swear the oath, leading to a judgment against him.
مؤكد (mu'akkad): Emphatic or strengthened; used here to describe the nature of the batat oath as stronger than ilm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A defendant is required to swear on knowledge (ilm) but he swears on certainty (batat). What is the ruling?
A) The oath is invalid and he must be judged by default
B) The oath is valid and he is relieved from further swearing
C) He must repeat the oath correctly or be judged
D) The plaintiff may choose to administer a different oath

Key Takeaway
If a defendant swears on a stronger form (batat) when a weaker form (ilm) is required, the oath is valid; but the opposite invalidates it and may lead to default.

Fidya and Sulh: Ransoming and Settling Oaths
Ruling on fidya and sulh with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fidya and sulh for oaths, distinguish valid ransoming from invalid purchase, and explain the legal effect on the right to the oath.

Core Idea
Ransoming an oath is valid; buying it is not—an oath is not property.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario: a defendant in a claim may wish to avoid swearing an oath. Is it permissible to pay the plaintiff to drop the oath? The Hanafi school permits this as a valid settlement (sulh) or ransom (fidya), provided it does not involve a sale of the oath itself. We will explore the textual evidence and the precise legal distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The matn states that if the defendant ransoms (iftada) his oath or settles (salaha) with the plaintiff, it is valid, and he does not swear afterwards. Ransoming means the defendant pays something to avoid the oath; settlement involves the plaintiff reducing the claim in exchange for dropping the oath.
Both are valid and drop the right to the oath.
2
Locate the textual evidence
The commentator cites the practice of Uthman ibn Affan (RA) and the hadith 'Protect your honors with your wealth' (dhubbi 'an a'radikum bi-amwalikum). Uthman ransomed his oath when sued, and the hadith supports using wealth to protect honor.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in practice.
3
Analyze the rationale
The defendant benefits by avoiding litigation and the accusation of lying, and the plaintiff receives compensation. It is a legitimate compromise.
The plaintiff takes the payment as his right or compensation.
4
Distinguish from purchase of the oath
The text explicitly says that purchasing the oath (shira' al-yamin) is invalid because an oath is not property; sale requires property. Thus the right to the oath remains with the plaintiff.
The invalidity is due to the absence of the pillar of sale (mal).
5
Determine the effect on the right to the oath
After valid fidya or sulh, the right to the oath is permanently dropped because the plaintiff took a substitute. However, if the plaintiff unilaterally drops the right without consideration, it is ineffective because the right belongs to the judge (al-tahlif li'l-hakim).
This nuance prevents arbitrary waivers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو وهب له أو اشتراه، ولو افتدى المنكر بيمينه أو صالحه منها على شيء، للمنكر أي أو ملكه بشراء صح، ولم يحلف بعده. الافتداء والصلح صح: أي إذا دفع المدعى عليه إلى المدعي شيئاً فداء عن يمينه كي لا يحلف وقبل الآخر، أو صالح المنكر المدعي بأن قال المدعي: صالحت عن دعوى الحلف على كذا وقبل الآخر، الافتداء وصح الصلح، أي سقط حق صح الحلف؛ لما روي أن عثمان الله ادعي عليه أربعون درهماً، فأعطى شيئاً وافتدى يمينه، و لم يحلف، فقيل له: ألا تحلف وأنت صادق؟ فقال: أخاف أن يوافق قدره بيميني، فيقال: هذا بسبب يمينه الكاذبة، ولأن المنكر يدفع به الخصومة وتهمة الكذب عن نفسه، ويصون عرضه وهو حسن، قال عليه: "ذبوا عن أعراضكم بأموالكم" والمدعي يأخذه على أنه حقه أو عوض عنه فيجوز، وإن لم يكن الحق مالاً كالقصاص فإذا صح الفداء والصلح بطل حقه في اليمين رمز الحقائق: ١٩٤/٢] ولم يحلف بعده أي بعد الفداء والصلح أبداً؛ لأنه أسقط حقه في اليمين بأخذ البدل، والافتداء قد يكون بمال مثل المدعى، وقد يكون بأقل منه، وأما الصلح عنه فإنما يكون على مال أقل هو من المدعي غالباً؛ لأن الصلح ينبئ عن الحطيطة، والافتداء والصلح كلاهما مشروع، أما إذا اشترى يمينه بمال لا يجوز؛ لأن الشراء عقد تمليك المال بالمال واليمين ليست بمال فانعدم ركن البيع، فبطل وبقي حقه في اليمين على حاله، وظاهره: أنه عليه بما دفعه إليه. واعلم أنه كما لا يبطل حقه في اليمين بالشراء فكذا لو أسقط اليمين قصداً بأن قال: برئت فلا وله التحليف، بخلاف البراءة من المال؛ لأن التحليف للحاكم، يصح، من الحلف، أو تركته عليه، أو وهبته، فلا يصح براءته من غيره. (عيني، فتح) يرجع
If he gives it to him as a gift or buys it, and if the defendant ransoms his oath or settles with him for something, for the defendant, i.e., he owns it by purchase, it is valid, and he does not swear afterwards. Al-Iftida' (ransom) and al-Sulh (settlement). It is valid: i.e., if the defendant pays something to the plaintiff as a ransom for his oath so that he does not swear, and the other accepts, or the defendant settles with the plaintiff by the plaintiff saying: I have settled regarding the claim of the oath for such-and-such, and the other accepts, the ransom and the settlement are valid, i.e., the right to the oath falls. This is because it is narrated that Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) was sued for forty dirhams, so he gave something and ransomed his oath, and did not swear. It was said to him: Why don't you swear while you are truthful? He said: I fear that the decree may coincide with my oath, and it will be said: This is due to his false oath. Also because the defendant thereby repels the litigation and the accusation of lying from himself, and protects his honor, and that is good. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Protect your honors with your wealth.' And the plaintiff takes it as his right or as compensation for it, so it is permissible. Even if the right is not property, such as retaliation, then if the ransom and settlement are valid, his right to the oath becomes void. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 194/2) And he does not swear afterwards, i.e., after the ransom and settlement, ever, because he has forfeited his right to the oath by taking the substitute. The ransom may be with property equal to the claim or less, while the settlement is usually for less property from the plaintiff, because settlement implies reduction. Both ransom and settlement are legislated. However, if he purchases his oath with property, it is not valid because purchase is a contract of transferring property for property, and the oath is not property, so the pillar of sale is absent; thus it becomes void and his right to the oath remains as it was. And apparently, he is liable for what he paid to him. Know that just as his right to the oath is not nullified by purchase, similarly if he intentionally drops the oath by saying: I am free from it, then he still has the right to administer the oath, unlike dropping from property, because administering the oath belongs to the judge. It is valid from the oath, or he leaves it upon him, or gives it as a gift, so his dropping from other than that is not valid. (Ayni, Fath) He returns.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage continues from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, now addressing the situation where the defendant may avoid swearing by paying the plaintiff (ifitida') or by settling (sulh). The matn states that if the defendant ransoms his oath or settles for something, it is valid and he does not swear afterwards. The commentator explains: the defendant pays something to the plaintiff as a ransom to avoid the oath, and this is valid as per the practice of Uthman (RA) who was sued for forty dirhams and paid to avoid swearing, fearing that his truthful oath might coincide with divine decree and be blamed. Also, the hadith 'Protect your honors with your wealth' supports this. The ransom or settlement can be for the same amount as the claim or less, but settlement typically involves a reduction (hatita). The text distinguishes this from purchasing the oath, which is invalid because an oath is not property and cannot be sold; thus the right to administer the oath remains. Also, if the plaintiff intentionally drops the right to the oath, it is not effective because the oath is a right of the judge (or the court), not purely a private right. The passage concludes with a note that the defendant's right to administer the oath is not lost by purchase or by an invalid dropping, but it is lost by valid ransom or settlement.

Key Terms
افتداء (iftida'): Ransom or redemption of an oath by the defendant paying the plaintiff to avoid swearing.
صلح (sulh): Settlement between plaintiff and defendant regarding the oath, often involving a reduced payment from the plaintiff, leading to the dropping of the oath.
يمين (yamin): The oath that the defendant would be required to take; the right to administer the oath is considered a legal right that can be forfeited through ransom or settlement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is purchasing an oath with money invalid, while ransoming it is valid?
A) Because the oath is not property and cannot be sold
B) Because the judge does not allow it
C) Because the plaintiff must always get his full right
D) Because the defendant cannot pay less than the claim

Key Takeaway
Ransoming (fidya) and settling (sulh) an oath are valid in Hanafi law, but purchasing the oath is invalid because an oath is not property.

Mutual Oaths: Disputes with Evidence on Both Sides
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence in sale disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when both parties have evidence in a price/quantity dispute and distinguish the later view of Abu Hanifa from Zufar's view.

Core Idea
When both have evidence, the increase in each claim prevails; no averaging.

In sale disputes, if both buyer and seller produce evidence for their conflicting claims, the judge cannot simply reject both or average them. The Hanafi school has a detailed rule: the party who proves a higher price or larger quantity wins on that dimension. This lesson unpacks that rule, especially when both price and subject matter are contested, and highlights the shift from an earlier averaging approach to the final relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The dispute involves both the price and the sold item. For example, seller claims: 'I sold you one slave-girl for 100 dinars.' Buyer claims: 'You sold me two slave-girls for 50 dinars.' Both have evidence.
2
State the later Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa's later view (the mufta bihi), the seller's evidence on price prevails (100 dinars), and the buyer's evidence on the number of items prevails (two slave-girls). Result: buyer gets both slave-girls for 100 dinars.
Each claim is upheld in the aspect where it proves more.
3
State the opposing view
Zufar and early Abu Hanifa held that the two sets of evidence should be combined, leading to an average: 125 dinars for two slave-girls (average of 100 and 50 for price, and no change in quantity).
This averages both price and quantity claims.
4
Analyze the reasoning
The later view argues that evidence is for proof; the one who proves more does not contradict the other's proof because each proves a different aspect. Evidence cannot conflict unless it addresses the same fact.
Proof of increase is stronger than proof of decrease.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the later view, buyer pays 100 dinars for two slave-girls. Under the earlier view, buyer pays 125 dinars. This difference affects the final distribution, and the judge must apply the later view as the relied-upon position.
Tarjih: the later view is preferred.

Source Text

أي قضى القاضي وحكم
