(عيني)] مطلقاً سواء كان بائعاً أو مشترياً؛ لأن البينات للإثبات ومثبت الأقل لا يعارض مثبت الأكثر ، ولو كان الاختلاف في الثمن والمبيع جميعاً بأن ادعى البائع أكثر مما يدعيه المشتري من الثمن، وادعى المشتري أكثر مما يقر البائع من المبيع في حالة واحدة بأن قال البائع: بعتك هذه الجارية بمائة دينار. وقال المشتري: بعتنيها وهذه معها بخمسين ديناراً، وأقاما بينة، فبينة البائع أولى في الثمن، وبيئة المشتري أولى في المبيع نظراً إلى زيادة الإثبات، ففي الصورة المذكورة تكون الجاريتان جميعاً للمشتري بمائة دينار؛ لأن حجة البائع في الثمن أكثر إثباتاً، المشتري في المبيع أكثر إثباتاً، وقيل: هذا قول أبي حنيفة آخراً، وكان يقول أولاً وهو قول زفر: يقضى بهما للمشتري بمائة وخمسة وعشرين ديناراً.(فتح، عناية) ولم يرضيا إلخ لو قال: "ولم يرض واحد منهما بدعوى صاحبه بدل قوله: "و" لم يرضيا" لكان أولى؛ لأن شرط التحالف عدم رضا واحد لا عدم رضا كل منهما أي إن لم يكن لكل واحد منهما بينة، وعجزا عن إقامتها، قيل للمشتري: إما أن ترضى بثمن ما ادعاه البائع، وإلا فسخنا البيع، وقيل للبائع إما أن تسلم ما ادعاه المشتري من المبيع، وإلا فسخنا البيع، ويجب ، أن لا يعجل القاضي بالفسخ حتى يسأل كل واحد منهما عما يختاره؛ لأن المقصود قطع المنازعة، وهذا السؤال جهة في قطعها؛ لأنه ربما لا يرضيان بالفسخ، فإذا علما به يتراضيان.
Rulings of Mutual Oath. Chapter of Mutual Oath. i.e., in explaining its rulings. If they differ regarding the amount of the price or the sold item, judgment is for the one who provides proof; if both provide proof, then for the one who establishes the increase. i.e., the judge passes judgment and rules. And if they are unable and do not accept the claim of either. The two contractors are unable to produce evidence. i.e., with what his companion said. Mutual oath (tah aluf): it is tafa'ul from al-halif, meaning participation of two or more. And he delayed the oath of two from the oath of one to suit the natural situation. (Ayni, Inayah. They differ etc. by the seller saying it is a thousand, and the buyer saying it is five hundred, or in its description and type. (T., Ayni). It lacks completeness; for there is no difference in the dispute whether in amount or description, like the seller claiming the sale is in current dirhams and the buyer in obsolete dirhams, or in its type like the seller claiming it is by dinars and the buyer by dirhams. So if the author had omitted 'amount' and restricted it to his saying 'they differ in the price' to include difference in amount, description, or type, it would have been better. Or the sold item: i.e., or they differ in the amount of the sold item, like the seller saying it is ten pounds and the buyer saying fifteen pounds. (Ayni) Judgment is for the one who provides proof because he illuminated his claim with evidence, while the other side remains with mere claim, and evidence is stronger because it obligates the judge to rule, unlike the claim. In al-Bahr from al-Misbah: regarding 'burhan' (proof) and the increase of the nun and its originality, there are two views. (Ayni, Fath) And if both provide proof: i.e., each of them produces evidence for what he claims. (Ayni) And proof: So for the one who establishes the increase: i.e., the judge rules for the one who establishes the increase because it is free from opposition. (Ayni) Absolutely, whether seller or buyer; because evidence is for proof, and the one who proves less does not oppose the one who proves more. And if the dispute is over both the price and the sold item together, like the seller claiming more than the buyer claims regarding the price, and the buyer claiming more than the seller admits regarding the sold item in one instance, such as the seller saying: 'I sold you this slave-girl for one hundred dinars,' and the buyer saying: 'You sold her to me and this one with her for fifty dinars,' and they both produce evidence. Then the seller's evidence prevails in the price, and the buyer's evidence prevails in the sold item, considering the increase in proof. So in the mentioned case, both slave-girls are for the buyer for one hundred dinars; because the seller's proof is more in the price, the buyer's proof is more in the sold item. And it is said: this is the later view of Abu Hanifa, and earlier he used to say—and it is Zufar's view—that judgment is for the two slave-girls for the buyer for one hundred and twenty-five dinars. (Fath, Inayah) And if they do not accept etc. If he had said: 'And if not one of them accepts the claim of his companion' instead of his saying: 'and if they do not accept,' it would have been better; because the condition for mutual oath is the non-acceptance of one, not the non-acceptance of each. That is, if neither of them has evidence and they are unable to produce it. It is said to the buyer: either accept the price claimed by the seller, or we rescind the sale, and it is said to the seller: either hand over the sold item claimed by the buyer, or we rescind the sale. And it is necessary that the judge not hasten to rescind until he asks each one about his choice; because the goal is to cut off the dispute, and this question is a means to cut it off, because perhaps they will not be satisfied with rescission, but when they know about it, they may come to mutual agreement.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on mutual oaths (al-tah aluf) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with disputes between buyer and seller over the price or the object of sale. The initial ruling: if they differ on the amount of price or sold item, and one has evidence (bayyina), judgment goes to that party. If both have evidence, the one who proves the increase (of price or quantity) prevails. The commentary notes that the increase can be in description or type as well. The passage then details a scenario where both the price and the sold item are disputed: seller claims one slave-girl for 100 dinars, buyer claims two slave-girls for 50 dinars. Here, the seller's evidence on price (100 dinars) prevails, and the buyer's evidence on the number of slave-girls (two) prevails. The result is both slave-girls for the buyer for 100 dinars. The text mentions that this is the later view of Abu Hanifa; earlier he held with Zufar that the buyer gets both for 125 dinars (the average of the two claims). The passage also explains the procedure when neither party has evidence: the judge offers them to either accept the other's claim or rescind the sale. The judge should not rush to rescind but ask each party for their choice, hoping they reach agreement.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-tah aluf): Mutual oaths taken by both parties when they disagree and have no evidence.
البينة (al-bayyina): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.
البرهان (al-burhan): Proof; in this context, synonymous with bayyina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The average of both claims is taken.
D) The judge decides based on his own opinion.

Key Takeaway
When both parties have evidence, the judge upholds the increase in each claim separately, not an average.

Defendant Claims Item Belongs to Absent Third Party: Conditions for Repelling the Lawsuit
Rulings on defendant claiming item is deposit, lease, loan, or pledge from an absent person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a defendant can repel a lawsuit by claiming the item is a deposit, lease, loan, pledge, or usurpation from an absent person, and identify the type of evidence required.

Core Idea
A lawsuit is repelled if the defendant proves a specific transaction with an absent person and the item exists; mere ownership claim is insufficient.

In litigation, a defendant may claim that the disputed item is not his but belongs to an absent third party through a specific legal relationship (deposit, lease, etc.). This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the conditions for this defense to be accepted. You will learn what constitutes valid evidence and why the item must be existing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defendant's claims
The defendant must specifically state that the item is held as a deposit, lease, loan, pledge, or even a usurpation from a named absent person. Vague claims are insufficient.
The text lists: 'awda'anīhi' (deposit), 'ājarānīhi' (lease), 'rahanahu' (pledge), 'ghaṣabtuhu' (usurpation).
2
Verify the item's existence
The item must be existing (qā'im, not hālik). If it is lost, the claim becomes a debt against the defendant, which cannot be repelled by this mechanism.
A lost item transforms the lawsuit into a debt claim, requiring different procedures.
3
Identify the type of proof required
The defendant must provide burhān (proof), defined as witness testimony, the judge's personal knowledge, or the plaintiff's own acknowledgment. Mere assertion is not enough.
Burhān is broader than just bayyinah; it includes any legally acceptable evidence.
4
Note the effect of valid proof
Once valid proof is established, the lawsuit is repelled absolutely, regardless of the defendant's character (even if known for tricks).
The judge's ruling dismisses the suit because the defendant's hand is proven not to be a hand of dispute.
5
Distinguish between proving the transaction vs. ownership
If the witnesses only testify that the absent party owns the item, without specifying the transaction, the lawsuit is not repelled. The transaction must be proven directly.
Ownership of the absent does not absolve the defendant; the specific legal relationship must be established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان أحكام ما تندفع به الخصومة [رمز الحقائق: ۱۹۹/۲] قال المدعى عليه: أي لو ادعى رجل ملكاً مطلقا في عين في يد المدعى عليه فقال المدعى عليه في جوابه هذا الشيء أودعنيه، وهذا الشيء يشمل المنقول والعقار، وظاهر الإشارة أنه قائم، فلو كان هالكاً لم تندفع الخصومة؛ لأنه يدعي الدين عليه، وإيداع الدين لا يمكن أودعنيه : أي أودعني إياه فلان الغائب. (عيني) أو غصبته أي أو قال: غصبت هذا الشيء من فلان الغائب (عيني) وبرهن عليه [ أي أقام البينة على ما قال، والعين قائمة لا هالكة. (ط عيني) المراد بالبرهان وجود حجة على ما قاله سواء كانت ببينة، أو علم القاضي، أو إقرار المدعي، وقيد به؛ لأنه ما لم يقم البينة لا تندفع الخصومة. (عيني، فتح، عناية) دفعت عنه: أي مطلقاً سواء كان ذو اليد صالحاً أو معروفاً بالحيل، والمعنى حكم القاضي يدفع الخصومة؛ لأنه ببینته أن يده ليست بيد خصومة، فصار كما لو أقر المدعي بذلك، وأثبت ذو اليد إقراره بذلك، والشرط إثبات هذه الأشياء دون الملك للغائب، حتى لو شهدوا بالملك للغائب دون هذه الأشياء، لم تندفع الخصومة - أثبت =
Section: On explaining the rulings of what repels the lawsuit. [Code of Realities: 199/2] The defendant said: meaning if a man claims absolute ownership of a specific asset in the hand of the defendant, then the defendant says in response: 'This thing is deposited with me', and 'this thing' includes movable and immovable property, and the apparent reference is that it exists, for if it were lost, the lawsuit would not be repelled because he claims a debt against him, and depositing a debt is not possible. 'Deposited with me': meaning so-and-so the absent deposited it with me. (Ayni) 'or leased to me': meaning he gave it to me as a lease from so-and-so the absent. (Ayni) 'So-and-so the absent' refers to all due to conjunction. (Ayni) 'or pawned it': meaning the defendant said: the absent pawned this thing with me. (Ayni) 'or I usurped it': meaning he said: I usurped this thing from so-and-so the absent. (Ayni) 'and he provides evidence on it': meaning he establishes proof for what he said, and the asset is existing, not lost. (T. Ayni) By 'evidence' is meant existence of a proof for what he said, whether by witnesses, knowledge of the judge, or acknowledgment of the plaintiff; it is restricted because if he does not establish proof, the lawsuit is not repelled. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) 'It is repelled from him': meaning absolutely, whether the possessor is upright or known for tricks; the meaning is the judge's ruling repels the lawsuit because by his evidence his hand is not a hand of dispute, so it becomes as if the plaintiff acknowledged that and the possessor established that acknowledgment. The condition is establishing these matters without ownership for the absent, for if they testified to ownership of the absent without these matters, the lawsuit is not repelled. - Established =
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how a defendant can repel a lawsuit by claiming that the disputed item is actually a deposit, lease, loan, pledge, or even a usurpation from an absent third party. The key condition is that the defendant must provide evidence (bayyinah, judge's knowledge, or plaintiff's acknowledgment) and the item must be existing. If proven, the lawsuit is dismissed absolutely, even if the defendant is known for tricks. The evidence must show the specific transaction (deposit, lease, etc.) and not just ownership of the absent, otherwise the suit continues. This section is from the chapter on Rules of Litigation.

Key Terms
أودعنيه (awda'anīhi): He deposited it with me (from the absent).
برهان (burhān): Proof or evidence, defined here as witness testimony, judge's knowledge, or plaintiff's acknowledgment.
دفعت عنه (dufi'at 'anhu): The lawsuit is repelled from him (the defendant).

A) Simply stating it is a deposit

C) Swearing an oath
D) The absent person must be declared guilty

Key Takeaway
A defendant's claim that an item belongs to an absent third party only repels the lawsuit if the defendant proves a specific transaction and the item still exists.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Rebutting a Lawsuit by Proving Property from an Absent Third Party
Explains how defendant rebuts a lawsuit by claiming property from absent third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a defendant repels a lawsuit by claiming property from an absent third party, and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding the required witness knowledge of the absent person.

Core Idea
To repel a lawsuit, the defendant must prove the property came from an absent person by deposit, lease, etc., and the quality of witness knowledge determines success.

In Hanafi procedural law, a defendant in possession of property can block a plaintiff's ownership claim by asserting that the property actually belongs to an absent third party who deposited, leased, pawned, loaned, or was usurped by the defendant. However, this defense is not automatic; it requires proof and the asset must be extant. Moreover, the degree of witness knowledge of the absent person is crucial and was disputed among early Hanafi masters. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defendant's claims that repel a lawsuit
The defendant must assert one of five specific relationships: deposit (wadi'a), lease (ijara), loan (ariya), pawn (rahn), or usurpation (ghasb) from an absent person. The asset must be extant, not destroyed.
If destroyed, the claim becomes a debt, which follows different rules.
2
Recognize the requirement of evidence (bayyinah)
The defendant must produce evidence, which can be witness testimony, the judge's knowledge, or the plaintiff's admission. Without evidence, the lawsuit is not repelled.
The evidence must prove the specific relationship, not just the absent person's ownership.
3
Analyze exceptions when the plaintiff claims purchase, theft, or direct usurpation
If the plaintiff claims to have bought the property from the absent person, or accuses the defendant of theft or direct usurpation, the defendant's defense of deposit etc. is ineffective because the defendant's own claim admits ownership or the plaintiff's accusation makes the defendant a direct opponent.
Muhammad disagreed, holding that the defense still works on qiyas, but the relied-upon view (istihsan) is that it does not.
4
Examine the disagreement on witness knowledge of the absent person
The core dispute: Abu Hanifa held that witnesses knowing the absent person by appearance alone suffices to repel the lawsuit. Muhammad required name, lineage, and appearance, citing a hadith. Abu Yusuf later conditioned it on the defendant's upright character.
The hadith states: 'If you do not know his name and lineage, then you do not know him.'
5
Determine the mufta bihi position and its practical consequence
The relied-upon position is Muhammad's: witnesses must know name, lineage, and appearance. If they only know appearance, the lawsuit is not repelled. This protects the plaintiff's ability to pursue the absent person.
This scenario is called 'al-mukhammasa' (the quintuple) due to five types of claims and five juristic views.

(ط) لا [ أي أقام ذو اليد بينة على ما قال (معدن)] أي لا تندفع الخصومة في الصور الثلاث، أما الأولى: فإن المدعى عليه يقول : اشتريت هذا الشيء من الغائب فهو بدعواه الشراء صار معترفاً بأن يده يد ملك، فيكون معترفاً بأنه خصم، وفي الثانية: أي فيما إذا قال المدعي للمدعى عليه غصبته مني صار خصماً باعتبار دعوى الفعل عليه لا بيده، وفي الثالثة: أي فيما إذا قال المدعي: سرق مني يصير خصماً بدعوى الفعل عليه أيضاً في المعنى، وإنما جعله بالبناء للمفعول؛ لأجل الستر عليه كي لا يقطع يده، فصار كأنه قال: سرقته مني، وقال محمد: تندفع الخصومة عنه، وهو القياس؛ لأنه لم يدع عليه الفعل، فصار كما لو قال غصب مني على البناء للمفعول، فإنه تندفع به ووجه الاستحسان ما بيناه (فتح) اتفاقاً،
A chapter on explaining the rulings of what repels a claim. The defendant says: i.e., if a man claims absolute ownership of an asset in the possession of the defendant, and the defendant responds: 'This thing is a deposit with me,' and 'this thing' includes movable and immovable property, and the apparent reference is that it is extant; if it were destroyed, the claim would not be repelled because he is claiming a debt, and depositing a debt is not possible. 'A deposit with me': i.e., so-and-so the absent one deposited it with me. (Ayni) 'Or leased to me': i.e., so-and-so the absent one gave it to me as a lease. (Ayni) 'So-and-so the absent one' refers back to all, by virtue of conjunction. (Ayni) 'Or pawned it': i.e., or the defendant said: the absent one pawned this thing with me. (Ayni) 'Or I usurped it': i.e., or he said: I usurped this thing from so-and-so the absent one. (Ayni) 'And he proves it' [i.e., he produces evidence for what he said, and the asset is extant, not destroyed. (T, Ayni) The meaning of proof is the existence of evidence for what he said, whether it be witnesses, the judge's knowledge, or the plaintiff's admission; and it is conditioned because unless he produces evidence, the claim is not repelled. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) 'It is repelled from him': i.e., absolutely, whether the possessor is upright or known for tricks; and the meaning is that the judge's ruling repels the claim because by his evidence his hand is not a hand of contention; so it becomes as if the plaintiff admitted that, and the possessor proved his admission. The condition is proving these things, not proving ownership for the absent person; if they testify to the absent person's ownership without these things, the claim is not repelled. (Text continues) 'And the claim of the plaintiff, even if he says: I bought it from the absent one, or the plaintiff says: he stole it from me, and the defendant says: I bought it' — for the defendant this thing [i.e., the possessor says: so-and-so deposited it with me, and he proves it, no]; and if the plaintiff says: I bought it from so-and-so, and the one who is the defendant [i.e., the possessor] says: so-and-so deposited this same thing with me. [i.e., the defendant, i.e., this same thing, i.e., the thing itself, i.e., I bought this thing from the absent one] — this [i.e., the opposite] repels. Ibn Shubruma said: it is not repelled even if he produces evidence. Ibn Abi Layla said: it is repelled by his admission for the absent one without producing evidence. Abu Yusuf later said: if the defendant is upright, it is repelled; if he is known for tricks and fabrication, it is not repelled even if he produces evidence. And all this applies when the witnesses say: we know the owner of the property, i.e., the depositor, lender, or lessor, by his name, lineage, and appearance, because the plaintiff can pursue him. If they say: we know him by his appearance but not by any of that, the judge does not accept their testimony, because it is possible that the depositor is the plaintiff, and because he has not referred to a specific person whom the plaintiff can pursue; if the claim were repelled, the plaintiff would be harmed. So the claim is not repelled due to the plaintiff's established right. And the claim is not repelled from the possessor by consensus. If they say: we know him by his appearance but not by his name and lineage, the claim is not repelled according to Muhammad, because knowledge by appearance is not knowledge; due to the narration that the Prophet (peace be upon him) told a man: 'Do you know so-and-so?' He said: yes. He said: 'Do you know his name and lineage?' He said: no. He said: 'Then you do not know him.' According to Abu Hanifa, the claim is repelled from him, because the defendant has proved by his evidence that the asset came to him from someone else, since the witnesses recognized him by appearance, knowning certainly that the depositor is not the plaintiff; so the testimony establishes that his hand is not a hand of contention, which is the objective. The hadith indicates the negation of complete knowledge, and the defendant is not required to fully identify the plaintiff's opponent; he only needs to prove that he is not an opponent, and he has done so. This issue is called the 'quintuple' (mukhammasa) of the book of lawsuits, either because it involves five forms: deposit, loan, lease, pawn, and usurpation, or because it involves differences among five imams. (Fath, Inayah) 'Stole from me' or he said: 'He usurped it from me' or 'He stole it.' (T) 'No' [i.e., the possessor produced evidence for what he said (Ma'din)] i.e., the claim is not repelled in three forms: the first, because the defendant says: I bought this thing from the absent one; by his claim of purchase he has admitted that his hand is a hand of ownership, thus he admits he is an opponent. In the second, i.e., when the plaintiff says to the defendant: you usurped it from me, he becomes an opponent by virtue of the claim of an act against him, not by his hand. In the third, i.e., when the plaintiff says: he stole from me, he becomes an opponent by claim of an act against him also in meaning; and it is put in the passive to conceal it so that his hand is not cut off; thus it is as if he said: I stole it from me. Muhammad said: the claim is repelled from him, which is the analogy, because he did not claim an action against him; so it is as if he said: it was usurped from me in the passive, and that repels. The basis of istihsan is what we have explained. (Fath) 'By agreement.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation (sharh) of the rules for how a defendant can rebut a lawsuit (khushumat) by claiming that the property in his possession actually belongs to an absent third party. The author first explains the scenario: the plaintiff claims absolute ownership of an asset in the defendant's hand. The defendant responds by stating that the asset is a deposit (wadi'a), lease (ijara), loan (ariya), pawn (rahn), or usurpation (ghasb) from an absent person. Importantly, the asset must be extant (qaim) and not destroyed, because if destroyed, the claim becomes a debt, and the rules differ. The defendant must produce evidence (bayyinah) to support his claim; without evidence, the lawsuit is not repelled. The evidence can be witness testimony, the judge's knowledge, or the plaintiff's own admission. The condition is only to prove the specific claim (e.g., deposit) and not the absent person's ownership; testifying to ownership without the specific claim is insufficient. The passage then discusses exceptions: if the plaintiff says he bought the item from the absent person, or the plaintiff accuses the defendant of theft or usurpation, the rules change. In those cases, the defendant's claim of deposit etc. does not repel the lawsuit according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, because by claiming purchase, the defendant admits ownership, and by being accused of theft/usurpation, he becomes a direct opponent. However, Imam Muhammad differs and says the claim is repelled in all cases based on qiyas (analogy), but the preferred view (istihsan) is that it is not repelled. The text then mentions differing opinions from Ibn Shubruma (never repelled even with evidence), Ibn Abi Layla (repelled by mere admission without evidence), and Abu Yusuf's later view (depends on the character of the defendant). The core of the passage addresses the quality of witness knowledge: the witnesses must know the absent person by name, lineage, and appearance (knowing by appearance alone is insufficient according to Muhammad, citing a prophetic hadith). Abu Hanifa disagrees and holds that knowing by appearance is sufficient to repel the lawsuit because the defendant only needs to prove he is not an opponent, not to fully identify the opponent. This issue is called the 'quintuple' (mukhammasa) because it involves five types of claims (deposit, loan, lease, pawn, usurpation) and five imams with differing views. The relied-upon position is that if witnesses know the absent person by name and lineage, the lawsuit is repelled; if only by appearance, it is not repelled according to Muhammad (which is the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school).

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): The lawsuit or legal claim brought by the plaintiff against the defendant regarding a right or property.
البرهان (al-burhan): Proof or evidence, including witness testimony, the judge's knowledge, or the plaintiff's admission.
يد الخصومة (yad al-khusumah): The hand of contention; i.e., possession that subjects the holder to a lawsuit because it is not derived from a third party.
المخمسة (al-mukhammasah): The quintuple issue; a famous problem in Hanafi law involving five types of claims (deposit, loan, lease, pawn, usurpation) and five jurists with differing views.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what must witnesses know about the absent person for the defendant to successfully rebut the lawsuit?
A) Only the absent person's appearance (wajh).
B) The absent person's name, lineage, and appearance.
C) Only the absent person's name.
D) No knowledge of the absent person is required.

Key Takeaway
The defendant can repel a lawsuit by proving the property came from an absent third party through one of five claims, but the witnesses must know the absent person by name, lineage, and appearance per the mufta bihi position.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Ruling on Defendant Claiming Item from Absentee
Inter-madhhab dispute on repelling lawsuit by absentee claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when a defendant can repel a lawsuit by claiming the item is from an absentee, and distinguish the Hanafi view from the views of Ibn Shubruma, Ibn Abi Layla, and Abu Yusuf's later view.

Core Idea
A defendant can repel a lawsuit by proving the item is from an absentee, but only if witnesses can fully identify that absentee.

In this passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the author presents a key ruling in qada: when the defendant (possessor) claims that the disputed item came from an absentee third party, can the lawsuit be repelled? The Hanafi school provides a conditional yes, while several other scholars object. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi emphasis on enabling the plaintiff to pursue the true owner.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: the lawsuit is repelled if the defendant produces evidence and the witnesses know the absentee by name, lineage, and face.
This allows the plaintiff to pursue the absentee directly.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Ibn Shubruma: lawsuit is never repelled even with evidence. Ibn Abi Layla: confession by defendant is enough. Abu Yusuf later: conditional on defendant's righteousness.
Each view has a different threshold for repelling.
3
Follow the opponents' proofs
Ibn Shubruma: the defendant remains a litigant regardless of evidence. Ibn Abi Layla: confession creates an admission. Abu Yusuf: character impacts credibility.
No explicit counter-proof given in this text.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi view is based on enabling the plaintiff to pursue the real owner; once a clear path exists, the defendant should be released.
This is a practical rationale supporting the rule.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi, defendant is freed if conditions met; under Ibn Shubruma, defendant always remains liable; under Ibn Abi Layla, confession alone suffices; under Abu Yusuf, defendant's character matters.
The mufta bihi follows the Hanafi condition of full witness knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خصومة المدعي وإن قال: ابتعته من الغائب، أو قال المدعي: سرق مني، وقال المدعى عليه أي اشتريته للمدعى عليه هذا الشيء ذو اليد: أودعنيه فلان، وبرهن عليه لا، وإن قال المدعي: ابتعته من فلان، وقال الذي هو المدعى عليه هذا العين ذو اليد : أودعنيه فلان ذلك . أي المدعى عليه أي هذا العين أي الشيء بنفسه أي اشتريت هذا العين الغائب هذا وبالعكس تندفع، وقال ابن الشبرمة لا تندفع ولو أقام البينة، وقال ابن أبي ليلى: تندفع بإقراره للغائب من غير إقامة البينة، وقال أبو يوسف آخراً: إن كان المدعى عليه صالحاً تندفع، وإن كان معروفاً بالحيل والافتعال لا تندفع وإن أقام بينة وهذا كله فيما إذا قال الشهود: نعرف صاحب المال، وهو المودع أو المعير أو المؤجر باسمه ونسبه ووجهه؛ لأن المدعي يمكنه أن يتبعه.
The plaintiff's lawsuit: If he says, 'I bought it from the absentee,' or the plaintiff says, 'It was stolen from me,' and the defendant (the possessor) says, 'Such-and-such deposited it with me,' and he proves it, then it is not [repelled]. If the plaintiff says, 'I bought it from so-and-so,' and the defendant (the possessor) says, 'Such-and-such deposited it with me' – the lawsuit is repelled in the reverse. Ibn Shubruma said: 'It is not repelled even if he produces evidence.' Ibn Abi Layla said: 'It is repelled by his confession to the absentee without producing evidence.' Abu Yusuf later said: 'If the defendant is righteous, it is repelled; if he is known for tricks and fabrication, it is not repelled even if he produces evidence.' All this applies when the witnesses say: 'We know the owner of the property – the depositor, lender, or lessor – by name, lineage, and face,' because the plaintiff can then pursue him.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on how a defendant (dhu al-yad) can repel a lawsuit by claiming the disputed item came from an absentee third party. The base ruling states that if the defendant proves his claim, the lawsuit is repelled – but only if the witnesses can identify the absentee owner by name, lineage, and face. The passage then records three different opinions: Ibn Shubruma holds that even with evidence, the lawsuit is not repelled; Ibn Abi Layla holds it is repelled by mere confession without evidence; Abu Yusuf later distinguished between a righteous defendant (repelled) and one known for tricks (not repelled). The Hanafi mufta bihi position follows the condition that witnesses must know the owner fully, otherwise the repelling does not occur.

Key Terms
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The plaintiff or claimant who initiates the lawsuit.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a 'alayhi): The defendant or the person against whom the claim is made.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The possessor of the disputed item; the defendant in a property claim.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): An absentee – the person from whom the defendant claims to have received the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when can a defendant who claims the item is from an absentee repel the plaintiff's lawsuit?
A) By simply claiming it is from an absentee.
B) By producing evidence, but only if witnesses know the absentee by name, lineage, and face.
C) By confessing to the absentee.
D) Only if the defendant is known to be righteous.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi court repels the lawsuit against the defendant if he proves the item is from an absentee whom witnesses can fully identify, enabling the plaintiff to pursue the real owner.

Face Recognition vs. Full Knowledge in Qada
Intra-Hanafi dispute on face recognition in qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding face-only witness recognition and explain which position is mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
Face recognition suffices to repel a lawsuit under Abu Hanifa, but not under Muhammad.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school about the evidentiary value of witness recognition. The question is: if witnesses know the absentee only by face, can the defendant use that to prove the absentee is not the plaintiff, thereby escaping the lawsuit? Abu Hanifa says yes; Imam Muhammad says no. Understanding their respective proofs and the underlying purpose of evidence is key to grasping the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial consensus ruling
The passage begins with a case where witnesses recognize the absentee only by face and know nothing else. The judge does not accept their testimony by consensus because the plaintiff might be the depositor himself.
This is a separate scenario from the main dispute.
2
State the disputed scenario
Now witnesses recognize by face but do not know the name or lineage. Here, Muhammad and Abu Hanifa differ.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi.
3
Analyze Muhammad's position and proof
Muhammad says the lawsuit is not repelled because face recognition is not real knowledge. He cites a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said that not knowing name/lineage means not knowing the person.
Muhammad's proof is a hadith ahad with zanni thubut and dalala.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa says the lawsuit is repelled because the defendant has proven via eyewitnesses that the item came from someone else (the absentee), and face recognition gives certainty that the absentee is not the plaintiff. The witness's testimony removes the defendant from being a litigant.
Abu Hanifa reinterprets the hadith as denying complete knowledge, not the limited knowledge needed here.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: under Muhammad the defendant remains a litigant; under Abu Hanifa he is freed. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa: face recognition suffices.
The passage also notes this issue is called 'Mukhammasa' due to five applications or five imams differing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا: نعرفه بوجهه ولا نعرفه بشيء من ذلك، لا يقبل القاضي شهادتهم؛ لاحتمال أن يكون المودع هو المدعي؛ ولأنه ما أحاله إلى معين يمكن المدعي اتباعه فلو اندفعت الخصومة لتضرر المدعي، فلا تندفع الخصومة لتقرر المدعي فلا تندفع الخصومة عن ذي اليد بالإجماع وإن قالوا: نعرفه بوجهه ولا نعرفه باسمه ونسبه، لا تندفع الخصومة عند محمد؛ لأن المعرفة بالوجه ليست بمعرفة؛ لما روي أنه عليه السلام قال الرجل: 'تعرف فلاناً؟ فقال: نعم، فقال: هل تعرف اسمه ونسبه؟ فقال: لا، فقال: إذا لا تعرفه'. وعند أبي حنيفة تندفع الخصومة عنه؛ لأن المدعى عليه أثبت ببينته أن العين وصلت إليه من جهة غيره، حيث عرفه الشهود بوجه للعلم يقيناً أن المودع غير المدعي، فأفادت الشهادة أن يده ليست يد خصومة، وهو المقصود، والحديث دل على نفي المعرفة التامة، وليس على ذي اليد تعريف خصم المدعي تعريفاً تاماً، إنما عليه أن يثبت أنه ليس بخصم، وقد أثبت، وتسمى هذه المسألة مخمسة كتاب الدعوى، إما لأنها خمس صور من دعوى الوديعة والعارية والإجارة والرهن والغصب، أو فيها اختلاف خمسة من الأئمة رمز الحقائق: ١٩٩/٢] (فتح، عناية)
And if they say: 'We know him by his face but do not know anything else of that, the judge does not accept their testimony because it is possible that the depositor is the plaintiff himself; and because he has not referred him to a specific person whom the plaintiff can pursue. So if the lawsuit were repelled, the plaintiff would be harmed. Therefore, the lawsuit is not repelled from the possessor by consensus. And if they say: 'We know him by his face but do not know his name or lineage,' the lawsuit is not repelled according to Muhammad because knowledge by face is not knowledge, as it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to a man: 'Do you know so-and-so?' He said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Do you know his name and lineage?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'Then you do not know him.' According to Abu Hanifa, the lawsuit is repelled because the defendant has proved by his evidence that the item came to him from another, since the witnesses know him by face, so it is certain that the depositor is not the plaintiff. The testimony shows that his hand is not a hand of litigation, which is the purpose. The hadith indicates the denial of complete knowledge, but the possessor is not required to fully identify the plaintiff's opponent; he only needs to prove that he is not an opponent, and he has proven it. This issue is called the 'Mukhammasa' of the Book of Lawsuits, either because it involves five types of claims (deposit, loan, rent, pledge, usurpation) or because five imams differed on it. Rami al-Haqaiq 2/199 (Fath, Inayah).
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage discusses a variation of the previous ruling: what if the witnesses recognize the absentee only by face, not by name or lineage? The text first states that if witnesses recognize only by face and nothing else, the judge rejects their testimony – this is by consensus. Then there is a split: if they recognize by face but not name/lineage, Imam Muhammad holds the lawsuit is not repelled, citing a hadith that equates knowledge with knowing name and lineage. Imam Abu Hanifa argues the opposite: the witness's recognition by face is sufficient to confirm the absentee is not the plaintiff, so the defendant has met his burden. The passage also explains why this issue is called 'Mukhammasa' – either because it applies to five types of contracts (deposit, loan, rent, pledge, usurpation) or because five imams had different views. The mufta bihi position is that face recognition suffices to repel the lawsuit, following Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
المعرفة بالوجه (al-ma'rifa bi al-wajh): Recognition by face only, without knowing name or lineage.
مخمسة (mukhammasa): A five-fold issue in the Book of Lawsuits; either five types of claims or five imams differing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what is the ruling when witnesses recognize the absentee only by face, without knowing name or lineage?
A) The lawsuit is repelled because face recognition suffices to prove the absentee is not the plaintiff.
B) The lawsuit is not repelled because face recognition is not considered knowledge.
C) The judge rejects the testimony because the witnesses cannot name the absentee.
D) The case is referred to a higher judge for arbitration.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, face-only recognition of an absentee witness is sufficient to repel the lawsuit, following the mufta bihi view of Abu Hanifa.

Three Cases Where Lawsuit Is Not Repelled
Ruling statement with intra-Hanafi proof dispute over three procedural exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where a lawsuit is not repelled according to the majority Hanafi position and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in the passive voice case.

Core Idea
Passive voice does not shield defendant from being a litigant; istihsan overrides qiyas to preserve plaintiff's right.

In judicial procedure (qada), the phrasing of a claim can affect procedural outcomes. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq outlines three specific scenarios where the defendant cannot avoid litigation by claiming the item belongs to an absentee. While Imam Muhammad argues by qiyas that passive construction should repel the lawsuit, the majority relies on istihsan to keep the defendant in court. Let us examine each case and the reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
List the three scenarios where the lawsuit is not repelled: (1) plaintiff claims he bought from an absentee, (2) plaintiff claims defendant usurped it from him, (3) plaintiff says 'It was stolen from me' (passive).
The common thread: defendant is a direct adversary.
2
Understand why defendant becomes litigant
For each case, explain the legal effect: in (1) the defendant admits ownership; in (2) and (3) the claim implies action against the defendant despite passive wording.
Passive voice is used to protect defendant from hadd, but does not remove opponent status.
3
Contrast Muhammad's qiyas with majority istihsan
Muhammad argues by qiyas: if claim is passive (no direct action), lawsuit should be repelled, like in 'usurped from me' passive. Majority uses istihsan: passive construction is protective, but the underlying reality is a claim against defendant.
Istihsan prevails because the plaintiff's intention matters more than verbal form.
4
Confirm the mufta bihi position
Despite Muhammad's objection, the ruling 'unanimously' (ittiqa) holds that the lawsuit is not repelled in all three cases.
This is the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سرق مني أو قال: غصبته مني أو سرقته. (ط) لا [ أي أقام ذو اليد بينة على ما قال (معدن)] أي لا تندفع الخصومة في الصور الثلاث، أما الأولى: فإن المدعى عليه يقول : اشتريت هذا الشيء من الغائب فهو بدعواه الشراء صار معترفاً بأن يده يد ملك، فيكون معترفاً بأنه خصم، وفي الثانية: أي فيما إذا قال المدعي للمدعى عليه غصبته مني صار خصماً باعتبار دعوى الفعل عليه لا بيده، وفي الثالثة: أي فيما إذا قال المدعي: سرق مني يصير خصماً بدعوى الفعل عليه أيضاً في المعنى، وإنما جعله بالبناء للمفعول؛ لأجل الستر عليه كي لا يقطع يده، فصار كأنه قال: سرقته مني، وقال محمد: تندفع الخصومة عنه، وهو القياس؛ لأنه لم يدع عليه الفعل، فصار كما لو قال غصب مني على البناء للمفعول، فإنه تندفع به ووجه الاستحسان ما بيناه رمز الحقائق: ٢٠٠/٢] (فتح) اتفاقاً،
'[He says:] It was stolen from me' or he says: 'He usurped it from me' or 'He stole it from me.' 'No' – meaning the possessor established evidence for what he said – i.e., the lawsuit is not repelled in three cases: First: the defendant says 'I bought this thing from the absentee,' so by claiming purchase he admits that his hand is a hand of ownership, thus he admits he is an opponent. Second: when the plaintiff says to the defendant 'You usurped it from me,' he becomes an opponent by virtue of the claim of action against him, not by his hand. Third: when the plaintiff says 'It was stolen from me,' he becomes an opponent by the claim of action against him as well; he phrased it in passive voice to protect him so that his hand is not cut off; it is as if he said 'I stole it from me.' Muhammad said: 'The lawsuit is repelled from him, and that is by qiyas because he did not claim an action against him, so it is like if he said 'It was usurped from me' in passive voice, then it is repelled thereby. The basis of istihsan is what we explained.' Rami al-Haqaiq 2/200 (Fath) – unanimously.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage addresses three specific scenarios where, despite the defendant claiming the item from an absentee, the lawsuit is NOT repelled. The three are: (1) the plaintiff claims he bought the item from the absentee (which makes the defendant admit ownership), (2) the plaintiff claims the defendant usurped the item from him, (3) the plaintiff claims the item was stolen from him (passive voice). In each case, the defendant becomes a direct adversary. However, Imam Muhammad disagrees: he argues by qiyas that if the plaintiff's claim is passive (like 'usurped from me'), the defendant is not a litigant because no direct action is alleged. The text prefers istihsan to uphold the ruling that the lawsuit is not repelled. The phrase 'unanimously' at the end confirms this ruling is agreed upon despite Muhammad's objection.

Key Terms
البناء للمفعول (al-bina' li al-maf'ul): Passive voice construction used to avoid directly accusing the defendant of a crime.
الاستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding qiyas for public interest or to avoid hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the majority Hanafi ruling that the lawsuit is not repelled when the plaintiff uses passive voice?
A) Qiyas with the case of usurpation
B) Istihsan because the passive voice is a protective device
C) The explicit text of the Quran
D) Ijma of all scholars

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi judicial procedure, passive voice in a claim does not repel the lawsuit; istihsan keeps the defendant as a litigant to protect the plaintiff's right.

Dropping a Lawsuit When Both Parties Agree the Item Belongs to an Absentee
Ruling on dropping a lawsuit by mutual agreement on absentee ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a lawsuit is automatically dropped without evidence when both parties agree the item belongs to an absentee, and identify the exceptions that prevent this dropping.

Core Idea
Mutual agreement on absentee ownership ends the lawsuit without evidence.

This lesson examines a specific procedural rule in Hanafi qada: when both the plaintiff and the defendant (possessor) agree that the disputed property rightfully belongs to an absentee, the lawsuit is automatically terminated. No evidence is required because the parties are not truly in conflict. However, the rule has important exceptions involving agency and the identity of the absentee. Understanding this ruling helps you grasp how Hanafi law avoids unjust judgments against absent persons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
First, note the plaintiff claims ownership from an absentee; the possessor claims his possession is also from the same absentee. Both agree the absentee is the true owner.
2
Confirm mutual agreement on the absentee
The lawsuit drops only if both parties explicitly agree that the item belongs to the absentee. No evidence is needed for this dropping.
The agreement must be clear and unambiguous.
3
Check for evidence of agency
If the plaintiff produces evidence that he was appointed agent by the absentee to collect the item, the lawsuit is not dropped. The plaintiff then has a stronger right to possession.
Agency must be proven by evidence, not mere claim.
4
Verify the identity of the absentee
The absentee must be the same person for both parties. If the plaintiff claims purchase from one absentee and the defendant claims deposit from a different absentee, the lawsuit is not dropped.
Discrepancy in identity prevents the dropping.
5
Assess claims about deputy deposit
If the possessor says the item was deposited by a deputy of the absentee, this claim does not drop the lawsuit unless the deputy's agency is proven. The possessor must provide evidence of the agency.
Mere assertion of agency is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سقطت الخصومة [ أي خصومة المدعي عن ذي اليد بغير بينة بالاتفاق. (معدن)] أي بغير بينة، وحاصل هذه المسألة: أن المدعي ادعى الملك بسبب من جهة الغائب، فدفعه ذو اليد بأن يده من الغائب، فقد اتفقا على أن الملك فيه للغائب، فيكون وصولها إلى ذي اليد من جهته، فلم تكن يده يد خصومة إلا أن يقيم المدعي بينة أن فلاناً وكله بقبضه؛ لأنه أثبت ببينته كونه أحق بإمساكها. ولو صدقه ذو اليد في شرائه منه لا يأمره القاضي بالتسليم إليه حتى لا يكون قضاء على الغائب بإقراره، ودعوى الشراء في المسألة ليست بقيد، فلو ادعى المدعي أنه غصبه منه فلان الغائب وبرهن عليه، وزعم ذو اليد أن هذا الغائب أودعه عنده اندفعت الخصومة؛ لتوافقهما على أن اليد لذلك الرجل، وقوله: 'وقال ذو اليد: أودعنيه فلان ذلك أي أودعنيه بنفسه، ولو قال ذو اليد أودعنيه وكيل فلان ذلك، لا تندفع الخصومة بلا بينة؛ لأن الوكالة لا تثبت بقوله، وقيد باتحاد الغائب، لأنه لو ادعى المدعي الشراء من فلان الغائب المالك، وبرهن ذو اليد على إيداع غائب آخر، لا تندفع الخصومة. (عيني، فتح)
The lawsuit falls – meaning the plaintiff's lawsuit against the possessor without evidence, by agreement. That is, without evidence. The summary of this issue: The plaintiff claimed ownership by a cause from the absentee, and the possessor repelled by saying his hand is from the absentee. Both agreed that ownership belongs to the absentee. Thus, the item came to the possessor from him, so his hand is not a hand of litigation – unless the plaintiff produces evidence that so-and-so appointed him as agent to receive it, because by his evidence he proves he is more entitled to hold it. If the possessor affirms his purchase from him, the judge does not order delivery to him to avoid ruling against the absentee by his confession. The claim of purchase is not a restriction in the issue. If the plaintiff claims that so-and-so the absentee usurped it from him and proves it, and the possessor claims that this absentee deposited it with him, the lawsuit is repelled because they agree that the hand is for that man. And his statement: 'The possessor said: So-and-so deposited it with me' means he deposited it with him in person. If the possessor says 'The deputy of so-and-so deposited it with me,' the lawsuit is not repelled without evidence because agency is not established by his statement. It is conditioned on the identity of the absentee because if the plaintiff claims purchase from so-and-so the owner, and the possessor proves deposit from another absentee, the lawsuit is not repelled. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling: if both the plaintiff and the defendant (possessor) agree that the disputed item belongs to an absentee, the lawsuit is automatically dropped without requiring evidence. The reasoning is that both parties confirm the item came from a third party, so the defendant is not a true litigant. However, there are exceptions: if the plaintiff can prove he was the absentee's agent for collection, the lawsuit can continue. Also, if the possessor claims the item came from a designated agent of the absentee, he must prove that agency. The identity of the absentee must be the same – if the plaintiff claims purchase from one absentee and the defendant claims deposit from another, the lawsuit is not repelled. This ruling is final and prevents unnecessary litigation.

Key Terms
سقوط الخصومة (suqut al-khusumah): The termination of the lawsuit without a ruling on merits.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): A judgment against an absentee, which is not permitted in Hanafi law without evidence.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who currently possesses the disputed item.
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; the legal authority to act on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for the lawsuit to drop when both parties agree the item belongs to an absentee?
A) The plaintiff must produce evidence of his claim.
B) The defendant must produce evidence of his possession.
C) Both parties must agree on the absentee and the absentee must be the same person.
D) The judge must confirm the absentee's ownership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, when both parties agree the disputed item belongs to an absentee, the lawsuit is automatically dropped without evidence, unless the plaintiff proves agency or the absentee's identity differs.

Dismissal of Claim Against Possessor Holding from Missing Person
Explanation of when a lawsuit falls if possessor's hand is from a missing person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a lawsuit against a possessor is dismissed in Hanafi fiqh, and identify when the possessor's hand becomes litigious.

Core Idea
When both parties agree the property belongs to a missing person, the lawsuit falls unless the claimant proves agency.

In Hanafi procedural law, the default presumption is that possession indicates ownership. However, complications arise when a missing person (gha'ib) is involved. This lesson from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains when a claimant's lawsuit against a possessor (dhu al-yad) is dismissed because both agree the true owner is absent, and when the possessor's hand becomes litigious (yad khusuma).

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreement leading to dismissal
Recognize that when both claimant and possessor agree the property belongs to a missing person, the lawsuit falls. The possessor's hand is from the missing person and is not litigious.
The agreement must be about the same missing person.
2
Identify the exception requiring evidence
The lawsuit does not fall if the claimant brings evidence (bayyinah) that the missing person appointed him as agent to take possession. In that case, the possessor's hand becomes litigious.
Agency cannot be proven by the possessor's statement alone.
3
Apply the unity of the missing person
If the claimant claims from one missing person and the possessor claims from another, the lawsuit is not dismissed. The missing persons must be identical.
This is a key condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى أحكام الخصومة سقطت الخصومة. سقطت الخصومة [ أي خصومة المدعي عن ذي اليد بغير بينة بالاتفاق. (معدن)] أي بغير بينة، وحاصل هذه المسألة: أن المدعي ادعى الملك بسبب من جهة الغائب، فدفعه ذو اليد بأن يده من الغائب، فقد اتفقا على أن الملك فيه للغائب، فيكون وصولها إلى ذي اليد من جهته، فلم تكن يده يد خصومة إلا أن يقيم المدعي بينة أن فلاناً وكله بقبضه؛ لأنه أثبت ببينته كونه أحق بإمساكها. ولو صدقه ذو اليد في شرائه منه لا يأمره القاضي بالتسليم إليه حتى لا يكون قضاء على الغائب بإقراره، ودعوى الشراء في المسألة ليست بقيد، فلو ادعى المدعي أنه غصبه منه فلان الغائب وبرهن عليه، وزعم ذو اليد أن هذا الغائب أودعه عنده اندفعت الخصومة؛ لتوافقهما على أن اليد لذلك الرجل، وقوله: "وقال ذو اليد: أودعنيه فلان ذلك أي أودعنيه بنفسه، ولو قال ذو اليد أودعنيه وكيل فلان ذلك، لا تندفع الخصومة بلا بينة؛ لأن الوكالة لا تثبت بقوله، وقيد باتحاد الغائب، لأنه لو ادعى المدعي الشراء من فلان الغائب المالك، وبرهن ذو اليد على إيداع غائب الآخر، لا تندفع الخصومة. (عيني، فتح)
Book of Claims. Rulings on Litigation. The litigation falls. 'The litigation falls [i.e., the claimant's litigation against the possessor without evidence by agreement. (Ma'din)] i.e., without evidence. The summary of this issue: The claimant claimed ownership by a cause from the missing person, and the possessor rebutted by saying his hand is from the missing person. So they agreed that ownership belongs to the missing person. Thus the possession came to the possessor from him, so his hand is not a litigious hand unless the claimant brings evidence that so-and-so appointed him as agent to take possession, because he proves by his evidence that he is more entitled to hold it. And if the possessor affirms his purchase from him, the judge does not order him to hand it over to him so that there is no judgment against the missing person by his admission. The claim of purchase in the issue is not a restriction. So if the claimant claims that the missing person had usurped it from him and proves it, and the possessor claims that this missing person deposited it with him, the litigation is removed because they agree that the hand is for that man. And his statement: 'And the possessor said: So-and-so deposited it with me' means he deposited it with me himself. And if the possessor said: The agent of so-and-so deposited it with me, the litigation is not removed without evidence because agency is not established by his statement. And it is restricted by the unity of the missing person. Because if the claimant claims purchase from the missing owner, and the possessor proves deposit by another missing person, the litigation is not removed. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kitab al-Da'wa (Book of Claims) on when a lawsuit is dismissed. The basic ruling: If a claimant sues a possessor (dhu al-yad) claiming ownership from a missing person (gha'ib), and the possessor responds that he also holds from that same missing person, then both agree the property belongs to the missing person. In such a case, the possessor's hand is not considered litigious (yad khusuma) because it is derived from the absent owner. The lawsuit is dismissed unless the claimant brings evidence that the missing person had appointed him (the claimant) as an agent to take possession. The text further details: If the possessor admits that the claimant bought from the missing person, the judge still does not order delivery because that would be a judgment against the absent person based on admission. The claim of purchase is not a restricting condition; similar rules apply if the claimant claims usurpation (ghasb). The possessor's claim that the missing person deposited with him also leads to dismissal. However, if the possessor claims deposit by an agent of the missing person, the litigation is not dismissed without evidence, because agency is not proven by his mere statement. Also, the missing person must be the same; if the claimant claims from one missing person and the possessor claims deposit from another missing person, the litigation continues.

Key Terms
ذي اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who physically possesses the property in dispute; the defendant in a claim of property
غائب (gha'ib): An absent person; the third party from whom both claimant and possessor derive their claims
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually two male witnesses or one male and two females

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case described, when does the lawsuit fall (tandafi' al-khusuma)?
A) When the claimant brings evidence of agency from the missing person
B) When both claimant and possessor agree the property belongs to a missing person
C) When the possessor admits the claimant's purchase from the missing person
D) When the missing person appears in court

Key Takeaway
The lawsuit against a possessor falls when both parties agree the property belongs to the same missing person, unless the claimant proves agency.

Ruling When Two Claimants Present Evidence on a Property
Explanation of Hanafi ruling on two property claimants with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present evidence on an 'ayn in a third party's hand, and explain the proof from hadith and the response to opposing schools.

Core Idea
When both claimants bring evidence, the property is divided equally — the Prophet ruled so.

This lesson examines a classic qada' scenario: two claimants each produce evidence that an item in someone else's possession belongs to them. The Hanafi school rules that the item is divided half-half, based on explicit prophetic precedent. We will explore the proof, the objections from other madhahib, and the Hanafi rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two claimants claim an 'ayn in the hand of a third party, each claiming absolute ownership without specifying cause or date. Both bring evidence.
If only one brings evidence, he gets the whole.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The judge rules the property between them in halves (tansif).
3
Locate the dalil
The hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafa and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: the Prophet divided a camel half-half.
Hadith is ahad, so zanni in thubut but accepted as proof.
4
Analyze the dispute
Shafi'i says evidences cancel or lottery; Malik prefers more just witnesses; Awza'i prefers more witnesses.
Hanafi response: lottery was abrogated when gambling was prohibited.
5
Understand the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, supported by the explicit hadith and rejection of lottery.
This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى ما يدعيه الرجلان باب ما يدعيه الرجلان أي في بيان أحكامه رف ك د برهنا على ما في يد آخر، قضي لهما، وعلى نكاح امرأة، سقطا، وهي لمن صدقته، أي حكم بذلك العين أي ولو برهنا على إلخ أي المرأة الرجلان أي على ثالث أو أحدهما على الآخر، ووجه تأخير هذا الباب عن دعوى الواحد: أن الواحد قبل المتعدد. (عيني) قضي لهما أي إذا ادعى المدعيان عيناً في يد آخر، وكل يزعم أنها له ملكاً مطلقاً، ولم يذكرا سبب الملك، ولا تاريخه وبرهنا على دعواهما، قضى بها لهما على سبيل التنصيف، وقيد ببرهانهما؛ إذ لو برهن أحدهما فقط، فإنه يقضى له بالكل، وإنما يقضى لهما على التنصيف؛ لتساويهما، ولحديث تميم بن طرفة الطائي: "أن رجلين اختصما إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في ناقة أقام كل واحد منهما البينة، فقضى بها بينهما نصفين". ولما رواه أبو داود عن أبي موسى الأشعرى أن رجلين ادعيا بعيراً على عهد رسول الله وبعث كل واحد منهما بشاهدين، فقسمه رسول الله له نصفين" وقال الشافعي: إن البينتين يتساقطان، وبه قال أحمد في رواية، وعن الشافعي: : أنه يقرع بينهما؛ لما روى سعيد بن المسيب: أن رجلين تنازعا في أمة بين يدي رسول الله، وأقاما البينة، فأقرع رسول الله بينهما، وقال: "اللهم أنت تقضي بين عبادك بالحق"، ثم قضى بها لمن خرجت قرعته"، والجواب: أن القرعة كانت في ابتداء الإسلام وقت إباحة القمار، ثم نسخت بحرمة القمار؛ فإنها في معناه، وعن الشافعي في قول يوقف أبداً حتى يعلم أحد البينتين، وعنه كقولنا وعن مالك في رواية: يقضى بأعدل البينتين، وقال الأوزاعي : يقضى لمن كان شهوده أكثر عدداً. (فتح، عيني، عناية) وعلى نكاح إلخ : أي لو أقام كل واحد من الرجلين بينة على أن هذه المرأة امرأته، سقط برهانهما؛ لتعذر العمل بهما؛ إذ النكاح لا يقبل الاشتراك، وفرق القاضي بينهما حيث لا مرجح، وإنما يسقط البرهانان إذا كانت الدعوى حال حياتها؛ لعدم ما تترجح به إحدى البينتين على الأخرى بأن لم يؤرخا، فلو أرخت إحداهما دون الأخرى قضى للمؤرخ، وإن كانت دعوى بعد موتها تقبل البينتان إن لم يؤرخا أو أرخا واستوى تاريخهما، وعلى كل منهما نصف المهر ويرثان ميراث زوج واحد. (عيني، فتح) وهي لمن صدقته [ أي تكون لمن صدقته في دعواه بأن تقول: هذا زوجي. (معدن)] لأن النكاح مما يحكم به بتصادق الزوجين، فيرجع إلى تصديقها، إلا إذا كانت في بيت أحدهما أو دخل بها أحدهما، فيكون هو أولى، ولا يعتبر قولها؛ لأن تمكنه من نقلها، أو من الدخول بها دليل على سبق عقده، إلا أن يقيم الآخر البينة أنه تزوجها قبله، فيكون هو أولى؛ لأن الصريح يفوق الدلالة.
Book of Claims. What two men claim. Chapter on what two men claim, i.e., explaining its rulings. ... They both presented evidence on what is in the hand of another, so it is adjudicated to them. And on the marriage of a woman, they both fall, and she belongs to the one she affirms. i.e., the ruling is upon that 'ayn. i.e., even if they both presented evidence, etc. i.e., the woman. The two men (i.e., against a third party or one of them against the other). The reason for placing this chapter after the claim of one is that the single person comes before multiple. (Ayni) 'Adjudicated to them' means: When two claimants claim an 'ayn in the hand of another, each claiming it as his absolute ownership, and they do not mention the cause of ownership nor its date, and they both present evidence on their claims, the judge rules it to them by way of half (tansif). It is restricted by their both presenting evidence; because if only one of them presented evidence, he would be awarded the whole. And it is only awarded to them half each because they are equal, and due to the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafa al-Ta'i: 'Two men disputed before the Messenger of Allah concerning a she-camel, each of them brought evidence, and he ruled it between them half each.' And also what Abu Dawud narrated from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: 'Two men claimed a camel during the time of the Messenger of Allah, and each sent two witnesses, so the Messenger of Allah divided it between them half each.' Al-Shafi'i said: The two evidences cancel each other out, and Ahmad said so in one narration. And from al-Shafi'i: He would draw lots between them, based on what Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib narrated: Two men disputed over a slave-girl before the Messenger of Allah, and they brought evidence, so the Messenger of Allah drew lots between them and said: 'O Allah, You judge between Your servants by truth,' then he ruled her to the one whose lot came out. The response: The lot was in early Islam at the time when gambling was permitted, then it was abrogated by the prohibition of gambling, for it is in its meaning. And from al-Shafi'i in one opinion: It is suspended forever until one of the two evidences is known. And from him like our saying. And from Malik in one narration: He judges by the more just of the two evidences. And al-Awza'i said: He judges in favor of the one whose witnesses are more numerous. (Fath, Ayni, Inaya) 'And on a marriage, etc.' i.e., if each of the two men brings evidence that this woman is his wife, their evidences fall because it is impossible to act upon both, since marriage does not accept sharing. The judge separates them when there is no preponderating factor. The two evidences only fall if the claim is during her lifetime, because there is nothing to preponderate one over the other if they are not dated. If one is dated and the other not, he rules for the one with the date. If the claim is after her death, both evidences are accepted if they are not dated or if they are dated and their dates are equal. Each of them pays half the mahr and they inherit the inheritance of one husband. (Ayni, Fath) 'She belongs to the one she affirms' i.e., she belongs to the one she affirms in his claim by saying: 'This is my husband.' (Ma'din) Because marriage is such that it is judged by mutual agreement of the spouses, so recourse is to her affirmation. Except if she is in the house of one of them or one of them has consummated (dakhala) with her, then he is more entitled, and her statement is not considered, because his ability to transfer her or consummation indicates the precedence of his contract. Unless the other brings evidence that he married her before him, then he is more entitled, because the explicit overrides the indication.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter 'On What Two Men Claim' from Kitab al-Da'wa. The matn states: If two men both present evidence on property in the hand of another, it is adjudicated to them (in halves). If on a marriage of a woman, both evidences fall, and she belongs to the one she affirms. The commentary expands: The ruling applies to a case where two claimants each claim an 'ayn (specific property) in the hand of a third party, each claiming absolute ownership without specifying a cause or date. They both bring evidence; the judge rules it between them in halves. This is based on the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafa and the hadith of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari reported by Abu Dawud. The Shafi'i school is reported to hold that the two evidences cancel each other out (yatasaqatan), or that a lottery (qur'a) should be drawn. The Hanafi response is that lottery was abrogated when gambling was prohibited, as it is similar to gambling. Other views: Ahmad in one narration agrees with Shafi'i; another narration from Shafi'i agrees with Hanafis; Malik in one narration says to prefer the more just witnesses; al-Awza'i says to prefer the more numerous witnesses. Then the text turns to the marriage case: If each man brings evidence that a woman is his wife, both evidences are discarded because marriage cannot be shared. The judge separates them. However, if the woman is alive, the evidences are only discarded if they are undated. If one is dated, the one with the earlier date prevails. If after her death, both evidences are accepted if undated or if dated equally, and each pays half the mahr and they inherit as one husband. The final part: The woman belongs to the one she affirms (by saying 'this is my husband') because marriage is established by mutual consent. However, if she is in the house of one of them or one has consummated the marriage with her, that man is preferred; her statement is not considered because his ability to transfer her or consummation indicates a prior contract. But if the other brings evidence that he married her earlier, he is preferred because explicit proof overrides circumstantial indication.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence brought by a claimant to prove his claim
قضاء (qada'): Judicial ruling by the judge
تنصيف (tansif): Division into halves
صدقت (saddaqat): The woman affirms a specific man as her husband in a marriage dispute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when two men each present evidence claiming ownership of the same item in a third party's hand, the item is:
A) Given to the first claimant
B) Divided equally between them
C) Held in trust until the truth emerges
D) Given to the possessor if he also claims it

Key Takeaway
When two claimants bring evidence on an 'ayn without dating, the Hanafi judge divides it equally.

Ruling When Two Men Claim Marriage to the Same Woman
Explanation of Hanafi ruling on two marriage claimants with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi ruling when two men each bring evidence claiming marriage to the same woman, and identify when the woman's affirmation or other factors determine the outcome.

Core Idea
In marriage claims, both evidences fall, and the woman's affirmation, consummation, or dating decides.

This lesson turns to a different scenario: two men each claim a woman as their wife, each with evidence. Since marriage cannot be shared, the Hanafi school says both evidences are discarded. The ruling then depends on factors like dating, the woman's affirmation, and physical indications like consummation. We will detail these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two men each bring evidence that the same woman is their wife. Marriage cannot be shared.
2
State the basic Hanafi ruling
Both evidences fall (sakat), and the judge separates them (if alive).
This applies when the evidence is undated.
3
Analyze the effect of dating
If one evidence is dated and the other not, the dated one prevails. If both dated equally, both fall.
4
Consider the case after death
If the woman is deceased and both evidences are undated or dated equally, both are accepted; each man pays half mahr and inherits half husband's share.
5
Examine the woman's affirmation
If alive and no dating, the woman belongs to the one she affirms (by saying 'this is my husband'). But if she is in one man's house or he has consummated, he is preferred, unless the other proves earlier marriage.
Explicit evidence overrides circumstantial indication.

Source Text

The two evidences only fall if the claim is during her lifetime, because there is nothing to preponderate one over the other if they are not dated. If one is dated and the other not, he rules for the one with the date. If the claim is after her death, both evidences are accepted if they are not dated or if they are dated and their dates are equal. Each of them pays half the mahr and they inherit the inheritance of one husband. (Ayni, Fath) 'She belongs to the one she affirms' i.e., she belongs to the one she affirms in his claim by saying: 'This is my husband.' (Ma'din) Because marriage is such that it is judged by mutual agreement of the spouses, so recourse is to her affirmation. Except if she is in the house of one of them or one of them has consummated (dakhala) with her, then he is more entitled, and her statement is not considered, because his ability to transfer her or consummation indicates the precedence of his contract. Unless the other brings evidence that he married her before him, then he is more entitled, because the explicit overrides the indication.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter 'On What Two Men Claim' from Kitab al-Da'wa. The matn states: If two men both present evidence on property in the hand of another, it is adjudicated to them (in halves). If on a marriage of a woman, both evidences fall, and she belongs to the one she affirms. The commentary expands: The ruling applies to a case where two claimants each claim an 'ayn (specific property) in the hand of a third party, each claiming absolute ownership without specifying a cause or date. They both bring evidence; the judge rules it between them in halves. This is based on the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafa and the hadith of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari reported by Abu Dawud. The Shafi'i school is reported to hold that the two evidences cancel each other out (yatasaqatan), or that a lottery (qur'a) should be drawn. The Hanafi response is that lottery was abrogated when gambling was prohibited, as it is similar to gambling. Other views: Ahmad in one narration agrees with Shafi'i; another narration from Shafi'i agrees with Hanafis; Malik in one narration says to prefer the more just witnesses; al-Awza'i says to prefer the more numerous witnesses. Then the text turns to the marriage case: If each man brings evidence that a woman is his wife, both evidences are discarded because marriage cannot be shared. The judge separates them. However, if the woman is alive, the evidences are only discarded if they are undated. If one is dated, the one with the earlier date prevails. If after her death, both evidences are accepted if undated or if dated equally, and each pays half the mahr and they inherit as one husband. The final part: The woman belongs to the one she affirms (by saying 'this is my husband') because marriage is established by mutual consent. However, if she is in the house of one of them or one has consummated the marriage with her, that man is preferred; her statement is not considered because his ability to transfer her or consummation indicates a prior contract. But if the other brings evidence that he married her earlier, he is preferred because explicit proof overrides circumstantial indication.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence brought by a claimant to prove his claim
قضاء (qada'): Judicial ruling by the judge
تنصيف (tansif): Division into halves
صدقت (saddaqat): The woman affirms a specific man as her husband in a marriage dispute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if two men bring undated evidence claiming the same woman as wife while she is alive, the judge:
A) Divides her time between them
B) Accepts both and lets her choose
C) Rejects both and separates them
D) Accepts the one with more witnesses

Key Takeaway
When two men claim the same woman, both evidences are rejected unless one is dated, and the woman's affirmation or consummation determines the outcome.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Hanafi Ruling on Conflicting Property Claims with Equal Evidence
Inter-madhhab dispute on conflicting property claims with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present equal evidence for a property, explain the Shafi'i opposition, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafah.

Core Idea
Equal evidence leads to equal division — Hanafi law gives effect to both proofs rather than canceling them.

When two men each claim ownership of the same item and both produce witnesses, the judge faces a dilemma. The Hanafi school resolves this by equally dividing the property, a ruling derived from the Prophet's own judgment. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position, the Shafi'i challenge, and the evidence behind each.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
When two claimants both bring evidence for a property without dates or causes, the judge rules that it belongs to both equally, divided half and half.
This is derived from the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafah.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the evidences cancel each other (tasāqut) or that lots (qur'ah) should be drawn. Other views include Malik preferring the more just evidence and al-Awza'i preferring the larger number of witnesses.
Ahmad also follows tasāqut in one narration.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Prophet divided a camel between two claimants with equal evidence. This is taken as a binding precedent by Hanafis.
Hadith narrated by Tamim ibn Tarafah and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i evidence
Shafi'i relies on a hadith where the Prophet cast lots between two claimants over a slave-woman and a logical argument that contradictory proofs cancel.
The qur'ah hadith is from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Hanafi scholars argue that qur'ah was abrogated because it resembles gambling, which was later prohibited. They assert that the evidences do not cancel but must both be given effect by division.
The cancellation view would ignore valid proofs; division is a practical reconciliation.
