.
Book of Claims. Chapter: What two men claim – i.e., an explanation of its rulings. If they provide proof regarding something in the hand of another, it is judged for them both; and regarding a marriage of a woman, both proofs are dropped, and the woman belongs to the one she affirms. i.e., the judgment is given for the property, even if they provide proof etc. i.e., the woman. The two men: i.e., against a third party, or one against the other. The reason for delaying this chapter after the claim of a single person: because the single person comes before multiple. (Ayni) 'Judged for them both' – i.e., if two claimants claim a property in the hand of another, each claiming it is absolutely his ownership, and they do not mention the cause of ownership nor its date, and they provide evidence for their claim, then it is judged for them both by way of half and half. It is restricted to their providing evidence; because if only one provided evidence, then it is judged entirely for him. They are judged half and half because of their equality, and due to the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafah al-Ta'i: 'Two men disputed before the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) regarding a she-camel, each brought evidence, and he judged it between them in halves.' And also what Abu Dawud narrated from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: two men claimed a camel during the time of the Messenger of Allah and each sent two witnesses, so the Messenger of Allah divided it between them in halves. Al-Shafi'i said: the two evidences cancel each other; Ahmad also said that in one narration. And from al-Shafi'i: he draws lots between them, based on the narration of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: two men disputed over a slave-woman before the Messenger of Allah, and each brought evidence, so the Messenger of Allah cast lots between them and said, 'O Allah, You judge between Your servants with truth,' then he judged for the one whose lot came out. The response: casting lots was in early Islam at the time when gambling was permissible, then it was abrogated by the prohibition of gambling, for it is similar in meaning. And from al-Shafi'i in one opinion: it is suspended forever until one of the two evidences is known. And from him is a saying like ours. And from Malik in one narration: judgment is given for the more just of the two evidences. Al-Awza'i said: judgment is given for the one whose witnesses are more in number. (Fath, Ayni, Inaya) 'And regarding a marriage etc.': i.e., if each of the two men provides evidence that this woman is his wife, then their evidences are dropped due to the impossibility of acting upon both, because marriage does not accept sharing, and the judge separates them when there is no preponderance. The two evidences are only dropped if the claim is during her lifetime, due to the lack of anything that could give preponderance to one evidence over the other, if they did not mention a date. If one of them mentions a date and the other does not, judgment is given for the one with the date. If the claim is after her death, both evidences are accepted if they did not mention a date or if they mentioned a date and the dates are equal. And each of the two is obliged to pay half the dowry, and they inherit the inheritance of one husband. (Ayni, Fath) 'And she belongs to the one she affirms': i.e., she belongs to the one she affirms in his claim, by saying 'this is my husband.' (Ma'din) Because marriage is something that can be judged by the mutual affirmation of the spouses, so recourse is taken to her affirmation, except if she is in the house of one of them or if one has had sexual intercourse with her, in which case he has priority, and her statement is not considered; because his ability to move her or to have intercourse with her is evidence of the precedence of his contract, unless the other brings evidence that he married her before him, in which case he has priority, because clear proof outweighs indication. (Fath, Ayni) 'or his evidence preceded, and regarding purchase from him, each has half of it for its price if he wishes, and if one refuses after the judgment, the other does not take the whole.' i.e., even if they provide evidence etc. the owner of the hand. 'half of the claimed amount' i.e., half of the price. 'the judgment, the other does not take the whole. And if they mention a date, then it is for the earlier; otherwise, for the one in possession.' i.e., the other claimant. 'or his evidence preceded' i.e., or it is for the one whose evidence for marriage preceded. (T) The apparent meaning is that preference by affirmation is on the same level as preference by precedence of date, but it is not so. Even if she affirms the one whose date does not precede, her affirmation is not considered, and judgment is given for marriage to the one whose date precedes, because precedence of date is more weighty than everything, then possession, then sexual intercourse, then acknowledgment. If the author said: 'and she belongs to the one she affirms if no date precedes the acknowledgment,' it would be better. As for preference by the existence of a date from only one of them, it is conditional on two matters: the absence of her affirmation of the other, and that she is not in his possession. The conclusion is that if two claimants dispute over a woman and bring evidence, then if they mention a date and the date of one is earlier, it is for the earlier; if they do not mention dates or the dates are equal, and if one has possession such as sexual intercourse or moving her to his house, then that is considered; if nothing of that exists, then recourse is to the woman's affirmation. (Fath, Ayni) 'each has half of it for its price' i.e., the thing belongs to each of the two claimants. (T) i.e., if a slave is in the hand of a man, and each claims to have bought the slave from the man, and they bring evidence, and neither evidence mentions a time, then half of the slave is for each of the two claimants for half of the price, and if he wishes to leave it and take all the price. Because the condition of the contract he claims has changed upon him, and perhaps his desire was to own the whole thing and he did not achieve that, so he returns it and takes the full price. If it is said: the two evidences should be invalid because one of the parties is lying, as it is impossible for two contracts to fully occur at the same moment, the answer is that they did not testify that they occurred at the same moment, but they testified to the contract itself, so it is possible that each relied on a cause at a time that permitted him to testify to it. (Fath, Inaya) 'if he wishes' – if he wishes to leave it. Because when they are equal in the cause, they are given a choice, due to the impossibility of judging the entire thing for each one. (Ayni) 'and if one refuses' – by one of them refusing after the judgment, i.e., after the judge's ruling of it between them. (Ayni) 'the other does not take the whole' – i.e., all of the sold item. Because he has become subject to a judgment against him for half, so the contract is rescinded regarding it, and when a contract is rescinded by a judge's ruling, it cannot return except by a new contract, and that is not found. Unlike the case where he leaves it before the judgment of it between them, where the other may take all of it; because he proved by his evidence that he bought the whole, and he only returns to half due to the necessary competition of the judgment, and that has not occurred. (Fath, Inaya) 'And if they mention a date, then it is for the earlier' – i.e., if each of the two proofs mentions a date, then the ruling is for the earlier, because he proved the purchase at a time when no one else contested him, so the other is refuted by that. This is by consensus, except for one saying of al-Shafi'i that it is between them. 'Otherwise, for the one in possession' – i.e., if they do not mention a date, or they mention a single date, or one of them mentions a date, then the claimed item is for the one in possession, because his ability to take possession indicates the precedence of his purchase. (Fath)
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute from the Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the scenario where two men both claim ownership or marriage rights over the same subject, each providing evidence (bayyinah). The Hanafi school holds that when two claimants each bring evidence for a property (ayn) in the hand of a third party, and neither provides a cause or date of ownership, the judge rules that the property is divided equally between them. This is based on the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafah, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided a she-camel between two claimants with equal evidence. The text then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who in one view says the evidences cancel each other (tasāqut), and in another view advocates drawing lots (qur'ah). The Hanafi response argues that the qur'ah was abrogated as it resembled gambling. Other views are cited: Ahmad agrees with tasāqut, Malik prefers the more just evidence, and al-Awza'i prefers the party with more witnesses. The passage then discusses marriage claims: if two men each bring evidence that a woman is his wife, both evidences are dropped because marriage cannot be shared. The woman goes to the one she affirms (if she confirms one of them), unless one has prior date, possession (e.g., she lives in his house), or intercourse. In purchase claims from a hand-owner (dhū al-yad), if both buyers bring evidence without dates, the item is divided half and half, and each may take his half or return it and take back his price. If one refuses after judgment, the other does not get the whole item because the contract was judicially rescinded. If dates are given, the earlier date takes priority. If no dates or equal dates, the one in possession (who has taken delivery) is preferred. The commentary from 'Ayni, Fath, and 'Inaya provides detailed elaboration. The key lesson is that Hanafi jurisprudence prioritizes the practical possibility of acting on both evidences over their formal contradiction, using equality and external indicators (dates, possession, affirmation) to resolve conflicts.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence consisting of witnesses (shuhūd) presented in court to prove a claim.
برهان (burhān): Proof or argument; in this context, synonymous with bayyinah (evidence).
قضاء (qaḍā'): The judge's ruling or decision; also the act of adjudication.
تنصيف (tanṣīf): Division into two equal halves; the ruling of sharing equally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if two men both present evidence that they each own the same camel in the hand of a third party, and no dates or causes are given, how does the judge rule?
A) The judge drops both evidences and leaves the camel with the third party.
B) The judge divides the camel equally between the two claimants.
C) The judge draws lots to decide who gets the camel.
D) The judge awards the camel to the claimant with more witnesses.

Key Takeaway
When two claimants present equal evidence for a property without dates, Hanafi law divides it equally, reflecting the Prophet's ruling and reconciling both proofs.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Resolving Conflicting Marriage Claims in Hanafi Law
Hanafi ruling on conflicting marriage claims with evidence and preponderance factors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for prioritizing marriage claims when each claimant presents evidence, including the roles of dates, possession, and the woman's affirmation.

Core Idea
In marriage disputes, the woman's affirmation decides unless a preponderance (date, possession, intercourse) overrides.

When two men both claim to be married to the same woman and each brings evidence, the Hanafi school prescribes a detailed priority system. The woman's own statement is the default decider, but if one man has a prior date or has taken possession (living together or intercourse), that overrides her affirmation. This lesson explores those rules and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Two men each claim marriage to a woman and both provide evidence. The default ruling: both evidences drop because marriage cannot be shared, and the woman is asked to affirm one as her husband.
2
Identify preponderance factors that override affirmation
Priority is given to: (1) Earlier date of contract, (2) Possession (the woman in his house), (3) Having had intercourse with her, (4) The woman's own affirmation in the absence of the above.
These factors are hierarchical: date > possession > intercourse > affirmation.
3
Apply the hierarchy to a scenario
If one claimant has a prior date, he wins regardless of possession or affirmation. If dates are equal or absent, possession (residence) is considered. If no possession, intercourse is considered. If none, the woman's affirmation is decisive.
Exception: If the woman affirms a claimant with a later date, her affirmation is ignored if the other has a prior date.
4
Handle cases with only one evidence with date
If one evidence has a date and the other does not, the one with the date wins. This is by consensus except a Shafi'i view.
This parallels the property rule for dates.
5
Consider posthumous claims
If the woman is deceased, both evidences are accepted if no dates or equal dates; each man pays half the dowry and both inherit one husband's share.
This is because the woman cannot affirm, so the court relies on the evidence equally.

الملك، ولا تاريخه وبرهنا على دعواهما، قضى بها لهما على سبيل التنصيف، وقيد ببرهانهما؛ إذ لو برهن أحدهما فقط، فإنه يقضى له بالكل، وإنما يقضى لهما على التنصيف؛ لتساويهما، ولحديث تميم بن طرفة الطائي:
"أن رجلين اختصما إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في ناقة أقام كل واحد منهما البينة، فقضى بها بينهما نصفين". ولما رواه أبو داود عن أبي موسى الأشعرى أن رجلين ادعيا بعيراً على عهد رسول الله وبعث كل واحد منهما بشاهدين، فقسمه رسول الله له نصفين" وقال الشافعي: إن البينتين يتساقطان، وبه قال أحمد في رواية، وعن الشافعي: : أنه يقرع بينهما؛ لما روى سعيد بن المسيب: أن رجلين تنازعا في أمة بين يدي رسول الله ، وأقاما البينة، فأقرع رسول الله بينهما، وقال: "اللهم أنت تقضي بين عبادك بالحق"، ثم قضى بها لمن خرجت قرعته"، والجواب: أن القرعة كانت في ابتداء الإسلام وقت إباحة القمار، ثم نسخت بحرمة القمار؛ فإنها في معناه، وعن الشافعي في قول يوقف أبداً حتى يعلم أحد البينتين، وعنه كقولنا وعن مالك في رواية: يقضى بأعدل البينتين، وقال الأوزاعي : يقضى لمن كان شهوده أكثر عدداً. (فتح، عيني، عناية) وعلى نكاح إلخ : أي لو أقام كل واحد من الرجلين بينة على أن هذه المرأة امرأته، سقط برهانهما؛ لتعذر العمل بهما؛ إذ النكاح لا يقبل الاشتراك، وفرق القاضي بينهما حيث لا مرجح، وإنما يسقط البرهانان إذا كانت الدعوى حال حياتها؛ لعدم ما تترجح به إحدى البينتين على الأخرى بأن لم يؤرخا، فلو أرخت إحداهما دون الأخرى قضى للمؤرخ ، وإن كانت دعوى بعد موتها تقبل البينتان إن لم يؤرخا أو أرخا واستوى تاريخهما، وعلى كل منهما نصف المهر ويرثان ميراث زوج واحد. (عيني، فتح) وهي لمن صدقته [ أي تكون لمن صدقته في دعواه بأن تقول: هذا زوجي. (معدن)] لأن النكاح مما يحكم به بتصادق الزوجين، فيرجع إلى تصديقها، إلا إذا كانت في بيت أحدهما أو دخل بها أحدهما، فيكون هو أولى، ولا يعتبر قولها؛ لأن تمكنه من نقلها، أو من الدخول بها دليل على سبق عقده، إلا أن يقيم الآخر البينة أنه تزوجها قبله، فيكون هو أولى؛ لأن الصريح يفوق الدلالة.

أو سبقت بينته [ أي أو تكون لمن تقدمت بينته بالنكاح. (ط) ظاهره أن الترجيح بالتصديق في رتبة الترجيح بسبق التاريخ، وليس كذلك حتى لو صدقت من لم يسبق تاريخه لا يعتبر تصديقها، ويقضى بالنكاح لمن سبق تاريخه؛ لأن سبق التاريخ أرجح من الكل، ثم اليد، ثم الدخول ثم الإقرار ، فلو قال المصنف: "وهي لمن صدقت إن لم يسبق تاريخ الإقرار"، لكان أولى، وأما الترجيح بوجود التاريخ من أحدهما فقط فمشروط بأمرين: عدم إقرارها على الآخر، وعدم كونها في يده. والحاصل أن المدعيين إذا تنازعا في امرأة وأقاما ،البينة، فإن أرخها وكان تاريخ أحدهما أقدم كان لم يؤرخا أو استوى تاريخهما، فإن كان مع أحدهما قبض كالدخول بها، أو نقلها إلى منزله كان ذلك لم يوجد شيء من يرجع إلى تصديق المرأة. (عيني) لم يأخذ الآخر كله أي كل المبيع؛ لأنه صار مقضياً عليه بالنصف، فانفسخ العقد فيه والعقد متى انفسخ بقضاء القاضي لا يعود إلا بتجديد ولم يوجد، بخلاف ما لو ترك أحدهما قبل القضاء به بينهما، حيث يكون للآخر أن يأخذه كله؛ لأنه أثبت ببينته أنه اشترى الكل، وإنما يرجع إلى النصف بالمزاحمة ضرورة القضاء به و لم يوجد رمز الحقائق: ٢٠١/٢] (فتح، عناية) وإن أرخا فللسابق: أي لو ذكر كل واحد من البرهانين تاريخا فيكون الحكم للسابق منهما؛ لأنه أثبت الشراء في زمن لا ينازعه فيه أحد فاندفع الآخر به، وهذا بالإجماع إلا في قول للشافعي، فإنه يكون بينهما. وإلا فلذي القبض: أي وإن لم يؤرخا أو أرخا تاريخاً واحداً، أو أرخ واحد منهما، فيكون المدعى لذي القبض منهما؛ لأن تمكنه من القبض يدل على سبق شرائه رمز الحقائق: ٢٠١/٢] (فتح)
.
Book of Claims. Chapter: What two men claim – i.e., an explanation of its rulings. If they provide proof regarding something in the hand of another, it is judged for them both; and regarding a marriage of a woman, both proofs are dropped, and the woman belongs to the one she affirms. i.e., the judgment is given for the property, even if they provide proof etc. i.e., the woman. The two men: i.e., against a third party, or one against the other. The reason for delaying this chapter after the claim of a single person: because the single person comes before multiple. (Ayni) 'Judged for them both' – i.e., if two claimants claim a property in the hand of another, each claiming it is absolutely his ownership, and they do not mention the cause of ownership nor its date, and they provide evidence for their claim, then it is judged for them both by way of half and half. It is restricted to their providing evidence; because if only one provided evidence, then it is judged entirely for him. They are judged half and half because of their equality, and due to the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafah al-Ta'i: 'Two men disputed before the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) regarding a she-camel, each brought evidence, and he judged it between them in halves.' And also what Abu Dawud narrated from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari: two men claimed a camel during the time of the Messenger of Allah and each sent two witnesses, so the Messenger of Allah divided it between them in halves. Al-Shafi'i said: the two evidences cancel each other; Ahmad also said that in one narration. And from al-Shafi'i: he draws lots between them, based on the narration of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: two men disputed over a slave-woman before the Messenger of Allah, and each brought evidence, so the Messenger of Allah cast lots between them and said, 'O Allah, You judge between Your servants with truth,' then he judged for the one whose lot came out. The response: casting lots was in early Islam at the time when gambling was permissible, then it was abrogated by the prohibition of gambling, for it is similar in meaning. And from al-Shafi'i in one opinion: it is suspended forever until one of the two evidences is known. And from him is a saying like ours. And from Malik in one narration: judgment is given for the more just of the two evidences. Al-Awza'i said: judgment is given for the one whose witnesses are more in number. (Fath, Ayni, Inaya) 'And regarding a marriage etc.': i.e., if each of the two men provides evidence that this woman is his wife, then their evidences are dropped due to the impossibility of acting upon both, because marriage does not accept sharing, and the judge separates them when there is no preponderance. The two evidences are only dropped if the claim is during her lifetime, due to the lack of anything that could give preponderance to one evidence over the other, if they did not mention a date. If one of them mentions a date and the other does not, judgment is given for the one with the date. If the claim is after her death, both evidences are accepted if they did not mention a date or if they mentioned a date and the dates are equal. And each of the two is obliged to pay half the dowry, and they inherit the inheritance of one husband. (Ayni, Fath) 'And she belongs to the one she affirms': i.e., she belongs to the one she affirms in his claim, by saying 'this is my husband.' (Ma'din) Because marriage is something that can be judged by the mutual affirmation of the spouses, so recourse is taken to her affirmation, except if she is in the house of one of them or if one has had sexual intercourse with her, in which case he has priority, and her statement is not considered; because his ability to move her or to have intercourse with her is evidence of the precedence of his contract, unless the other brings evidence that he married her before him, in which case he has priority, because clear proof outweighs indication. (Fath, Ayni) 'or his evidence preceded, and regarding purchase from him, each has half of it for its price if he wishes, and if one refuses after the judgment, the other does not take the whole.' i.e., even if they provide evidence etc. the owner of the hand. 'half of the claimed amount' i.e., half of the price. 'the judgment, the other does not take the whole. And if they mention a date, then it is for the earlier; otherwise, for the one in possession.' i.e., the other claimant. 'or his evidence preceded' i.e., or it is for the one whose evidence for marriage preceded. (T) The apparent meaning is that preference by affirmation is on the same level as preference by precedence of date, but it is not so. Even if she affirms the one whose date does not precede, her affirmation is not considered, and judgment is given for marriage to the one whose date precedes, because precedence of date is more weighty than everything, then possession, then sexual intercourse, then acknowledgment. If the author said: 'and she belongs to the one she affirms if no date precedes the acknowledgment,' it would be better. As for preference by the existence of a date from only one of them, it is conditional on two matters: the absence of her affirmation of the other, and that she is not in his possession. The conclusion is that if two claimants dispute over a woman and bring evidence, then if they mention a date and the date of one is earlier, it is for the earlier; if they do not mention dates or the dates are equal, and if one has possession such as sexual intercourse or moving her to his house, then that is considered; if nothing of that exists, then recourse is to the woman's affirmation. (Fath, Ayni) 'each has half of it for its price' i.e., the thing belongs to each of the two claimants. (T) i.e., if a slave is in the hand of a man, and each claims to have bought the slave from the man, and they bring evidence, and neither evidence mentions a time, then half of the slave is for each of the two claimants for half of the price, and if he wishes to leave it and take all the price. Because the condition of the contract he claims has changed upon him, and perhaps his desire was to own the whole thing and he did not achieve that, so he returns it and takes the full price. If it is said: the two evidences should be invalid because one of the parties is lying, as it is impossible for two contracts to fully occur at the same moment, the answer is that they did not testify that they occurred at the same moment, but they testified to the contract itself, so it is possible that each relied on a cause at a time that permitted him to testify to it. (Fath, Inaya) 'if he wishes' – if he wishes to leave it. Because when they are equal in the cause, they are given a choice, due to the impossibility of judging the entire thing for each one. (Ayni) 'and if one refuses' – by one of them refusing after the judgment, i.e., after the judge's ruling of it between them. (Ayni) 'the other does not take the whole' – i.e., all of the sold item. Because he has become subject to a judgment against him for half, so the contract is rescinded regarding it, and when a contract is rescinded by a judge's ruling, it cannot return except by a new contract, and that is not found. Unlike the case where he leaves it before the judgment of it between them, where the other may take all of it; because he proved by his evidence that he bought the whole, and he only returns to half due to the necessary competition of the judgment, and that has not occurred. (Fath, Inaya) 'And if they mention a date, then it is for the earlier' – i.e., if each of the two proofs mentions a date, then the ruling is for the earlier, because he proved the purchase at a time when no one else contested him, so the other is refuted by that. This is by consensus, except for one saying of al-Shafi'i that it is between them. 'Otherwise, for the one in possession' – i.e., if they do not mention a date, or they mention a single date, or one of them mentions a date, then the claimed item is for the one in possession, because his ability to take possession indicates the precedence of his purchase. (Fath)
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute from the Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the scenario where two men both claim ownership or marriage rights over the same subject, each providing evidence (bayyinah). The Hanafi school holds that when two claimants each bring evidence for a property (ayn) in the hand of a third party, and neither provides a cause or date of ownership, the judge rules that the property is divided equally between them. This is based on the hadith of Tamim ibn Tarafah, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) divided a she-camel between two claimants with equal evidence. The text then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who in one view says the evidences cancel each other (tasāqut), and in another view advocates drawing lots (qur'ah). The Hanafi response argues that the qur'ah was abrogated as it resembled gambling. Other views are cited: Ahmad agrees with tasāqut, Malik prefers the more just evidence, and al-Awza'i prefers the party with more witnesses. The passage then discusses marriage claims: if two men each bring evidence that a woman is his wife, both evidences are dropped because marriage cannot be shared. The woman goes to the one she affirms (if she confirms one of them), unless one has prior date, possession (e.g., she lives in his house), or intercourse. In purchase claims from a hand-owner (dhū al-yad), if both buyers bring evidence without dates, the item is divided half and half, and each may take his half or return it and take back his price. If one refuses after judgment, the other does not get the whole item because the contract was judicially rescinded. If dates are given, the earlier date takes priority. If no dates or equal dates, the one in possession (who has taken delivery) is preferred. The commentary from 'Ayni, Fath, and 'Inaya provides detailed elaboration. The key lesson is that Hanafi jurisprudence prioritizes the practical possibility of acting on both evidences over their formal contradiction, using equality and external indicators (dates, possession, affirmation) to resolve conflicts.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence consisting of witnesses presented in court to prove a claim.
برهان (burhān): Proof or argument; here synonymous with bayyinah.
قضاء (qaḍā'): The judge's ruling or decision.
تصديق (taṣdīq): Affirmation; the woman's confirmation of one of the claimants as her husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men claim marriage to a woman. One has an earlier marriage contract with witnesses; the other lives with her and the woman affirms him. According to Hanafi law, who is her husband?
A) The man with the earlier contract
B) The man she lives with, because of possession
C) The man she affirms
D) Neither; the judge separates them

Key Takeaway
In marriage disputes, dates and possession override the woman's affirmation; the Hanafi hierarchy ensures clear resolution.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Purchase is Preferred Over Gift in Claims
Explains preference of purchase over gift in claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that purchase is preferred over gift in judicial claims and explain the reasoning based on contract strength.

Core Idea
Purchase is stronger than gift because it is a bilateral exchange that transfers ownership immediately.

In disputes over property, when two claimants present equal evidence—one claiming purchase and the other claiming a gift—the judge must decide. This passage from the Hanafi fiqh of judicial proceedings establishes that purchase is given precedence over gift when all else is equal. The reasoning is rooted in the nature of the contracts: sale is a bilateral exchange (mu'awada) that perfects ownership instantly, while gift (hiba) requires delivery (qabd) to complete ownership. Understanding this rule helps clarify how Hanafi jurists prioritize competing claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage and note the key elements: two claimants, one claims purchase (shira'), the other claims gift (hiba) with possession (qabd), from the same granter, with no dates on the evidence. This is the specific case where the rule applies.
If the granter is different or dates exist, the rule does not apply.
2
State the ruling
The ruling: purchase is preferred (الشراء أحق). The judge decides in favor of the purchase claimant.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
The text explains that purchase is 'stronger' (أقوى) than gift because it is a mu'awada (bilateral exchange) that establishes ownership by itself. Gift ownership depends on possession, so the purchase ownership is deemed earlier (سابقا).
This reasoning does not apply if the gift is with compensation (become a sale) or if the granter differs.
4
Examine exceptions
Note the exceptions: if the granter is different, each must prove ownership from their own granter, so they are equal. If dates are present, the earlier date prevails. Also, gift and charity (sadaqa) are equal because both are donations.
5
Understand the intra-Hanafi difference
The passage discusses whether the rule applies to divisible property. Abu Hanifa says no (because executing a gift in co-owned property is invalid); the Sahibayn permit it. The relied-upon position is that it does not apply.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشراء أحق من الهبة يعني أي على الملك المطلق وهو مختلف ذلك الشخص أحق من الهبة: إذا ادعى أحد المدعيين شراء من شخص، وادعى الآخر منهما هبة وقبضاً من بعينه، وأقاما البينة، ولا تاريخ معهما، كان الشراء أولى؛ لأنه أقوى من الهبة؛ لكونه معاوضة من الجانبين، ومثبتاً للملك بنفسه، والملك في الهبة يتوقف على القبض فكان ملك من يدعي الشراء سابقا فكان أولى، والمراد من الهبة: أن لا تكون بعوض؛ إذ لو كانت بعوض كانت بيعاً، فالشراء والهبة بعوض متساويان، وإذا اختلف المملك لهما، مثل أن يدعي أحدهما الشراء من شخص وادعى الآخر الهبة مع القبض من شخص آخر، أو كان معهما تاريخ، لا يكون الشراء فيه أولى؛ لأن عند اختلاف المملك يصير كل منهما خصماً عن مملكه؛ لحاجته إلى إثبات الملك، وهما في ذلك سواء، وفيما إذا اتحد المملك لا يحتاجان إلى إثبات الملك له؛ لثبوته باتفاقهما، وإنما حاجتهما إلى إثبات سبب الملك لأنفسهما، وفيه يقدم الأقوى. [تبيين الحقائق: ٣٧١/٥] والحاصل أنه إذا كان المملك لهما مختلفاً، فإنه يقضي به بينهما نصفين والهبة والصدقة سواء، فإذا ادعى أحدهما الشراء، والآخر الصدقة والقبض، كان الشراء أولى، ولو ادعى أحدهما هبة وقبضاً، والآخر صدقة وقبضاً، فهما سواء، ويقضى به بينهما؛ لاستوائهما في وجه التبرع، وهذا فيما لا يحتمل القسمة؛ لأن الشيوع لا يضره، واختلفوا فيما يحتمل القسمة، والأصح أنه لا يصح؛ لأنه تنفيذ الهبة في الشائع، فصار كإقامة البينتين على الارتمان، وقيل: هذا قول أبي حنيفة وعندهما يجوز بناء على أن هبة الواحد من الاثنين جائزة عندهما خلافاً له، وقيل: يجوز بالإجماع؛ لطروء الشيوع، والأصح أنه لا يجوز بالإجماع.
And purchase is more entitled than gift, meaning regarding absolute ownership, and it differs when the granter is different. That person is more entitled than gift: if one of the two claimants claims a purchase from a person, and the other claims a gift with possession from a specific person, and they present evidence, and there is no date with them, then purchase is preferred because it is stronger than gift, as it is a compensation from both sides, and it establishes ownership by itself, while ownership in gift is contingent on possession, so the ownership of the one claiming purchase is earlier, thus he is preferred. The intended meaning of gift is that it is without compensation; because if it were with compensation, it would be a sale, so purchase and gift with compensation are equal. If the granter is different for them, such as one claims purchase from a person and the other claims gift with possession from another person, or they have a date, then purchase is not preferred because when the granter differs, each becomes a litigant against their granter, as they need to prove ownership, and they are equal in that. And when the granter is the same, they do not need to prove ownership for him because it is established by their agreement, and they only need to prove the cause of ownership for themselves, and in that the stronger is given precedence. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 5/371] The conclusion is that if the granter is different for them, then judgment is given between them half and half. And gift and charity are equal. So if one claims purchase and the other claims charity with possession, purchase is preferred. And if one claims gift with possession and the other claims charity with possession, they are equal, and judgment is given between them because they are equal in the aspect of donation. This is regarding that which does not admit division because co-ownership does not harm it. And they differed regarding that which admits division, and the correct view is that it is not valid because it is execution of gift in co-owned property, thus it becomes like establishing two evidences on priority. And it was said: this is the view of Abu Hanifa, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permitted based on the permissibility of a gift by one person to two persons according to them contrary to him. And it was said: it is permitted by consensus due to the occurrence of co-ownership, but the correct view is that it is not permitted by consensus.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter of claims (kitab al-da'wa) explaining that when two claimants both present evidence, one claiming purchase (shira') of a specific property from a specific person and the other claiming a gift (hiba) with possession (qabd) from the same person, purchase is preferred. The reasoning is that purchase is a bilateral exchange (mu'awada) that establishes ownership immediately upon contract, while gift requires delivery (qabd) to perfect ownership. Even if both have evidence but no dates, purchase is given precedence because the ownership from purchase is deemed earlier. However, if the granter is different (e.g., one claims purchase from Zayd and the other claims gift from 'Amr), then they are equal because each must prove ownership from their respective granter, and no precedence is given. Similarly, if both have dates, the earlier date prevails. The text also equates gift and charity (sadaqa) – both are donations (tabarru') – so if one claims gift and the other charity, they are equal. The discussion includes a dispute about whether these rules apply to property that is divisible. The relied-upon position (al-ashah) is that for indivisible property, the judgment is between them equally, but for divisible property, the ruling does not apply because executing a gift in co-owned property is problematic.

Key Terms
شراء (shira'): A contract of sale where ownership transfers immediately upon contract without need for delivery.
هبة (hiba): A gift without compensation, which requires delivery (qabd) to perfect ownership.
معاوضة (mu'awada): Bilateral exchange where each party gives and receives compensation, as in sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two claimants both present evidence without dates: one claims purchase from Zayd, the other claims a gift with possession from Zayd. What is the ruling?
A) They are equal and split the property.
B) The purchase claim is preferred.
C) The gift claim is preferred because it involves possession.
D) The judge dismisses both claims.

Key Takeaway
Purchase is preferred over gift in claims because it is a bilateral exchange that transfers ownership immediately, provided the granter is the same and no dates are present.

Equality of Purchase and Dower Claims
The passage states the equality of purchase and dower claims and presents intra-Hanafi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that purchase and dower are equal in claim strength when evidence is equal, and state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Purchase and dower are both compensations that immediately establish ownership, making their claims equal.

This lesson addresses a common dispute scenario: a man claims to have purchased a property from a seller, while a woman claims that same property was given as her dower in marriage. How does a judge decide when both present equal evidence? The Hanafi school treats both contracts as compensations (mu'awada), so they are equal in strength. However, an intra-madhhab difference exists between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the preferred solution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
Recognize that the man claims purchase (shira') and the woman claims dower (mahr) over the same property from the same owner. Both are evidence-based claims.
The property is a specific 'ayn (عين).
2
Apply the general rule: equality when evidence is equal
If neither claim has a date, or both have the same date, the property is divided equally between them. This is because each contract is a compensation that establishes ownership immediately.
If one claim has an earlier date, that claim takes precedence.
3
Distinguish Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the woman receives half the property and half its value from the husband, and the buyer receives half the price and may rescind due to partial loss.
This aims to partially fulfill both claims.
4
Distinguish Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that purchase is preferred because it is possible to act on both claims: marriage against property owned by another is valid, and the husband must pay its value.
Thus the buyer retains the whole property, and the woman has a claim for value.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah)
The difference affects whether the property is split or entirely given to the buyer. Abu Yusuf splits; Muhammad gives full property to the buyer.
The text presents Abu Yusuf first, but both are valid within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشراء والمهر سواء يعني إذا ادعى رجل بشراء عين من رجل، وادعت امرأة أن ذلك الرجل تزوجها على تلك العين فهما سواء، ويكون العين بينهما؛ لاستواء البينتين في القوة فإن كل واحد من الشراء والمهر معاوضة تثبت الملك بنفسه، وهذا إذا لم يؤرخا أو أرخا وتاريخهما على السواء، فإن سبق تاريخ أحدهما فالسابق أولى، ثم للمرأة نصف العين ونصف قيمة العين على الزوج وللمشتري نصف الثمن، ويرجع بنصف الثمن إن شاء، إن شاء و فسخ؛ لتفرق الصفقة عليه، وهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد الشراء أولى؛ لأنه أمكن العمل بالبينتين بتقديم الشراء؛ إذ التزوج على عين مملوك للغير صحيح وتجب قيمته عند تعذر تسليمه [رمز الحقائق: ٢٠١/٢] (فتح) والرهن أحق إلخ: يعني لو ادعى أحدهما رهناً وقبضاً والآخر هبة وقبضاً من صاحب اليد، وأقاما البينة، ولا تاريخ لهما، كان الرهن أولى؛ لأنه مضمون والهبة أمانة والمضمون أقوى، بخلاف الهبة بشرط العوض؛ لأنها بيع انتهاء. [ رمز الحقائق: ۲۰۲/۲] والبيع أولى؛ لكونه عقد ضمان يثبت الملك للحال صورة ومعنى، والرهن لا يثبته إلا عند الهلاك معنى لا صورة وفي القياس الهبة أولى من الرهن؛ لأنها تثبت الملك والرهن لا يثبته، ووجه الاستحسان ما قلنا. (فتح)
And purchase and dower are equal, meaning if a man claims purchase of a specific property from a man, and a woman claims that that man married her against that property, then they are equal, and the property is between them because the two evidences are equal in strength, for each of purchase and dower is a compensation that establishes ownership by itself. This is when they have not dated or they have dated and their dates are equal. If one of them has a prior date, then the earlier is preferred. Then the woman gets half of the property and half of its value from the husband, and the buyer gets half of the price, and he may take back half of the price if he wishes, and if he wishes he may rescind due to the division of the transaction upon him. This is according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, purchase is preferred because it is possible to act on both evidences by giving precedence to purchase, for marrying against property owned by another is valid and its value is obligatory when delivery is impossible. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/201] (Fath) And the pledge is more entitled, etc.: meaning if one claims a pledge with possession and the other claims a gift with possession from the possessor, and they present evidence, and there is no date for them, then the pledge is preferred because it is guaranteed (madhmun) and the gift is a trust (amana), and the guaranteed is stronger, contrary to a gift with condition of compensation because it is a sale. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/202] And sale is more entitled because it is a contract of guarantee that establishes ownership immediately in form and meaning, while pledge does not establish it except at destruction in meaning not in form. In analogy, gift is more entitled than pledge because it establishes ownership and pledge does not, but the basis of istihsan is what we said. (Fath)
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rules from the chapter of claims. First, when a man claims to have purchased a specific property from a person, and a woman claims that same person married her with that property as her dower (mahr), their claims are equal because both purchase and dower are compensations (mu'awada) that immediately establish ownership. If neither has a date or both have the same date, the property is divided equally between them. However, if one has an earlier date, that claim prevails. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf holds that the woman gets half the property and half its value from the husband, and the buyer gets half the price with an option to rescind due to part of his purchase being lost. Muhammad holds that purchase is preferred because it is possible to uphold both claims by giving precedence to purchase, as marriage against property owned by another is valid but the husband must pay its value. Second, the text states that a claim of pledge (rahn) with possession is stronger than a claim of gift with possession from the same party, because a pledge is a guaranteed contract (madhmun) while a gift is a trust (amana). The guaranteed is stronger. But if the gift is with a condition of compensation (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad), it is considered a sale, and then sale is even stronger than pledge. This ruling is based on istihsan, as analogy would suggest gift is stronger because it conveys ownership, while pledge does not. But istihsan prioritizes the guarantee aspect.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower given by a husband to his wife, which is a compensation for marriage, similar to a sale price.
معاوضة (mu'awada): A commutative contract where one thing is exchanged for another, like sale and dower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a man claims purchase and a woman claims dower over the same property, with equal evidence and no dates, what is the ruling according to Abu Yusuf?
A) The woman gets the whole property.
B) The man gets the whole property.
C) The property is divided equally, and the woman gets half its value from the husband.
D) The judge delays the decision.

Key Takeaway
Purchase and dower are equal in claim strength because both are compensations that immediately establish ownership.

Preference of Pledge Over Gift
This part of the passage explains the preference of pledge over gift with istihsan justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a pledge claim is stronger than a gift claim in Hanafi law, and distinguish between the treatment of simple gifts and gifts with compensation.

Core Idea
Pledge is preferred over gift because it is a guaranteed contract, while gift is a trust.

This lesson covers another evidentiary preference: when two parties claim a property from a possessor, one claiming pledge with possession and the other claiming gift with possession, the claim of pledge takes precedence. The Hanafi school bases this on the concept that a pledge is a guaranteed (madhmun) contract, while a gift is a trust (amana). The passage also notes that a gift with a condition of compensation is treated as a sale, which is even stronger than a pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims: pledge vs. gift
One person claims the possessor gave them the property as a pledge (rahn) and they took possession. Another claims the possessor gave it as a gift (hiba) and they took possession. Both have evidence but no dates.
The possessor (dhu al-yad) is the defendant.
2
Apply the general rule: pledge is preferred
The pledge claim is stronger because a pledge is a contract of guarantee (madhmun), while a gift is a trust (amana). In Hanafi law, guaranteed contracts prevail over trust contracts in evidentiary disputes.
This is based on istihsan, not qiyas.
3
Consider the exception: gift with compensation
If the gift was given with a condition of compensation (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad), it is considered a sale, which is even stronger than a pledge because sale establishes ownership immediately in both form and meaning.
So if the claim is of a gift with compensation, it would beat a claim of pledge.
4
Understand the istihsan reasoning
Analogy (qiyas) would suggest the gift is stronger because it conveys ownership, while the pledge does not. But istihsan prefers the pledge because of the guarantee element, which is more protective of rights.
The source passage explicitly mentions 'face of istihsan' as what we said.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشراء والمهر سواء يعني إذا ادعى رجل بشراء عين من رجل، وادعت امرأة أن ذلك الرجل تزوجها على تلك العين فهما سواء، ويكون العين بينهما؛ لاستواء البينتين في القوة فإن كل واحد من الشراء والمهر معاوضة تثبت الملك بنفسه، وهذا إذا لم يؤرخا أو أرخا وتاريخهما على السواء، فإن سبق تاريخ أحدهما فالسابق أولى، ثم للمرأة نصف العين ونصف قيمة العين على الزوج وللمشتري نصف الثمن، ويرجع بنصف الثمن إن شاء، إن شاء و فسخ؛ لتفرق الصفقة عليه، وهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد الشراء أولى؛ لأنه أمكن العمل بالبينتين بتقديم الشراء؛ إذ التزوج على عين مملوك للغير صحيح وتجب قيمته عند تعذر تسليمه [رمز الحقائق: ٢٠١/٢] (فتح) والرهن أحق إلخ: يعني لو ادعى أحدهما رهناً وقبضاً والآخر هبة وقبضاً من صاحب اليد، وأقاما البينة، ولا تاريخ لهما، كان الرهن أولى؛ لأنه مضمون والهبة أمانة والمضمون أقوى، بخلاف الهبة بشرط العوض؛ لأنها بيع انتهاء. [ رمز الحقائق: ۲۰۲/۲] والبيع أولى؛ لكونه عقد ضمان يثبت الملك للحال صورة ومعنى، والرهن لا يثبته إلا عند الهلاك معنى لا صورة وفي القياس الهبة أولى من الرهن؛ لأنها تثبت الملك والرهن لا يثبته، ووجه الاستحسان ما قلنا. (فتح)
And purchase and dower are equal, meaning if a man claims purchase of a specific property from a man, and a woman claims that that man married her against that property, then they are equal, and the property is between them because the two evidences are equal in strength, for each of purchase and dower is a compensation that establishes ownership by itself. This is when they have not dated or they have dated and their dates are equal. If one of them has a prior date, then the earlier is preferred. Then the woman gets half of the property and half of its value from the husband, and the buyer gets half of the price, and he may take back half of the price if he wishes, and if he wishes he may rescind due to the division of the transaction upon him. This is according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, purchase is preferred because it is possible to act on both evidences by giving precedence to purchase, for marrying against property owned by another is valid and its value is obligatory when delivery is impossible. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/201] (Fath) And the pledge is more entitled, etc.: meaning if one claims a pledge with possession and the other claims a gift with possession from the possessor, and they present evidence, and there is no date for them, then the pledge is preferred because it is guaranteed (madhmun) and the gift is a trust (amana), and the guaranteed is stronger, contrary to a gift with condition of compensation because it is a sale. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/202] And sale is more entitled because it is a contract of guarantee that establishes ownership immediately in form and meaning, while pledge does not establish it except at destruction in meaning not in form. In analogy, gift is more entitled than pledge because it establishes ownership and pledge does not, but the basis of istihsan is what we said. (Fath)
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rules from the chapter of claims. First, when a man claims to have purchased a specific property from a person, and a woman claims that same person married her with that property as her dower (mahr), their claims are equal because both purchase and dower are compensations (mu'awada) that immediately establish ownership. If neither has a date or both have the same date, the property is divided equally between them. However, if one has an earlier date, that claim prevails. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf holds that the woman gets half the property and half its value from the husband, and the buyer gets half the price with an option to rescind due to part of his purchase being lost. Muhammad holds that purchase is preferred because it is possible to uphold both claims by giving precedence to purchase, as marriage against property owned by another is valid but the husband must pay its value. Second, the text states that a claim of pledge (rahn) with possession is stronger than a claim of gift with possession from the same party, because a pledge is a guaranteed contract (madhmun) while a gift is a trust (amana). The guaranteed is stronger. But if the gift is with a condition of compensation (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad), it is considered a sale, and then sale is even stronger than pledge. This ruling is based on istihsan, as analogy would suggest gift is stronger because it conveys ownership, while pledge does not. But istihsan prioritizes the guarantee aspect.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge where a property is given as security for a debt, with possession transferred but ownership not transferred.
مضمون (madhmun): Guaranteed: a property that is subject to liability in case of loss, such as a pledge or sold item.
أمانة (amana): Trust: a property held without liability for loss, like a gift, deposit, or loan for use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a pledge claim preferred over a simple gift claim in Hanafi law?
A) Because pledge involves possession and gift does not.
B) Because pledge is a guaranteed contract and gift is a trust.
C) Because pledge establishes ownership immediately.
D) Because gift requires delivery of possession.

Key Takeaway
In disputes, a claim of pledge beats a claim of simple gift because pledge is a guaranteed contract, whereas gift is a trust.
